Partakers, and uncropt falls to the ground.<br/>
But thou hast promis'd from us two a Race<br/>
To fill the Earth, who shall with us extoll<br/>
Thy goodness infinite, both when we wake,<br/>
And when we seek, as now, thy gift of sleep.

This said unanimous, and other Rites<br/>
Observing none, but adoration pure<br/>
Which God likes best, into thir inmost bower<br/>
Handed they went; and eas'd the putting off<br/>
These troublesom disguises which wee wear,<br/>
Strait side by side were laid, nor turnd I weene<br/>
ADAM from his fair Spouse, nor EVE the Rites<br/>
Mysterious of connubial Love refus'd:<br/>
Whatever Hypocrites austerely talk<br/>
Of puritie and place and innocence,<br/>
Defaming as impure what God declares<br/>
Pure, and commands to som, leaves free to all.<br/>
Our Maker bids increase, who bids abstain<br/>
But our Destroyer, foe to God and Man?<br/>
Haile wedded Love, mysterious Law, true source<br/>
Of human ofspring, sole proprietie,<br/>
In Paradise of all things common else.<br/>
By thee adulterous lust was driv'n from men<br/>
Among the bestial herds to raunge, by thee<br/>
Founded in Reason, Loyal, Just, and Pure,<br/>
Relations dear, and all the Charities<br/>
Of Father, Son, and Brother first were known.<br/>
Farr be it, that I should write thee sin or blame,<br/>
Or think thee unbefitting holiest place,<br/>
Perpetual Fountain of Domestic sweets,<br/>
Whose Bed is undefil'd and chast pronounc't,<br/>
Present, or past, as Saints and Patriarchs us'd.<br/>
Here Love his golden shafts imploies, here lights<br/>
His constant Lamp, and waves his purple wings,<br/>
Reigns here and revels; not in the bought smile<br/>
Of Harlots, loveless, joyless, unindeard,<br/>
Casual fruition, nor in Court Amours<br/>
Mixt Dance, or wanton Mask, or Midnight Bal,<br/>
Or Serenate, which the starv'd Lover sings<br/>
To his proud fair, best quitted with disdain.<br/>
These lulld by Nightingales imbraceing slept,<br/>
And on thir naked limbs the flourie roof<br/>
Showrd Roses, which the Morn repair'd. Sleep on,<br/>
Blest pair; and O yet happiest if ye seek<br/>
No happier state, and know to know no more.

Now had night measur'd with her shaddowie Cone<br/>
Half way up Hill this vast Sublunar Vault,<br/>
And from thir Ivorie Port the Cherubim<br/>
Forth issuing at th' accustomd hour stood armd<br/>
To thir night watches in warlike Parade,<br/>
When GABRIEL to his next in power thus spake.

UZZIEL, half these draw off, and coast the South<br/>
With strictest watch; these other wheel the North,<br/>
Our circuit meets full West. As flame they part<br/>
Half wheeling to the Shield, half to the Spear.<br/>
From these, two strong and suttle Spirits he calld<br/>
That neer him stood, and gave them thus in charge.

ITHURIEL and ZEPHON, with wingd speed<br/>
Search through this Garden, leav unsearcht no nook,<br/>
But chiefly where those two fair Creatures Lodge,<br/>
Now laid perhaps asleep secure of harme.<br/>
This Eevning from the Sun's decline arriv'd<br/>
Who tells of som infernal Spirit seen<br/>
Hitherward bent (who could have thought?) escap'd<br/>
The barrs of Hell, on errand bad no doubt:<br/>
Such where ye find, seise fast, and hither bring.

So saying, on he led his radiant Files,<br/>
Daz'ling the Moon; these to the Bower direct<br/>
In search of whom they sought: him there they found<br/>
Squat like a Toad, close at the eare of EVE;<br/>
Assaying by his Devilish art to reach<br/>
The Organs of her Fancie, and with them forge<br/>
Illusions as he list, Phantasms and Dreams,<br/>
Or if, inspiring venom, he might taint<br/>
Th' animal Spirits that from pure blood arise<br/>
Like gentle breaths from Rivers pure, thence raise<br/>
At least distemperd, discontented thoughts,<br/>
Vain hopes, vain aimes, inordinate desires<br/>
Blown up with high conceits ingendring pride.<br/>
Him thus intent ITHURIEL with his Spear<br/>
Touch'd lightly; for no falshood can endure<br/>
Touch of Celestial temper, but returns<br/>
Of force to its own likeness: up he starts<br/>
Discoverd and surpriz'd. As when a spark<br/>
Lights on a heap of nitrous Powder, laid<br/>
Fit for the Tun som Magazin to store<br/>
Against a rumord Warr, the Smuttie graine<br/>
With sudden blaze diffus'd, inflames the Aire:<br/>
So started up in his own shape the Fiend.<br/>
Back stept those two fair Angels half amaz'd<br/>
So sudden to behold the grieslie King;<br/>
Yet thus, unmovd with fear, accost him soon.

Which of those rebell Spirits adjudg'd to Hell<br/>
Com'st thou, escap'd thy prison, and transform'd,<br/>
Why satst thou like an enemie in waite<br/>
Here watching at the head of these that sleep?

Know ye not then said SATAN, filld with scorn,<br/>
Know ye not me? ye knew me once no mate<br/>
For you, there sitting where ye durst not soare;<br/>
Not to know mee argues your selves unknown,<br/>
The lowest of your throng; or if ye know,<br/>
Why ask ye, and superfluous begin<br/>
Your message, like to end as much in vain?<br/>
To whom thus ZEPHON, answering scorn with scorn.<br/>
Think not, revolted Spirit, thy shape the same,<br/>
Or undiminisht brightness, to be known<br/>
As when thou stoodst in Heav'n upright and pure;<br/>
That Glorie then, when thou no more wast good,<br/>
Departed from thee, and thou resembl'st now<br/>
Thy sin and place of doom obscure and foule.<br/>
But come, for thou, be sure, shalt give account<br/>
To him who sent us, whose charge is to keep<br/>
This place inviolable, and these from harm.

So spake the Cherube, and his grave rebuke<br/>
Severe in youthful beautie, added grace<br/>
Invincible: abasht the Devil stood,<br/>
And felt how awful goodness is, and saw<br/>
Vertue in her shape how lovly, saw, and pin'd<br/>
His loss; but chiefly to find here observd<br/>
His lustre visibly impar'd; yet seemd<br/>
Undaunted. If I must contend, said he,<br/>
Best with the best, the Sender not the sent,<br/>
Or all at once; more glorie will be wonn,<br/>
Or less be lost. Thy fear, said ZEPHON bold,<br/>
Will save us trial what the least can doe<br/>
Single against thee wicked, and thence weak.

The Fiend repli'd not, overcome with rage;<br/>
But like a proud Steed reind, went hautie on,<br/>
Chaumping his iron curb: to strive or flie<br/>
He held it vain; awe from above had quelld<br/>
His heart, not else dismai'd. Now drew they nigh<br/>
The western point, where those half-rounding guards<br/>
Just met, & closing stood in squadron joind<br/>
Awaiting next command. To whom thir Chief<br/>
GABRIEL from the Front thus calld aloud.

O friends, I hear the tread of nimble feet<br/>
Hasting this way, and now by glimps discerne<br/>
ITHURIEL and ZEPHON through the shade,<br/>
And with them comes a third of Regal port,<br/>
But faded splendor wan; who by his gate<br/>
And fierce demeanour seems the Prince of Hell,<br/>
Not likely to part hence without contest;<br/>
Stand firm, for in his look defiance lours.

He scarce had ended, when those two approachd<br/>
And brief related whom they brought, wher found,<br/>
How busied, in what form and posture coucht.

To whom with stern regard thus GABRIEL spake.<br/>
Why hast thou, SATAN, broke the bounds prescrib'd<br/>
To thy transgressions, and disturbd the charge<br/>
Of others, who approve not to transgress<br/>
By thy example, but have power and right<br/>
To question thy bold entrance on this place;<br/>
Imploi'd it seems to violate sleep, and those<br/>
Whose dwelling God hath planted here in bliss?

To whom thus SATAN with contemptuous brow.<br/>
GABRIEL, thou hadst in Heav'n th' esteem of wise,<br/>
And such I held thee; but this question askt<br/>
Puts me in doubt. Lives ther who loves his pain?<br/>
Who would not, finding way, break loose from Hell,<br/>
Though thither doomd? Thou wouldst thy self, no doubt,<br/>
And boldly venture to whatever place<br/>
Farthest from pain, where thou mightst hope to change<br/>
Torment with ease, & soonest recompence<br/>
Dole with delight, which in this place I sought;<br/>
To thee no reason; who knowst only good,<br/>
But evil hast not tri'd: and wilt object<br/>
His will who bound us? let him surer barr<br/>
His Iron Gates, if he intends our stay<br/>
In that dark durance: thus much what was askt.<br/>
The rest is true, they found me where they say;<br/>
But that implies not violence or harme.

Thus hee in scorn. The warlike Angel mov'd,<br/>
Disdainfully half smiling thus repli'd.<br/>
O loss of one in Heav'n to judge of wise,<br/>
Since SATAN fell, whom follie overthrew,<br/>
And now returns him from his prison scap't,<br/>
Gravely in doubt whether to hold them wise<br/>
Or not, who ask what boldness brought him hither<br/>
Unlicenc't from his bounds in Hell prescrib'd;<br/>
So wise he judges it to fly from pain<br/>
However, and to scape his punishment.<br/>
So judge thou still, presumptuous, till the wrauth,<br/>
Which thou incurr'st by flying, meet thy flight<br/>
Seavenfold, and scourge that wisdom back to Hell,<br/>
Which taught thee yet no better, that no pain<br/>
Can equal anger infinite provok't.<br/>
But wherefore thou alone? wherefore with thee<br/>
Came not all Hell broke loose? is pain to them<br/>
Less pain, less to be fled, or thou then they<br/>
Less hardie to endure? courageous Chief,<br/>
The first in flight from pain, had'st thou alleg'd<br/>
To thy deserted host this cause of flight,<br/>
Thou surely hadst not come sole fugitive.

To which the Fiend thus answerd frowning stern.<br/>
Not that I less endure, or shrink from pain,<br/>
Insulting Angel, well thou knowst I stood<br/>
Thy fiercest, when in Battel to thy aide<br/>
The blasting volied Thunder made all speed<br/>
And seconded thy else not dreaded Spear.<br/>
But still thy words at random, as before,<br/>
Argue thy inexperience what behooves<br/>
From hard assaies and ill successes past<br/>
A faithful Leader, not to hazard all<br/>
Through wayes of danger by himself untri'd.<br/>
I therefore, I alone first undertook<br/>
To wing the desolate Abyss, and spie<br/>
This new created World, whereof in Hell<br/>
Fame is not silent, here in hope to find<br/>
Better abode, and my afflicted Powers<br/>
To settle here on Earth, or in mid Aire;<br/>
Though for possession put to try once more<br/>
What thou and thy gay Legions dare against;<br/>
Whose easier business were to serve thir Lord<br/>
High up in Heav'n, with songs to hymne his Throne,<br/>
And practis'd distances to cringe, not fight.

To whom the warriour Angel soon repli'd.<br/>
To say and strait unsay, pretending first<br/>
Wise to flie pain, professing next the Spie,<br/>
Argues no Leader, but a lyar trac't,<br/>
SATAN, and couldst thou faithful add? O name,<br/>
O sacred name of faithfulness profan'd!<br/>
Faithful to whom? to thy rebellious crew?<br/>
Armie of Fiends, fit body to fit head;<br/>
Was this your discipline and faith ingag'd,<br/>
Your military obedience, to dissolve<br/>
Allegeance to th' acknowledg'd Power supream?<br/>
And thou sly hypocrite, who now wouldst seem<br/>
Patron of liberty, who more then thou<br/>
Once fawn'd, and cring'd, and servilly ador'd<br/>
Heav'ns awful Monarch? wherefore but in hope<br/>
To dispossess him, and thy self to reigne?<br/>
But mark what I arreede thee now, avant;<br/>
Flie thither whence thou fledst: if from this houre<br/>
Within these hallowd limits thou appeer,<br/>
Back to th' infernal pit I drag thee chaind,<br/>
And Seale thee so, as henceforth not to scorne<br/>
The facil gates of hell too slightly barrd.

So threatn'd hee, but SATAN to no threats<br/>
Gave heed, but waxing more in rage repli'd.

Then when I am thy captive talk of chaines,<br/>
Proud limitarie Cherube, but ere then<br/>
Farr heavier load thy self expect to feel<br/>
From my prevailing arme, though Heavens King<br/>
Ride on thy wings, and thou with thy Compeers,<br/>
Us'd to the yoak, draw'st his triumphant wheels<br/>
In progress through the rode of Heav'n Star-pav'd.

While thus he spake, th' Angelic Squadron bright<br/>
Turnd fierie red, sharpning in mooned hornes<br/>
Thir Phalanx, and began to hemm him round<br/>
With ported Spears, as thick as when a field<br/>
Of CERES ripe for harvest waving bends<br/>
Her bearded Grove of ears, which way the wind<br/>
Swayes them; the careful Plowman doubting stands<br/>
Least on the threshing floore his hopeful sheaves<br/>
Prove chaff. On th' other side SATAN allarm'd<br/>
Collecting all his might dilated stood,<br/>
Like TENERIFF or ATLAS unremov'd:<br/>
His stature reacht the Skie, and on his Crest<br/>
Sat horror Plum'd; nor wanted in his graspe<br/>
What seemd both Spear and Shield: now dreadful deeds<br/>
Might have ensu'd, nor onely Paradise<br/>
In this commotion, but the Starrie Cope<br/>
Of Heav'n perhaps, or all the Elements<br/>
At least had gon to rack, disturbd and torne<br/>
With violence of this conflict, had not soon<br/>
Th' Eternal to prevent such horrid fray<br/>
Hung forth in Heav'n his golden Scales, yet seen<br/>
Betwixt ASTREA and the SCORPION signe,<br/>
Wherein all things created first he weighd,<br/>
The pendulous round Earth with ballanc't Aire<br/>
In counterpoise, now ponders all events,<br/>
Battels and Realms: in these he put two weights<br/>
The sequel each of parting and of fight;<br/>
The latter quick up flew, and kickt the beam;<br/>
Which GABRIEL spying, thus bespake the Fiend.

SATAN, I know thy strength, and thou knowst mine,<br/>
Neither our own but giv'n; what follie then<br/>
To boast what Arms can doe, since thine no more<br/>
Then Heav'n permits, nor mine, though doubld now<br/>
To trample thee as mire: for proof look up,<br/>
And read thy Lot in yon celestial Sign<br/>
Where thou art weigh'd, & shown how light, how weak,<br/>
If thou resist. The Fiend lookt up and knew<br/>
His mounted scale aloft: nor more; but fled<br/>
Murmuring, and with him fled the shades of night.

<h2>Book 5</h2>

Now Morn, her rosy steps in the eastern clime<br/>
Advancing, sowed the earth with orient pearl,<br/>
When Adam waked, so customed; for his sleep<br/>
Was aerie light, from pure digestion bred,<br/>
And temperate vapours bland, which the only sound<br/>
Of leaves and fuming rills, Aurora's fan,<br/>
Lightly dispersed, and the shrill matin song<br/>
Of birds on every bough. So much the more<br/>
His wonder was to find unwakened Eve,<br/>
With tresses discomposed, and glowing cheek,<br/>
As through unquiet rest. He, on his side<br/>
Leaning half raised, with looks of cordial love<br/>
Hung over her enamoured, and beheld<br/>
Beauty which, whether waking or asleep,<br/>
Shot forth peculiar graces; then, with voice<br/>
Mild as when Zephyrus on Flora breathes,<br/>
Her hand soft touching, whispered thus:—"Awake,<br/>
My fairest, my espoused, my latest found,<br/>
Heaven's last, best gift, my ever-new delight!<br/>
Awake! the morning shines, and the fresh field<br/>
Calls us; we lose the prime to mark how spring<br/>
Our tended plants, how blows the citron grove,<br/>
What drops the myrrh, and what the balmy reed,<br/>
How Nature paints her colours, how the bee<br/>
Sits on the bloom extracting liquid sweet."

Such whispering waked her, but with startled eye<br/>
On Adam; whom imbracing, thus she spake:—

"O sole in whom my thoughts find all repose,<br/>
My glory, my perfection! glad I see<br/>
Thy face, and morn returned; for I this night<br/>
(Such night till this I never passed) have dreamed,<br/>
If dreamed, not, as I oft am wont, of thee,<br/>
Works of day past, or morrow's next design;<br/>
But of offence and trouble, which my mind<br/>
Knew never till this irksome night. Methought<br/>
Close at mine ear one called me forth to walk<br/>
With gentle voice; I thought it thine. It said,<br/>
'Why sleep'st thou, Eve? now is the pleasant time,<br/>
The cool, the silent, save where silence yields<br/>
To the night-warbling bird, that now awake<br/>
Tunes sweetest his love-laboured song; now reigns<br/>
Full-orbed the moon, and, with more pleasing light,<br/>
Shadowy sets off the face of things-in vain,<br/>
If none regard. Heaven wakes with all his eyes;<br/>
Whom to behold but thee, Nature's desire,<br/>
In whose sight all things joy, with ravishment<br/>
Attracted by thy beauty still to gaze?<br/>
I rose as at thy call, but found thee not:<br/>
To find thee I directed then my walk;<br/>
And on, methought, alone I passed through ways<br/>
That brought me on a sudden to the Tree<br/>
Of interdicted Knowledge. Fair it seemed,<br/>
Much fairer to my fancy than by day;<br/>
And, as I wondering looked, beside it stood<br/>
One shaped and winged like one of those from Heaven<br/>
By us oft seen: his dewy locks distilled<br/>
Ambrosia. On that Tree he also gazed;<br/>
And, 'O fair plant,' said he, 'with fruit surcharged,<br/>
Deigns none to ease thy load, and taste thy sweet,<br/>
Nor God nor Man? Is knowledge so despised?<br/>
Or envy, or what reserve forbids to taste?<br/>
Forbid who will, none shall from me withhold<br/>
Longer thy offered good, why else set here?<br/>
This said, he paused not, but with ventrous arm<br/>
He plucked, he tasted. Me damp horror chilled<br/>
At such bold words vouched with a deed so bold;<br/>
But he thus, overjoyed: 'O fruit divine,<br/>
Sweet of thyself, but much more sweet thus cropt,<br/>
Forbidden here, it seems, as only fit<br/>
For gods, yet able to make gods of men!<br/>
And why not gods of men, since good, the more<br/>
Communicated, more abundant grows,<br/>
The author not impaired, but honoured more?<br/>
Here, happy creature, fair angelic Eve!<br/>
Partake thou also: happy though thou art,<br/>
Happier thou may'st be, worthier canst not be.<br/>
Taste this, and be henceforth among the gods<br/>
Thyself a goddess; not to Earth confined,<br/>
But sometimes in the Air; as we; sometimes<br/>
Ascend to Heaven, by merit thine, and see<br/>
What life the gods live there, and such live thou.'<br/>
So saying, he drew nigh, and to me held,<br/>
Even to my mouth of that same fruit held part<br/>
Which he had plucked: the pleasant savoury smell<br/>
So quickened appetite that I, methought,<br/>
Could not but taste. Forthwith up to the clouds<br/>
With him I flew, and underneath beheld<br/>
The Earth outstretched immense, a prospect wide<br/>
And various. Wondering at my flight and change<br/>
To this high exaltation, suddenly<br/>
My guide was gone, and I, methought, sunk down,<br/>
And fell asleep; but, O, how glad I waked<br/>
To find this but a dream!" Thus Eve her night<br/>
Related, and thus Adam answered sad:—

"Best image of myself, and dearer half,<br/>
The trouble of thy thoughts this night in sleep<br/>
Affects me equally; nor can I like<br/>
This uncouth dream-of evil sprung, I fear;<br/>
Yet evil whence? In thee can harbour none,<br/>
Created pure. But know that in the soul<br/>
Are many lesser faculties, that serve<br/>
Reason as chief. Among these Fancy next<br/>
Her office holds; of all external things,<br/>
Which the five watchful senses represent,<br/>
She forms imaginations, aerie shapes,<br/>
Which Reason, joining or disjoining, frames<br/>
All what we affirm or what deny, and call<br/>
Our knowledge or opinion; then retires<br/>
Into her private cell when Nature rests.<br/>
Oft, in her absence, mimic Fancy wakes<br/>
To imitate her; but, misjoining shapes,<br/>
Wild work produces oft, and most in dreams,<br/>
Ill matching words and deeds long past or late.<br/>
Some such resemblances, methinks, I find<br/>
Of our last evening's talk in this thy dream,<br/>
But with addition strange. Yet be not sad:<br/>
Evil into the mind of God or Man<br/>
May come and go, so unapproved, and leave<br/>
No spot or blame behind; which gives me hope<br/>
That what in sleep thou didst abhor to dream<br/>
Waking thou never wilt consent to do.<br/>
Be not disheartened, then, nor cloud those looks,<br/>
That wont to be more cheerful and serene<br/>
Than when fair Morning first smiles on the world;<br/>
And let us to our feesh imployments rise<br/>
Among the groves, the fountains, and the flowers,<br/>
That open now their choicest bosomed smells,<br/>
Reserved from night, and kept for thee in store."

So cheered he his fair spouse; and she was cheered,<br/>
But silently a gentle tear let fall<br/>
From either eye, and wiped them with her hair:<br/>
Two other precious drops that ready stood,<br/>
Each in their crystal sluice, he, ere they fell,<br/>
Kissed as the gracious signs of sweet remorse<br/>
And pious awe, that feared to have offended.

So all was cleared, and to the field they haste.<br/>
But first, from under shady arborous roof<br/>
Soon as they forth were come to open sight<br/>
Of day-spring, and the Sun-who, scarce uprisen,<br/>
With wheels yet hovering o'er the ocean-brim,<br/>
Shot parallel to the Earth his dewy ray,<br/>
Discovering in wide lantskip all the east<br/>
Of Paradise and Eden's happy plains—<br/>
Lowly they bowed, adoring, and began<br/>
Their orisons, each morning duly paid<br/>
In various style; for neither various style<br/>
Nor holy rapture wanted they to praise<br/>
Their Maker, in fit strains pronounced, or sung<br/>
Unmeditated; such prompt eloquence<br/>
Flowed from their lips, in prose or numerous verse,<br/>
More tuneable than needed lute or harp<br/>
To add more sweetness. And they thus began:—

"These are thy glorious works, Parent of good,<br/>
Almighty! thine this universal frame,<br/>
Thus wondrous fair: Thyself how wondrous then!<br/>
Unspeakable! who sitt'st above these heavens<br/>
To us invisible, or dimly seen<br/>
In these thy lowest works; yet these declare<br/>
Thy goodness beyond thought, and power divine.<br/>
Speak, ye who best can tell, ye Sons of Light,<br/>
Angels-for ye behold him, and with songs<br/>
And choral symphonies, day without night,<br/>
Circle his throne rejoicing-ye in Heaven;<br/>
On Earth join, all ye creatures, to extol<br/>
Him first, him last, him midst, and without end.<br/>
Fairest of Stars, last in the train of Night,<br/>
If better thou belong not to the Dawn,<br/>
Sure pledge of day, that crown'st the smiling morn<br/>
With thy bright circlet, praise him in thy sphere<br/>
While day arises, that sweet hour of prime.<br/>
Thou Sun, of this great World both eye and soul,<br/>
Acknowledge him thy Greater; sound his praise<br/>
In thy eternal course, both when thou climb'st,<br/>
And when high noon hast gained, and when thou fall'st.<br/>
Moon, that now meet'st the orient Sun, now fliest,<br/>
With the fixed Stars, fixed in their orb that flies;<br/>
And ye five other wandering Fires, that move<br/>
In mystic dance, not without song, reasound<br/>
His praise who out of Darkness called up Light.<br/>
Air, and ye Elements, the eldest birth<br/>
Of Nature's womb, that in quaternion run<br/>
Perpetual circle, multiform, and mix<br/>
And nourish all things, let your ceaseless change<br/>
Vary to our great Maker still new praise.<br/>
Ye Mists and Exhalations, that now rise<br/>
From hill or steaming lake, dusky or gray,<br/>
Till the sun paint your fleecy skirts with gold,<br/>
In honour to the World's great Author rise;<br/>
Whether to deck with clouds the uncoloured sky,<br/>
Or wet the thirsty earth with falling showers,<br/>
Rising or falling, still advance his praise.<br/>
His praise, ye Winds, that from four quarters blow,<br/>
Breathe soft or loud; and wave your tops, ye Pines,<br/>
With every Plant, in sign of worship wave.<br/>
Fountains, and ye, that warble, as ye flow,<br/>
Melodious murmurs, warbling tune his praise.<br/>
Join voices, all ye living Souls. Ye Birds,<br/>
That, singing, up to Heaven-gate ascend,<br/>
Bear on your wings and in your notes his praise.<br/>
Ye that in waters glide, and ye that walk<br/>
The earth, and stately tread, or lowly creep,<br/>
Witness if I be silent, morn or even,<br/>
To hill or valley, fountain, or fresh shade,<br/>
Made vocal by my song, and taught his praise.<br/>
Hail, universal Lord! Be bounteous still<br/>
To give us only good; and, if the night<br/>
Have gathered aught of evil, or concealed,<br/>
Disperse it, as now light dispels the dark."

So prayed they innocent, and to their thoughts<br/>
Firm peace recovered soon, and wonted calm.<br/>
On to their morning's rural work they haste,<br/>
Among sweet dews and flowers, where any row<br/>
Of fruit-trees, over-woody, reached too far<br/>
Their pampered boughs, and needed hands to check<br/>
Fruitless imbraces; or they led the vine<br/>
To wed her elm; she, spoused, about him twines<br/>
Her marriageable arms, and with her brings<br/>
Her dower, the adopted clusters, to adorn<br/>
His barren leaves. Them thus imployed beheld<br/>
With pity Heaven's high King, and to him called<br/>
Raphael, the sociable Spirit, that deigned<br/>
To travel with Tobias, and secured<br/>
His marriage with the seven-times-wedded maid.

"Raphael," said he, "thou hear'st what stir on Earth<br/>
Satan, from Hell scaped through the darksome Gulf,<br/>
Hath raised in Paradise, and how disturbed<br/>
This night the human pair; now he designs<br/>
In them at once to ruin all mankind.<br/>
Go, therefore; half this day, as friend with friend,<br/>
Converse with Adam, in what bower or shade<br/>
Thou find'st him from the heat of noon retired<br/>
To respite his day-labour with repast<br/>
Or with repose; and such discourse bring on<br/>
As may advise him of his happy state—<br/>
Happiness in his power left free to will,<br/>
Left to his own free will, his will though free<br/>
Yet mutable. Whence warn him to beware<br/>
He swerve not, too secure: tell him withal<br/>
His danger, and from whom; what enemy,<br/>
Late fallen himself from Heaven, is plotting now<br/>
The fall of others from like state of bliss.<br/>
By violence? no, for that shall be withstood;<br/>
But by deceit and lies. This let him know,<br/>
Lest, wilfully transgressing, he pretend<br/>
Surprisal, unadmonished, unforewarned."

So spake the Eternal Father, and fulfilled<br/>
All justice. Nor delayed the winged Saint<br/>
After his charge received; but from among<br/>
Thousand celestial Ardours, where he stood<br/>
Veiled with his gorgeous wings, upspringing light,<br/>
Flew through the midst of Heaven. The angelic quires<br/>
On each hand parting, to his speed gave way<br/>
Through all the empyreal road, till, at the gate<br/>
Of Heaven arrived, the gate self-opened wide,<br/>
On golden hinges turning, as by work<br/>
Divine the sovran Architect had framed.<br/>
From hence-no cloud or, to obstruct his sight,<br/>
Star interposed, however small-he sees,<br/>
Not unconform to other shining globes,<br/>
Earth, and the Garden of God, with cedars crowned<br/>
Above all hills; as when by night the glass<br/>
Of Galileo, less assured, observes<br/>
Imagined lands and regions in the Moon;<br/>
Or pilot from amidst the Cyclades<br/>
Delos or Samos first appearing kens,<br/>
A cloudy spot. Down thither prone in flight<br/>
He speeds, and through the vast ethereal sky<br/>
Sails between worlds and worlds, with steady wing<br/>
Now on the polar winds; then with quick fan<br/>
Winnows the buxom air, till, within soar<br/>
Of towering eagles, to all the fowls he seems<br/>
A phoenix, gazed by all, as that sole bird,<br/>
When, to enshrine his relics in the Sun's<br/>
Bright temple, to Aegyptian Thebes he flies.<br/>
At once on the eastern cliff of Paradise<br/>
He lights, and to his proper shape returns,<br/>
A Seraph winged. Six wings he wore, to shade<br/>
His lineaments divine: the pair that clad<br/>
Each shoulder broad came mantling o'er his breast<br/>
With regal ornament; the middle pair<br/>
Girt like a starry zone his waist, and round<br/>
Skirted his loins and thighs with downy gold<br/>
And colours dipt in heaven; the third his feet<br/>
Shadowed from either heel with feathered mail,<br/>
Sky-tinctured grain. Like Maia's son he stood,<br/>
And shook his plumes, that heavenly fragrance filled<br/>
The circuit wide. Straight knew him all the bands<br/>
Of Angels under watch, and to his state<br/>
And to his message high in honour rise;<br/>
For on some message high they guessed him bound.<br/>
Their glittering tents he passed, and now is come<br/>
Into the blissful field, through groves of myrrh,<br/>
And flowering odours, cassia, nard, and balm,<br/>
Aawilderness of sweets; for Nature here<br/>
Wantoned as in her prime, and played at will<br/>
Her virgin fancies, pouring forth more sweet,<br/>
Wild above rule or art, enormous bliss.<br/>
Him, through the spicy forest onward come,<br/>
Adam discerned, as in the door he sat<br/>
Of his cool bower, while now the mounted Sun<br/>
Shot down direct his fervid rays, to warm<br/>
Earth's inmost womb, more warmth than Adam needs'<br/>
And Eve, within, due at her hour, prepared<br/>
For dinner savoury fruits, of taste to please<br/>
True appetite, and not disrelish thirst<br/>
Of nectarous draughts between, from milky stream,<br/>
Berry or grape: to whom thus Adam called:—

"Haste hither, Eve, and, worth thy sight, behold<br/>
Eastward among those trees what glorious Shape<br/>
Comes this way moving; seems another morn<br/>
Risen on mid-noon. Some great behest from Heaven<br/>
To us perhaps he brings, and will voutsafe<br/>
This day to be our guest. But go with speed,<br/>
And what thy stores contain bring forth, and pour<br/>
Abundance fit to honour and receive<br/>
Our heavenly stranger; well may we afford<br/>
Our givers their own gifts, and large bestow<br/>
From large bestowed, where Nature multiplies<br/>
Her fertile growth, and by disburdening grows<br/>
More fruitful; which instructs us not to spare."

To whom thus Eve:—"Adam, Earth's hallowed mould,<br/>
Of God inspired, small store will serve where store,<br/>
All seasons, ripe for use hangs on the stalk;<br/>
Save what, by frugal storing, firmness gains<br/>
To nourish, and superfluous moist consumes.<br/>
But I will haste, and from each bough and brake,<br/>
Each plant and juiciest gourd, will pluck such choice<br/>
To entertain our Angel-guest as he,<br/>
Beholding, shall confess that here on Earth<br/>
God hath dispensed his bounties as in Heaven."

So saying, with dispatchful looks in haste<br/>
She turns, on hospitable thoughts intent<br/>
What choice to choose for delicacy best,<br/>
What order so contrived as not to mix<br/>
Tastes, not well joined, inelegant, but bring<br/>
Taste after taste upheld with kindliest change:<br/>
Bestirs her then, and from each tender stalk<br/>
Whatever Earth, all-bearing mother, yields<br/>
In India East or West, or middle shore<br/>
In Pontus or the Punic coast, or where<br/>
Alcinous reigned, fruit of all kinds, in coat<br/>
Rough or smooth-rined, or bearded husk, or shell,<br/>
She gathers, tribute large, and on the board<br/>
Heaps with unsparing hand. For drink the grape<br/>
She crushes, inoffensive must, and meaths<br/>
From many a berry, and from sweet kernels pressed<br/>
She tempers dulcet creams-nor those to hold<br/>
Wants her fit vessels pure; then strews the ground<br/>
With rose and odours from the shrub unfumed.

Meanwhile our primitive great Sire, to meet<br/>
His godlike guest, walks forth, without more train<br/>
Accompanied than with his own complete<br/>
Perfections; in himself was all his state,<br/>
More solemn than the tedious pomp that waits<br/>
On princes, when their rich retin'ue long<br/>
Of horses led and grooms besmeared with gold<br/>
Dazzles the crowd and sets them all agape.<br/>
Nearer his presence, Adam, though not awed,<br/>
Yet with submiss approach and reverence meek,<br/>
As to a superior nature, bowing low,<br/>
Thus said:—"Native of Heaven (for other place<br/>
None can than Heaven such glorious Shape contain),<br/>
Since, by descending from the Thrones above,<br/>
Those happy places thou hadst deigned a while<br/>
To want, and honour these, voutsafe with us,<br/>
Two only, who yet by sovran gift possess<br/>
This spacious ground, in yonder shady bower<br/>
To rest, and what the Garden choicest bears<br/>
To sit and taste, till this meridian heat<br/>
Be over, and the sun more cool decline."

Whom thus the angelic Virtue answered mild:—<br/>
"Adam, I therefore came; nor art thou such<br/>
Created, or such place hast here to dwell,<br/>
As may not oft invite, though Spirits of Heaven,<br/>
To visit thee. Lead on, then, where thy bower<br/>
O'ershades; for these mid-hours, till evening rise,<br/>
I have at will. "So to the sylvan lodge<br/>
They came, that like Pomona's arbour smiled,<br/>
With flowerets decked and fragrant smells. But Eve,<br/>
Undecked, save with herself, more lovely fair<br/>
Than wood-nymph, or the fairest goddess feigned<br/>
Of three that in Mount Ida naked strove,<br/>
Stood to entertain her guest from Heaven; no veil<br/>
She needed, virtue-proof; no thought infirm<br/>
Altered her cheek. On whom the Angel "Hail!"<br/>
Bestowed-the holy salutation used<br/>
Long after to blest Mary, second Eve:—

"Hail! Mother of mankind, whose fruitful womb<br/>
Shall fill the world more numerous with thy sons<br/>
Than with these various fruits the trees of God<br/>
Have heaped this table!" Raised of grassy turf<br/>
Their table was, and mossy seats had round,<br/>
And on her ample square, from side to side,<br/>
All Autumn piled, though Spring and Autumn here<br/>
Danced hand-in-hand. A while discourse they hold—<br/>
No fear lest dinner cool-when thus began<br/>
Our Author:—"Heavenly Stranger, please to taste<br/>
These bounties, which our Nourisher, from whom<br/>
All perfect good, unmeasured-out, descends.<br/>
To us for food and for delight hath caused<br/>
The Earth to yield: unsavoury food, perhaps,<br/>
To Spiritual Natures; only this I know,<br/>
That one Celestial Father gives to all."

To whom the Angel:—"Therefore, what he gives<br/>
(Whose praise be ever sung) to Man, in part<br/>
Spiritual, may of purest Spirits be found<br/>
No ingrateful food: and food alike those pure<br/>
Intelligential substances require<br/>
As doth your Rational; and both contain<br/>
Within them every lower faculty<br/>
Of sense, whereby they hear, see, smell, touch, taste,<br/>
Tasting concoct, digest, assimilate,<br/>
And corporeal to incorporeal turn.<br/>
For know, whatever was created needs<br/>
To be sustained and fed. Of Elements<br/>
The grosser feeds the purer: Earth the Sea;<br/>
Earth and the Sea feed Air; the Air those Fires<br/>
Ethereal, and, as lowest, first the Moon;<br/>
Whence in her visage round those spots, unpurged,<br/>
Vapours not yet into her substance turned.<br/>
Nor doth the Moon no nourishment exhale<br/>
From her moist continent to higher Orbs.<br/>
The Sun, that light imparts to all, receives<br/>
From all his alimental recompense<br/>
In humid exhalations, and at even<br/>
Sups with the Ocean. Though in Heaven the trees<br/>
Of life ambrosial fruitage bear, and vines<br/>
Yield nectar-though from off the boughs each morn<br/>
We brush mellifluous dews and find the ground<br/>
Covered with pearly grain-yet God hath here<br/>
Varied his bounty so with new delights<br/>
As may compare with Heaven; and to taste<br/>
Think not I shall be nice." So down they sat,<br/>
And to their viands fell; nor seemingly<br/>
The Angel, nor in mist-the common gloss<br/>
Of theologians-but with keen dispatch<br/>
Of real hunger, and concoctive heat<br/>
To transubstantiate: what redounds transpires<br/>
Through Spirits with ease; nor wonder, if by fire<br/>
Of sooty coal the Empiric Alchimist<br/>
Can turn, or holds it possible to turn,<br/>
Metals of drossiest ore to perfect gold,<br/>
As from the mine. Meanwhile at table Eve<br/>
Ministered naked, and their flowing cups<br/>
With pleasant liquors crowned. O innocence<br/>
Deserving Paradise! If ever, then,<br/>
Then had the Sons of God excuse to have been<br/>
Enamoured at that sight. But in those hearts<br/>
Love unlibidinous reigned, nor jealousy<br/>
Was understood, the injured lover's hell.

Thus when with meats and drinks they had sufficed,<br/>
Not burdened nature, sudden mind arose<br/>
In Adam not to let the occasion pass,<br/>
Given him by this great conference, to know<br/>
Of things above his world, and of their being<br/>
Who dwell in Heaven, whose excellence he saw<br/>
Transcend his own so far, whose radiant forms,<br/>
Divine effulgence, whose high power so far<br/>
Exceeded human; and his wary speech<br/>
Thus to the empyreal minister he framed:—

Inhabitant with God, now know I well<br/>
They favour, in this honour done to Man;<br/>
Under whose lowly roof thou hast voutsafed<br/>
To enter, and these earthly fruits to taste,<br/>
Food not of Angels, yet accepted so<br/>
As that more willingly thou couldst not seem<br/>
At Heaven's high feasts to have fed: yet what compare!"<br/>
To whom the winged Hierarch replied:—<br/>
"O Adam, one almighty is, from whom<br/>
All things proceed, and up to him return,<br/>
If not depraved from good, created all<br/>
Such to perfection; one first matter all,<br/>
Indued with various forms, various degrees<br/>
Of substance, and, in things that live, of life;<br/>
But more refined, more spiritous and pure,<br/>
As nearer to him placed or nearer tending<br/>
Each in their several active spheres assigned,<br/>
Till body up to spirit work, in bounds<br/>
Proportioned to each kind. So from the root<br/>
Springs lighter the green stalk, from thence the leaves<br/>
More aerie, last the bright consummate flower<br/>
Spirits odorous breathes: flowers and their fruit,<br/>
Man's nourishment, by gradual scale sublimed,<br/>
To vital spirits aspire, to animal,<br/>
To intellectual; give both life and sense,<br/>
Fancy and understanding; whence the Soul<br/>
Reason receives, and Reason is her being,<br/>
Discursive, or Intuitive: Discourse<br/>
Is oftest yours, the latter most is ours,<br/>
Differing but in degree, of kind the same.<br/>
Wonder not, then, what God for you saw good<br/>
If I refuse not, but convert, as you,<br/>
To proper substance. Time may come when Men<br/>
With Angels may participate, and find<br/>
No inconvenient diet, nor too light fare;<br/>
And from these corporal nutriments, perhaps,<br/>
Your bodies may at last turn all to spirit,<br/>
Improved by tract of time, and winged ascend<br/>
Ethereal, as we, or may at choice<br/>
Here or in heavenly paradises dwell,<br/>
If ye be found obedient, and retain<br/>
Unalterably firm his love entire<br/>
Whose progeny you are. Meanwhile enjoy,<br/>
Your fill, what happiness this happy state<br/>
Can comprehend, incapable of more."

To whom the Patriarch of Mankind replied:—<br/>
"O favourable Spirit, propitious guest,<br/>
Well hast thou taught the way that might direct<br/>
Our knowledge, and the scale of Nature set<br/>
From centre to circumference, whereon,<br/>
In contemplation of created things,<br/>
By steps we may ascend to God. But say,<br/>
What meant that caution joined, If ye be found<br/>
Obedient? Can we want obedience, then,<br/>
To him, or possibly his love desert,<br/>
Who formed us from the dust, and placed us here<br/>
Full to the utmost measure of what bliss<br/>
Human desires can seek or apprehend?"

To whom the Angel:—"Son of Heaven and Earth,<br/>
Attend! That thou art happy, owe to God;<br/>
That thou continuest such, owe to thyself,<br/>
That is, to thy obedience; therein stand.<br/>
This was that caution given thee; be advised.<br/>
God made thee perfect, not immutable;<br/>
And good he made thee; but to persevere<br/>
He left it in thy power-ordained thy will<br/>
By nature free, not over-ruled by fate<br/>
Inextricable, or strict necessity.<br/>
Our voluntary service he requires,<br/>
Not our necessitated. Such with him<br/>
Finds no acceptance, nor can find; for how<br/>
Can hearts not free be tried whether they serve<br/>
Willing or no, who will but what they must<br/>
By destiny, and can no other choose?<br/>
Myself, and all the Angelic Host, that stand<br/>
In sight of god enthroned, our happy state<br/>
Hold, as you yours, while our obedience holds.<br/>
On other surety none: freely we serve,<br/>
Because we freely love, as in our will<br/>
To love or not; in this we stand or fall.<br/>
And some are fallen, to disobedience fallen,<br/>
And so from Heaven to deepest Hell. Of fall<br/>
From what high state of bliss into what woe!"

To whom our great Progenitor:—"Thy words<br/>
Attentive, and with more delighted ear,<br/>
Divine instructor, I have heard, than when<br/>
Cherubic songs by night from neighbouring hills<br/>
Aerial music send. Nor knew I not<br/>
To be, both will and deed, created free.<br/>
Yet that we never shall forget to love<br/>
Our Maker, and obey him whose command<br/>
Single is yet so just, my constant thoughts<br/>
Assured me, and still assure; though what thou tell'st<br/>
Hath passed in Heaven some doubt within me move,<br/>
But more desire to hear, if thou consent,<br/>
The full relation, which must needs be strange,<br/>
Worthy of sacred silence to be heard.<br/>
And we have yet large day, for scarce the Sun<br/>
Hath finished half his journey, and scarce begins<br/>
His other half in the great zone of heaven."

Thus Adam made request; and Raphael,<br/>
After short pause assenting, thus began:—

"High matter thou injoin'st me, O prime of Men—<br/>
Sad task and hard; for how shall I relate<br/>
To human sense the invisible exploits<br/>
Of warring Spirits? how, without remorse,<br/>
The ruin of so many, glorious once<br/>
And perfect while they stood? how, last, unfold<br/>
The secrets of another world, perhaps<br/>
Not lawful to reveal? Yet for thy good<br/>
This is dispensed; and what surmounts the reach<br/>
Of human sense I shall delineate so,<br/>
By likening spiritual to corporal forms,<br/>
As may express them best-though what if Earth<br/>
Be but the shadow of Heaven, and things therein<br/>
Each to other like more than on Earth is thought!

"As yet this World was not, and Chaos wild<br/>
Reigned where these heavens now rowl, where Earth now rests<br/>
Upon her centre poised, when on a day<br/>
(For Time, though in Eternity, applied<br/>
To motion, measures all things durable<br/>
By present, past, and future), on such day<br/>
As Heaven's great year brings forth, the empyreal host<br/>
Of Angels, by imperial summons called,<br/>
Innumerable before the Almighty's throne<br/>
Forthwith from all the ends of Heaven appeared<br/>
Under their hierarchs in orders bright.<br/>
Ten thousand thousand ensigns high advanced,<br/>
Standards and gonfalons, 'twixt van and rear<br/>
Stream in the air, and for distinction serve<br/>
Of hierarchies, of orders, and degrees:<br/>
Or in their glittering tissues bear imblazed<br/>
Holy memorials, acts of zeal and love<br/>
Recorded eminent. Thus when in orbs<br/>
Of circuit inexpressible they stood,<br/>
Orb within orb, the Father Infinite,<br/>
By whom in bliss imbosomed sat the Son,<br/>
Amidst, as from a flaming Mount, whose top<br/>
Brightness had made invisible, thus spake:

"'Hear, all ye Angels, Progeny of Light,<br/>
Thrones, Dominations, Princedoms, Virtues, Powers,<br/>
Hear my decree, which unrevoked shall stand!<br/>
This day I have begot whom I declare<br/>
My only Son, and on this holy hill<br/>
Him have anointed, whom ye now behold<br/>
At my right hand. Your head I him appoint,<br/>
And by myself have sworn to him shall bow<br/>
All knees in Heaven, and shall confess him Lord.<br/>
Under his great vicegerent reign abide,<br/>
United as one individual soul,<br/>
For ever happy. Him who disobeys<br/>
Me disobeys, breaks union, and, that day,<br/>
Cast out form God and blessed vision, falls<br/>
Into utter darkness, deep ingulfed, his place<br/>
Ordained without redemption, without end.'

"So spake the Omnipotent, and with his words<br/>
All seemed well pleased; all seemed, but were not all.<br/>
That day, as other solemn days, they spent<br/>
In song and dance about the sacred Hill—<br/>
Mystical dance, which yonder starry sphere<br/>
Of planets and of fixed in all her wheels<br/>
Resembles, nearest; mazes intricate,<br/>
Eccentric, intervolved, yet regular<br/>
Then most when most irregular they seem;<br/>
And in their motions harmony divine<br/>
So smooths her charming tones that God's own ear<br/>
Listens delighted. Evening now approached<br/>
(For we have also our evening and our morn—<br/>
We ours for change delectable, not need);<br/>
Forthwith from dance to sweet repast they turn<br/>
Desirous: all in circles as they stood,<br/>
Tables are set, and on a sudden piled<br/>
With Angels' food; and rubied nectar flows<br/>
In pearl, in diamond, and massy gold,<br/>
Fruit of delicious vines, the growth of Heaven.<br/>
On flowers reposed, and with fresh flowerets crowned,<br/>
They eat, they drink, and in communion sweet<br/>
Quaff immortality and joy, secure<br/>
Of surfeit where full measure only bounds<br/>
Excess, before the all-bounteous King, who showered<br/>
With copious hand, rejoicing in their joy.<br/>
Now when ambrosial Night, with clouds exhaled<br/>
From that high mount of God whence light and shade<br/>
Spring both, the face of brightest Heaven had changed<br/>
To grateful twilight (for Night comes not there<br/>
In darker veil), and roseate dews disposed<br/>
All but the unsleeping eyes of God to rest,<br/>
Wide over all the plain, and wider far<br/>
Than all this globous Earth in plain outspread<br/>
(Such are the Courts of God), the Angelic throng,<br/>
Dispersed in bands and files, their camp extend<br/>
By living streams among the trees of life—<br/>
Pavilions numberless and sudden reared,<br/>
Celestial tabernacles, where they slept,<br/>
Fanned with cool winds; save those who, in their course,<br/>
Melodious hymns about the sovran Throne<br/>
Alternate all night long. But not so waked<br/>
Satan-so call him now; his former name<br/>
Is heard no more in Heaven. He, of the first,<br/>
If not the first Archangel, great in power,<br/>
In favour, and preeminence, yet fraught<br/>
With envy against the Son of God, that day<br/>
Honoured by his great Father, and proclaimed<br/>
Messiah, King Anointed, could not bear,<br/>
Through pride, that sight, and thought himself impaired.<br/>
Deep malice thence conceiving and disdain,<br/>
Soon as midnight brought on the dusky hour<br/>
Friendliest to sleep and silence, he resolved<br/>
With all his legions to dislodge, and leave<br/>
Unworshiped, unobeyed, the Throne supreme.<br/>
Contemptuous, and, his next subordinate<br/>
Awakening, thus to him in secret spake:—

"'Sleep'st thou, companion dear? what sleep can close<br/>
Thy eyelids? and rememberest what decree,<br/>
Of yesterday, so late hath passed the lips<br/>
Of Heaven's Almighty? Thou to me thy thoughts<br/>
Wast wont, I mine to thee was wont, to impart;<br/>
Both waking we were one; how, then, can now<br/>
Thy sleep dissent? New laws thou seest imposed;<br/>
New laws from him who reigns new minds may raise<br/>
In us who serve-new counsels, to debate<br/>
What doubtful may ensue. More in this place<br/>
To utter is not safe. Assemble thou<br/>
Of all those myriads which we lead the chief;<br/>
Tell them that, by command, ere yet dim Night<br/>
Her shadowy cloud withdraws, I am to haste,<br/>
And all who under me their banners wave,<br/>
Homeward with flying march where we possess<br/>
The Quarters of the North, there to prepare<br/>
Fit entertainment to receive our King,<br/>
The great Messiah, and his new commands,<br/>
Who speedily through all the Hierarchies<br/>
Intends to pass triumphant, and give laws.'

"So spake the false Archangel, and infused<br/>
Bad influence into the unwary breast<br/>
Of his associate. He together calls,<br/>
Or several one by one, the regent Powers,<br/>
Under him regent; tells, as he was taught,<br/>
That, the Most High commanding, now ere Night,<br/>
Now ere dim Night had disincumbered Heaven,<br/>
The great hierarchal standard was to move;<br/>
Tells the suggested cause, and casts between<br/>
Ambiguous words and jealousies, to sound<br/>
Or taint integrity. But all obeyed<br/>
The wonted signal, and superior voice<br/>
Of their great Potentate; for great indeed<br/>
His name, and high was his degree in Heaven:<br/>
His countenance, as the morning-star that guides<br/>
The starry flock allured them, and with lies<br/>
Drew after him the third part of Heaven's host.<br/>
Meanwhile, the Eternal Eye, whose sight discerns<br/>
Abstrusest thoughts, from forth his holy Mount,<br/>
And from within the golden Lamps that burn<br/>
Nightly before him, saw without their light<br/>
Rebellion rising-saw in whom, how spread<br/>
Among the Sons of Morn, what multitudes<br/>
Were banded to oppose his high decree;<br/>
And, smiling, to his only Son thus said:—

"'Son, thou in whom my glory I behold<br/>
In full resplendence, Heir of all my might,<br/>
Nearly it now concerns us to be sure<br/>
Of our Omnipotence, and with what arms<br/>
We mean to hold what anciently we claim<br/>
Of deity or empire: such a foe<br/>
Is rising, who intends to erect his throne<br/>
Equal to ours, throughout the spacious North;<br/>
Nor so content, hath in his thought to try<br/>
In battle what our power is or our right.<br/>
Let us advise, and to this hazard draw<br/>
With speed what force is left, and all imploy<br/>
In our defence, lest unawares we lose<br/>
This our high place, our Sanctuary, our Hill.'

"To whom the Son, with calm aspect and clear<br/>
Lightening divine, ineffable, serene,<br/>
Made answer:—'Mighty Father, thou thy foes<br/>
Justly hast in derision, and secure<br/>
Laugh'st at their vain designs and tumults vain—<br/>
Matter to me of glory, whom their hate<br/>
Illustrates, when they see all regal power<br/>
Given me to quell their pride, and in event<br/>
Know whether I be dextrous to subdue<br/>
Thy rebels, or be found the worst in Heaven.'

"So spake the Son; but Satan with his Powers<br/>
Far was advanced on winged speed, an host<br/>
Innumerable as the stars of night,<br/>
Or stars of morning, dew-drops which the sun<br/>
Impearls on every leaf and every flower.<br/>
Regions they passed, the mighty regencies<br/>
Of Seraphim and Potentates and Thrones<br/>
In their triple degrees-regions to which<br/>
All thy dominion, Adam, is no more<br/>
Than what this garden is to all the earth<br/>
And all the sea, from one entire globose<br/>
Stretched into longitude; which having passed,<br/>
At length into the limits of the North<br/>
They came, and Satan to his royal seat<br/>
High on a hill, far-blazing, as a mount<br/>
Raised on a mount, with pyramids and towers<br/>
From diamond quarries hewn and rocks of gold—<br/>
The palace of great Lucifer (so call<br/>
That structure, in the dialect of men<br/>
Interpreted) which, not long after, he,<br/>
Affecting all equality with God,<br/>
In imitation of that mount whereon<br/>
Messiah was declared in sight of Heaven,<br/>
The Mountain of the Congregation called;<br/>
For thither he assembled all his train,<br/>
Pretending so commanded to consult<br/>
About the great reception of their King<br/>
Thither to come, and with calumnious art<br/>
Of counterfeited truth thus held their ears:—

"'Thrones, Dominations, Princedoms, Virtues, Powers—<br/>
If these magnific titles yet remain<br/>
Not merely titular, since by decree<br/>
Another now hath to himself ingrossed<br/>
All power, and us eclipsed under the name<br/>
Of King Anointed; for whom all this haste<br/>
Of midnight march, and hurried meeting here,<br/>
This only to consult, how we may best,<br/>
With what may be devised of honours new,<br/>
Receive him coming to receive from us<br/>
Knee-tribute yet unpaid, prostration vile!<br/>
Too much to one! but double how endured—<br/>
To one and to his image now proclaimed?<br/>
But what if better counsels might erect<br/>
Our minds, and teach us to cast off this yoke!<br/>
Will ye submit your necks, and choose to bend<br/>
The supple knee? Ye will not, if I trust<br/>
To know ye right, or if ye know yourselves<br/>
Natives and Sons of Heaven possessed before<br/>
By none, and, if not equal all, yet free,<br/>
Equally free; for orders and degrees<br/>
Jar not with liberty, but well consist.<br/>
Who can in reason, then, or right, assume<br/>
Monarchy over such as live by right<br/>
His equals-if in power and splendour less,<br/>
In freedom equal? or can introduce<br/>
Law and edict on us, who without law<br/>
Err not? much less for this to be our Lord,<br/>
And look for adoration, to the abuse<br/>
Of those imperial titles which assert<br/>
Our being ordained to govern, not to serve!

"Thus far his bold discourse without control<br/>
Had audience, when, among the Seraphim,<br/>
Abdiel, than whom none with more zeal adored<br/>
The Deity, and divine commands obeyed,<br/>
Stood up, and in a flame of zeal severe<br/>
The current of his fury thus opposed:—

"'O argument blasphe mous, false, and proud—<br/>
Words which no ear ever to hear in Heaven<br/>
Expected; least of all from thee, ingrate,<br/>
In place thyself so high above thy peers!<br/>
Canst thou with impious obloquy condemn<br/>
The just decree of God, pronounced and sworn,<br/>
That to his only Son, by right endued<br/>
With regal sceptre, every soul in Heaven<br/>
Shall bend the knee, and in that honour due<br/>
Confess him rightful King? Unjust, thou say'st,<br/>
Flatly unjust, to bind with laws the free,<br/>
And equal over equals to let reign,<br/>
One over all with unsucceeded power!<br/>
Shalt thou give law to God? shalt thou dispute<br/>
With Him the points of liberty, who made<br/>
Thee what Thou art, and formed the Powers of Heaven<br/>
Such as he pleased, and circumscribed their being?<br/>
Yet, by experience taught, we know how good,<br/>
And of our good and of our dignity<br/>
How provident, he is-how far from thought<br/>
To make us less; bent rather to exalt<br/>
Our happy state, under one Head more near<br/>
United. But-to grant it thee unjust<br/>
That equal over equals monarch reign—<br/>
Thyself, though great and glorious, dost thou count,<br/>
Or all angelic nature joined in one,<br/>
Equal to him, begotten Son, by whom,<br/>
As by his Word, the mighty Father made<br/>
All things, even thee, and all the Spirits of Heaven<br/>
By him created in their bright degrees,<br/>
Crowned them with glory, and to their glory named<br/>
Thrones, Dominations, Princedoms, Virtues, Powers?—<br/>
Essential Powers; nor by his reign obscured,<br/>
But more illustrious made; since he, the head,<br/>
One of our number thus reduced becomes;<br/>
His laws our laws; all honour to him done<br/>
Returns our own. Cease, then, this impious rage,<br/>
And tempt not these; but hasten to appease<br/>
The incensed Father and the incensed Son<br/>
While pardon may be found, in time besought.'

"So spake the fervent Angel; but his zeal<br/>
None seconded, as out of season judged,<br/>
Or singular and rash. Whereat rejoiced<br/>
The Apostat, and, more haughty, thus replied:—

"'That we were formed, then, say'st thou? and the work<br/>
Of secondary hands, by task transferred<br/>
From Father to his Son? Strange point and new!<br/>
Doctrine which we would know whence learned! Who saw<br/>
When this creation was? Remember'st thou<br/>
Thy making, while the Maker gave thee being?<br/>
We know no time when we were not as now;<br/>
Know none before us, self-begot, self-raised<br/>
By our own quickening power when fatal course<br/>
Had circled his full orb, the birth mature<br/>
Of this our native Heaven, Ethereal Sons.<br/>
Our puissance is our own; our own right hand<br/>
Shall teach us highest deeds, by proof to try<br/>
Who is our equal. Then thou shalt behold<br/>
Whether by supplication we intend<br/>
Address, and to begirt the Almighty Throne<br/>
Beseeching or besieging. This report,<br/>
These tidings, carry to the Anointed King;<br/>
And fly, ere evil intercept thy flight.'

"He said; and, as the sound of waters deep,<br/>
Hoarse murmur echoed to his words applause<br/>
Through the infinite Host. Nor less for that<br/>
The flaming Seraph, fearless, though alone,<br/>
Encompassed round with foes, thus answered bold:—

"'O alienate from God, O Spirit accursed,<br/>
Forsaken of all good! I see thy fall<br/>
Determined, and thy hapless crew involved<br/>
In this perfidious fraud, contagion spread<br/>
Both of thy crime and punishment. Henceforth<br/>
No more be troubled how to quit the yoke<br/>
Of God's Messiah. Those indulgent laws<br/>
Will not be now voutsafed; other decrees<br/>
Against thee are gone forth without recall;<br/>
That golden sceptre which thou didst reject<br/>
Is now an iron rod to bruise and break<br/>
Thy disobedience. Well thou didst advise;<br/>
Yet not for thy advice or threats I fly<br/>
These wicked tents devoted, lest the wrauth<br/>
Impendent, raging into sudden flame,<br/>
Distinguish not: for soon expect to feel<br/>
His thunder on thy head, devouring fire.<br/>
Then who can created thee lamenting learn<br/>
When who can uncreate thee thou shalt know.'

"So spake the Seraph Abdiel, faithful found;<br/>
Among the faithless faithful only he;<br/>
Among innumerable false unmoved,<br/>
Unshaken, unseduced, unterrified,<br/>
His loyalty he kept, his love, his zeal;<br/>
Nor number nor example with him wrought<br/>
To swerve from truth, or change his constant mind,<br/>
Though single. From amidst them forth he passed,<br/>
Long way through hostile scorn, which he sustained<br/>
Superior, nor of violence feared aught;<br/>
And with retorted scorn his back he turned<br/>
On those proud towers, to swift destruction doomed."

<h2>Book 6</h2>

"All night the dreadless Angel, unpursued,<br/>
Through Heaven's wide champaign held his way, till Morn,<br/>
Waked by the circling Hours, with rosy hand<br/>
Unbarred the gates of Light. There is a cave<br/>
Within the Mount of God, fast by his Throne,<br/>
Where Light and Darkness in perpetual round<br/>
Lodge and dislodge by turns-which makes through Heaven<br/>
Grateful vicissitude, like day and night;<br/>
Light issues forth, and at the other door<br/>
Obsequious Darkness enters, till her hour<br/>
To veil the heaven, though darkness there might well<br/>
Seem twilight here. And now went forth the Morn<br/>
Such as in highest heaven, arrayed in gold<br/>
Empyreal; from before her vanished Night,<br/>
Shot through with orient beams; when all the pain<br/>
Covered with thick embattled squadrons bright,<br/>
Chariots, and flaming arms, and fiery steeds,<br/>
Reflecting blaze on blaze, first met his view.<br/>
War he perceived, war in precinct, and found<br/>
Already known what he for news had thought<br/>
To have reported. Gladly then he mixed<br/>
Among those friendly Powers, who him received<br/>
With joy and acclamations loud, that one,<br/>
That of so many myriads fallen yet one,<br/>
Returned not lost. On to the sacred Hill<br/>
They led him, high applauded, and present<br/>
Before the Seat supreme; from whence a voice,<br/>
From midst a golden cloud, thus mild was heard:—

"'Servant of God, well done! Well hast thou fought<br/>
The better fight, who single hast maintained<br/>
Against revolted multitudes the cause<br/>
Of truth, in word mightier than they in arms,<br/>
And for the testimony of truth hast borne<br/>
Universal reproach, far worse to bear<br/>
Than violence; for this was all thy care—<br/>
To stand approved in sight of God, though worlds<br/>
Judged thee perverse. The easier conquest now<br/>
Remains thee-aided by this host of friends,<br/>
Back on thy foes more glorious to return<br/>
Than scorned thou didst depart: and to subdue,<br/>
By force who reason for their law refuse—<br/>
Right reason for their law, and for their King<br/>
Messiah, who by right of merit reigns.<br/>
Go, Michael, of celestial armies prince,<br/>
And thou, in military prowess next,<br/>
Gabriel; lead forth to battle these my sons<br/>
Invincible; lead forth my armed Saints,<br/>
By thousands and by millions ranged for fight,<br/>
Equal in number to that godless crew<br/>
Rebellious. Them with fire and hostile arms<br/>
Fearless assault; and, to the brow of Heaven<br/>
Pursuing, drive them out from God and bliss<br/>
Into their place of punishment, the gulf<br/>
Of Tartarus, which ready opens wide<br/>
His fiery chaos to receive their fall.'

"So spake the Sovran Voice; and clouds began<br/>
To darken all the Hill, and smoke to rowl<br/>
In dusky wreaths reluctant flames, the sign<br/>
Of wrauth awaked; nor with less dread the loud<br/>
Ethereal trumpet from on high gan blow.<br/>
At which command the Powers Militant<br/>
That stood for Heaven, in mighty quadrate joined<br/>
Of union irresistible, moved on<br/>
In silence their bright legions to the sound<br/>
Of instrumental harmony, that breathed<br/>
Heroic ardour to adventurous deeds<br/>
Under their godlike leaders, in the cause<br/>
Of God and his Messiah. On they move,<br/>
Indissolubly firm; nor obvious hill,<br/>
Nor straitening vale, nor wood, nor stream, divides<br/>
Their perfect ranks; for high above the ground<br/>
Their march was, and the passive air upbore<br/>
Their nimble tread. As when the total kind<br/>
Of birds, in orderly array on wing,<br/>
Came summoned over Eden to receive<br/>
Their names of thee; so over many a tract<br/>
Of Heaven they marched, and many a province wide,<br/>
Tenfold the length of this terrene. At last<br/>
Far in the horizon, to the north, appeared<br/>
From skirt to skirt a fiery region, stretched<br/>
In battailous aspect; and, nearer view,<br/>
Bristled with upright beams innumerable<br/>
Of rigid spears, and helmets thronged, and shields<br/>
Various, with boastful argument portrayed,<br/>
The banded Powers of Satan hasting on<br/>
With furious expedition: for they weened<br/>
That self-same day, by fight or by surprise,<br/>
To win the Mount of God, and on his Throne<br/>
To set the envier of his state, the proud<br/>
Aspirer. But their thoughts proved fond and vain<br/>
In the mid-way; though strange to us it seemed<br/>
At first that Angel should with Angel war,<br/>
And in fierce hosting meet, who wont to meet<br/>
So oft in festivals of joy and love<br/>
Unanimous, as sons of one great Sire,<br/>
Hymning the Eternal Father. But the shout<br/>
Of battle now began, and rushing sound<br/>
Of onset ended soon each milder thought.<br/>
High in the midst, exalted as a God,<br/>
The Apostat in his sun-bright chariot sat,<br/>
Idol of majesty divine, enclosed<br/>
With flaming Cherubim and golden shields;<br/>
Then lighted from his gorgeous Throne-for now<br/>
'Twixt host and host but narrow space was left,<br/>
A dreadful interval, and front to front<br/>
Presented stood, in terrible array<br/>
Of hideous length. Before the cloudy van,<br/>
On the rough edge of battle ere it joined,<br/>
Satan, with vast and haughty strides advanced,<br/>
Came towering, armed in adamant and gold.<br/>
Abdiel that sight endured not, where he stood<br/>
Among the mightiest, bent on highest deeds,<br/>
And thus his own undaunted heart explores:—

"'O Heaven! that such resemblance of the Highest<br/>
Should yet remain, where faith and realty<br/>
Remain not! Wherefore should not strength and might<br/>
There fail where virtue fails, or weakest prove<br/>
Where boldest, though to sight unconquerable?<br/>
His puissance, trusting in the Almighty's aid,<br/>
I mean to try, whose reason I have tried<br/>
Unsound and false; nor is it aught but just<br/>
That he who in debate of truth hath won<br/>
Should win in arms, in both disputes alike<br/>
Victor. Though brutish that contest' and foul,<br/>
When reason hath to deal with force, yet so<br/>
Most reason is that reason overcome.'

"So pondering, and from his armed peers<br/>
Forth-stepping opposite, half-way he met<br/>
His daring foe, at this prevention more<br/>
Incensed, and thus securely him defied:—

"'Proud, art thou met? Thy hope was to have reached<br/>
The highth of thy aspiring unopposed—<br/>
The Throne of God unguarded, and his side<br/>
Abandoned at the terror of thy power<br/>
Or potent tongue. Fool! not to think how vain<br/>
Against the Omnipotent to rise in arms;<br/>
Who, out of smallest things, could without end<br/>
Have raised incessant armies to defeat<br/>
Thy folly; or with solitary hand,<br/>
Reaching beyond all limit, at one blow,<br/>
Unaided could have finished thee, and whelmed<br/>
Thy legions under darkness! But thou seest<br/>
All are not of thy train; there be who faith<br/>
Prefer, and piety to God, though then<br/>
To thee not visible when I alone<br/>
Seemed in thy world erroneous to dissent<br/>
From all: my Sect thou seest; now learn too late<br/>
How few sometimes may know when thousands err.'

"Whom the grand Foe, with scornful eye askance,<br/>
Thus answered:—'Ill for thee, but in wished hour<br/>
Of my revenge, first sought for, thou return'st<br/>
From flight, seditious Angel, to receive<br/>
Thy merited reward, the first assay<br/>
Of this right hand provoked, since first that tongue,<br/>
Inspired with contradiction, durst oppose<br/>
A third part of the Gods, in synod met<br/>
Their deities to assert: who, while they feel<br/>
Vigour divine within them, can allow<br/>
Omnipotence to none. But well thou com'st<br/>
Before thy fellows, ambitious to win<br/>
From me some plume, that thy success may show<br/>
Destruction to the rest. This pause between<br/>
(Unanswered lest thou boast) to let thee know.—<br/>
At first I thought that Liberty and Heaven<br/>
To heavenly souls had been all one; but now<br/>
I see that most through sloth had rather serve,<br/>
Ministering Spirits, trained up in feast and song;<br/>
Such hast thou armed, the minstrelsy of heaven—<br/>
Servility with freedom to contend,<br/>
As both their deeds compared this day shall prove.'

"To whom, in brief, thus Abdiel stern replied:—<br/>
'Apostat! still thou err'st, no end wilt find<br/>
Of erring, from the path of truth remote.<br/>
Unjustly thou deprav'st it with the name<br/>
Of servitude, to serve whom God ordains,<br/>
Or Nature: God and Nature bid the same,<br/>
When he who rules is worthiest, and excels<br/>
Them whom he governs. This is servitude—<br/>
To serve the unwise, or him who hath rebelled<br/>
Against his worthier, as thine now serve thee,<br/>
Thyself not free, but to thyself enthralled;<br/>
Yet lewdly dar'st our ministering upbraid.<br/>
Reign thou in Hell, thy kingdom; let me serve<br/>
In Heaven god ever blest, and His Divine<br/>
Behests obey, worthiest to be obeyed.<br/>
Yet chains in Hell, not realms, expect: meanwhile,<br/>
From me returned, as erst thou saidst, from flight,<br/>
This greeting on thy impious crest receive.'

"So saying, a noble stroke he lifted high,<br/>
Which hung not, but so swift with tempest fell<br/>
On the proud crest of Satan that no sight,<br/>
Nor motion of swift thought, less could his shield,<br/>
Such ruin intercept. Ten paces huge<br/>
He back recoiled; the tenth on bended knee<br/>
His massy spear upstayed: as if, on earth,<br/>
Winds under ground, or waters forcing way,<br/>
Sidelong had pushed a mountain from his seat,<br/>
Half-sunk with all his pines. Amazement seized<br/>
The rebel Thrones, but greater rage, to see<br/>
Thus foiled their mightiest; ours joy filled, and shout,<br/>
Presage of victory, and fierce desire<br/>
Of battle: whereat Michael bid sound<br/>
The Archangel trumpet. Through the vast of Heaven<br/>
It sounded, and the faithful armies rung<br/>
Hosannah to the Highest; nor stood at gaze<br/>
The adverse legions, nor less hideous joined<br/>
The horrid shock. Now storming fury rose,<br/>
And clamour such as heard in Heaven till now.<br/>
Was never; arms on armour clashing brayed<br/>
Horrible discord, and the madding wheels<br/>
Of brazen chariots raged; dire was the noise<br/>
Of conflict; overhead the dismal hiss<br/>
Of fiery darts in flaming volleys flew,<br/>
And, flying, vaulted either host with fire.<br/>
So under fiery cope together rushed<br/>
Both battles main with ruinous assault<br/>
And inextinguishable rage. All Heaven<br/>
Resounded; and, had Earth been then, all Earth<br/>
Had to her centre shook. What wonder, when<br/>
Millions of fierce encountering Angels fought<br/>
On either side, the least of whom could yield<br/>
These elements, and arm him with the force<br/>
Of all their regions? How much more of power<br/>
Army against army numberless to raise<br/>
Dreadful combustion warring, and disturb,<br/>
Though not destroy, their happy native seat;<br/>
Had not the Eternal King Omnipotent<br/>
From his strong hold of Heaven high overruled<br/>
And limited their might, though numbered such<br/>
As each divided legion might have seemed<br/>
A numerous host, in strength, each armed hand<br/>
A legion! Led in fight, yet leader seemed<br/>
Each warrior single as in chief; expert<br/>
When to advance, or stand, or turn the sway<br/>
Of battle, open when, and when to close<br/>
The ridges of grim war. No thought of flight,<br/>
None of retreat, no unbecoming deed<br/>
That argued fear; each on himself relied<br/>
As only in his arm the moment lay<br/>
Of victory. Deeds of eternal fame<br/>
Were done, but infinite; for wide was spread<br/>
That war, and various: sometimes on firm ground<br/>
A standing fight; then, soaring on main wing,<br/>
Tormented all the air; all air seemed then<br/>
Conflicting fire. Long time in even scale<br/>
The battle hung; till Satan, who that day<br/>
Prodigious power had shown, and met in arms<br/>
No equal, ranging through the dire attack<br/>
Of fighting Seraphim confused, at length<br/>
Saw where the sword of Michael smote, and felled<br/>
Squadrons at once: with huge two-handed sway<br/>
Brandished aloft, the horrid edge came down<br/>
Wide-wasting. Such destruction to withstand<br/>
He hasted, and opposed the rocky orb<br/>
Of tenfold adamant, his ample shield,<br/>
A vast circumference. At his approach<br/>
The great Archangel from his warlike toil<br/>
Surceased, and, glad, as hoping here to end<br/>
Intestine war in Heaven, the Arch-foe subdued,<br/>
Or captive dragged in chains, with hostile frown<br/>
And visage all inflamed, first thus began:—

"'Author of Evil, unknown till thy revolt,<br/>
Unnamed in Heaven, now plenteous as thou seest<br/>
These acts of hateful strife-hateful to all,<br/>
Though heaviest, by just measure, on thyself<br/>
And thy adherents-how hast thou disturbed<br/>
Heaven's blessed peace, and into Nature brought<br/>
Misery, uncreated till the crime<br/>
Of thy rebellion! how hast thou instilled<br/>
Thy malice into thousands, once upright<br/>
And faithful, now proved false! But think not here<br/>
To trouble holy rest; Heaven casts thee out<br/>
From all her confines; Heaven, the seat of bliss,<br/>
Brooks not the works of violence and war.<br/>
Hence, then, and Evil go with thee along,<br/>
Thy offspring, to the place of Evil, Hell—<br/>
Thou and thy wicked crew! there mingle broils!<br/>
Ere this avenging sword begin thy doom,<br/>
Or some more sudden vengeance, winged from God,<br/>
Precipitate thee with augmented pain.'

"So spake the Prince of Angels; to whom thus<br/>
The Adversary:—'Nor think thou with wind<br/>
Of airy threats to awe whom yet with deeds<br/>
Thou canst not. Hast thou turned the least of these<br/>
To flight-or, if to fall, but that they rise<br/>
Unvanquished-easier to transact with me<br/>
That thou shouldst hope, imperious, and with threats<br/>
To chase me hence? Err not that so shall end<br/>
The strife which thou call'st evil, but we style<br/>
The strife of glory; which we mean to win,<br/>
Or turn this Heaven itself into the Hell<br/>
Thou fablest; here, however, to dwell free,<br/>
If not to reign. Meanwhile, thy utmost force—<br/>
And join Him named Almighty to thy aid—<br/>
I fly not, but have sought thee far and nigh.'

"They ended parle, and both addressed for fight<br/>
Unspeakable; for who, though with the tongue<br/>
Of Angels, can relate, or to what things<br/>
Liken on earth conspicuous, that may lift<br/>
Human imagination to such highth<br/>
Of godlike power? for likest gods they seemed,<br/>
Stood they or moved, in stature, motion, arms,<br/>
Fit to decide the empire of great Heaven.<br/>
Now waved their fiery swords, and in the air<br/>
Made horrid circles; two broad suns their shields<br/>
Blazed opposite, while Expectation stood<br/>
In horror; from each hand with speed retired,<br/>
Where erst was thickest fight, the Angelic throng,<br/>
And left large field, unsafe with the wind<br/>
Of such commotion: such as (to set forth<br/>
Great things by small) if, Nature's concord broke,<br/>
Among the constellations war were sprung,<br/>
Two planets, rushing from aspect' malign<br/>
Of fiercest opposition, in mid sky<br/>
Should combat, and their jarring spheres confound.<br/>
Together both, with next to Almighty arm<br/>
Uplifted imminent, one stroke they aimed<br/>
That might determine, and not need repeat<br/>
As not of power, at once; nor odds appeared<br/>
In might or swift prevention. But the sword<br/>
Of Michael from the armoury of God<br/>
Was given him tempered so that neither keen<br/>
Nor solid might resist that edge: it met<br/>
The sword of Satan, with steep force to smite<br/>
Descending, and in half cut sheer; nor stayed,<br/>
But, with swift wheel reverse, deep entering, shared<br/>
All his right side. Then Satan first knew pain,<br/>
And writhed him to and fro convolved; so sore<br/>
The griding sword with discontinuous wound<br/>
Passed through him. But the ethereal substance closed,<br/>
Not long divisible; and from the gash<br/>
A stream of nectarous humour issuing flowed<br/>
Sanguin, such as celestial Spirits may bleed,<br/>
And all his armour stained, erewhile so bright,<br/>
Forthwith, on all sides, to his aid was run<br/>
By Angels many and strong, who interposed<br/>
Defence, while others bore him on their shields<br/>
Back to his chariot where it stood retired<br/>
From off the files of war: there they him laid<br/>
Gnashing for anguish, and despite, and shame<br/>
To find himself not matchless, and his pride<br/>
Humbled by such rebuke, so far beneath<br/>
His confidence to equal God in power.<br/>
Yet soon he healed; for Spirits, that live throughout<br/>
Vital in every part-not, as frail Man,<br/>
In entrails, heart or head, liver or reins—<br/>
Cannot but by annihilating die;<br/>
Nor in their liquid texture mortal wound<br/>
Receive, no more than can the fluid air:<br/>
All heart they live, all head, all eye, all ear,<br/>
All intellect, all sense; and as they please<br/>
They limb themselves, and colour, shape, or size<br/>
Assume, as likes them best, condense or rare.

"Meanwhile, in other parts, like deeds deserved<br/>
Memorial, where the might of Gabriel fought,<br/>
And with fierce ensigns pierced the deep array<br/>
Of Moloch, furious king, who him defied,<br/>
And at his chariot-wheels to drag him bound<br/>
Threatened, nor from the Holy One of Heaven<br/>
Refreined his tongue blasphemous, but anon,<br/>
Down cloven to the waist, with shattered arms<br/>
And uncouth pain fled bellowing. On each wing<br/>
Uriel and Raphael his vaunting foe,<br/>
Though huge and in a rock of diamond armed,<br/>
Vanquished-Adramelech and Asmadai,<br/>
Two potent Thrones, that to be less than Gods<br/>
Disdained, but meaner thoughts learned in their flight,<br/>
Mangled with ghastly wounds through plate and mail.<br/>
Nor stood unmindful Abdiel to annoy<br/>
The atheist crew, but with redoubled blow<br/>
Ariel, and Arioch, and the violence<br/>
Of Ramiel, scorched and blasted, overthrew.<br/>
I might relate of thousands, and their names<br/>
Eternize here on Earth; but those elect<br/>
Angels, contented with their fame in Heaven,<br/>
Seek not the praise of men: the other sort,<br/>
In might though wondrous and in acts of war,<br/>
Nor or renown less eager, yet by doom<br/>
Cancelled from Heaven and sacred memory,<br/>
Nameless in dark oblivion let them dwell<br/>
For strength from truth divided, and from just,<br/>
Illaudable, nought merits but dispraise<br/>
And ignominy, yet to glory aspires,<br/>
Vain-glorious, and through infamy seeks fame:<br/>
Therefore eternal silence be their doom!

"And now, their mightiest quelled, the battle swerved,<br/>
With many an inroad gored; deformed rout<br/>
Entered, and foul disorder; all the ground<br/>
With shivered armour strown, and on a heap<br/>
Chariot and charioter lay overturned,<br/>
And fiery foaming steeds; what stood recoiled,<br/>
O'er-wearied, through the faint Satanic host,<br/>
Defensive scarce, or with pale fear surprised—<br/>
Then first with fear surprised and sense of pain—<br/>
Fled ignominious, to such evil brought<br/>
By sin of disobedience, till that hour<br/>
Not liable to fear, or flight, or pain.<br/>
Far otherwise the inviolable Saints<br/>
In cubic phalanx firm advanced entire,<br/>
Invulnerable, impenetrably armed;<br/>
Such high advantages their innocence<br/>
Gave them above their foes-not to have sinned,<br/>
Not to have disobeyed; in fight they stood<br/>
Unwearied, unobnoxious to be pained<br/>
By wound, though from their place by violence moved.

"Now Night her course began, and, over Heaven<br/>
Inducing darkness, grateful truce imposed,<br/>
And silence on the odious din of war.<br/>
Under her cloudy covert both retired,<br/>
Victor and Vanquished. On the foughten field<br/>
Michael and his Angels, prevalent<br/>
Encamping, placed in guard their watches round,<br/>
Cherubic waving fires: on the other part,<br/>
Satan with his rebellious disappeared,<br/>
Far in the dark dislodged, and, void of rest,<br/>
His Potentates to council called by night,<br/>
And in the midst thus undismayed began:—

"'O now in danger tried, now known in arms<br/>
Not to be overpowered, companions dear,<br/>
Found worthy not of liberty alone—<br/>
Too mean pretence-but, what we more affect,<br/>
Honour, dominion, glory and renown;<br/>
Who have sustained one day in doubtful fight<br/>
(And, if one day, why not eternal days?)<br/>
What Heaven's Lord had powerfullest to send<br/>
Against us from about his Throne, and judged<br/>
Sufficient to subdue us to his will,<br/>
But proves not so: then fallible, it seems,<br/>
Of future we may deem him, though till now<br/>
Omniscient thought! True is, less firmly armed,<br/>
Some disadvantage we endured, and pain—<br/>
Till now not known, but, known, as soon contemned;<br/>
Since now we find this our empyreal form<br/>
Incapable of mortal injury,<br/>
Imperishable, and, though pierced with wound,<br/>
Soon closing, and by native vigour healed.<br/>
Of evil, then, so small as easy think<br/>
The remedy: perhaps more valid arms,<br/>
Weapons more violent, when next we meet,<br/>
May serve to better us and worse our foes,<br/>
Or equal what between us made the odds,<br/>
In nature none. If other hidden cause<br/>
Left them superior, while we can preserve<br/>
Unhurt our minds, and understanding sound,<br/>
Due search and consultation will disclose,'

"He sat; and in the assembly next upstood<br/>
Nisroch, of Principalities the prime.<br/>
As one he stood escaped from cruel fight<br/>
Sore toiled, his riven arms to havoc hewn,<br/>
And, cloudy in aspect, thus answering spake:—

"'Deliverer from new Lords, leader to free<br/>
Enjoyment of our right as Gods! yet hard<br/>
For Gods, and too unequal work, we find<br/>
Against unequal arms to fight in pain,<br/>
Against unpained, impassive; from which evil<br/>
Ruin must needs ensue. For what avails<br/>
Valour or strength, though matchless, quelled with pain,<br/>
Which all subdues, and makes remiss the hands<br/>
Of mightiest? Sense of pleasure we may well<br/>
Spare out of life perhaps, and not repine,<br/>
But live content-which is the calmest life;<br/>
But pain is perfect misery, the worst<br/>
Of evils, and, excessive, overturns<br/>
All patience. He who, therefore, can invent<br/>
With what more forcible we may offend<br/>
Our yet unwounded enemies, or arm<br/>
Ourselves with like defence, to me deserves<br/>
No less than for deliverance what we owe.'

"Whereto, with look composed, Satan replied:—<br/>
