'Not uninvented that, which thou aright<br/>
Believ'st so main to our success, I bring.<br/>
Which of us who beholds the bright surface<br/>
Of this ethereous mould whereon we stand—<br/>
This continent of spacious Heaven, adorned<br/>
With plant, fruit, flower ambrosial, gems and gold—<br/>
Whose eye so superficially surveys<br/>
These things as not to mind from whence they grow<br/>
Deep under ground: materials dark and crude,<br/>
Of spirituous and fiery spume, till, touched<br/>
With Heaven's ray, and tempered, they shoot forth<br/>
So beauteous, opening to the ambient light?<br/>
These in their dark nativity the Deep<br/>
Shall yield us, pregnant with infernal flame;<br/>
Which, into hollow engines long and round<br/>
Thick-rammed, at the other bore with touch of fire<br/>
Dilated and infuriate, shall send forth<br/>
From far, with thundering noise, among our foes<br/>
Such implements of mischief as shall dash<br/>
To pieces and o'erwhelm whatever stands<br/>
Adverse, that they shall fear we have disarmed<br/>
The Thunderer of his only dreaded bolt.<br/>
Nor long shall be our labour; yet ere dawn<br/>
Effect shall end our wish. Meanwhile revive;<br/>
Abandon fear; to strength and counsel joined<br/>
Think nothing hard, much less to be despaired.'

"He ended; and his words their drooping cheer<br/>
Enlightened, and their languished hope revived.<br/>
The invention all admired, and each how he<br/>
To be the inventor missed; so easy it seemed,<br/>
Once found, which yet unfound most would have thought<br/>
Impossible! Yet, haply, of thy race,<br/>
In future days, if malice should abound,<br/>
Some one, intent on mischief, or inspired<br/>
With devilish machination, might devise<br/>
Like instrument to plague the sons of men<br/>
For sin, on war and mutual slaughter bent.<br/>
Forthwith from council to the work they flew;<br/>
None arguing stood; innumerable hands<br/>
Were ready; in a moment up they turned<br/>
Wide the celestial soil, and saw beneath<br/>
The originals of Nature in their crude<br/>
Conception; sulphurous and nitrous foam<br/>
They found, they mingled, and, with subtle art<br/>
Concocted and adusted, they reduced<br/>
To blackest grain, and into store conveyed.<br/>
Part hidden veins digged up (nor hath this Earth<br/>
Entrails unlike) of mineral and stone,<br/>
Whereof to found their engines and their balls<br/>
Of missive ruin; part incentive reed<br/>
Provide, pernicious with one touch to fire.<br/>
So all ere day-spring, under conscious Night,<br/>
Secret they finished, and in order set,<br/>
With silent circumspection, unespied.

"Now, when fair Morn orient in Heaven appeared,<br/>
Up rose the victor Angels, and to arms<br/>
The matin trumpet sung. In arms they stood<br/>
Of golden panoply, refulgent host,<br/>
Soon banded; others from the dawning hills<br/>
Looked round, and scouts each coast light-armed scour,<br/>
Each quarter, to descry the distant foe,<br/>
Where lodged, or whither fled, or if for fight,<br/>
In motion or in halt. Him soon they met<br/>
Under spread ensigns moving nigh, in slow<br/>
But firm battalion: back with speediest sail<br/>
Zophiel, of Cherubim the swiftest wing,<br/>
Came flying, and in mid air aloud thus cried:—

"'Arm, Warriors, arm for fight! The foe at hand,<br/>
Whom fled we thought, will save us long pursuit<br/>
This day; fear not his flight; so thick a cloud<br/>
He comes, and settled in his face I see<br/>
Sad resolution and secure. Let each<br/>
His adamantine coat gird well, and each<br/>
Fit well his helm, gripe fast his orbed shield,<br/>
Borne even or high; for this day will pour down,<br/>
If I conjecture aught, no drizzling shower,<br/>
But rattling storm of arrows barbed with fire.'

"So warned he them, aware themselves, and soon<br/>
In order, quit of all impediment.<br/>
Instant, without disturb, they took alarm,<br/>
And onward more embattled: when, behold,<br/>
Not distant far, with heavy pace the Foe<br/>
Approaching gross and huge, in hollow cube<br/>
Training his devilish enginery, impaled<br/>
On every side with shadowing squadrons deep,<br/>
To hide the fraud. At interview both stood<br/>
A while; but suddenly at head appeared<br/>
Satan, and thus was heard commanding loud:—

"'Vanguard, to right and left the front unfold,<br/>
That all may see who hate us how we seek<br/>
Peace and composure, and with open breast<br/>
Stand ready to receive them, if they like<br/>
Our overture, and turn not back perverse:<br/>
But that I doubt. However, witness Heaven!<br/>
Heaven, witness thou anon! while we discharge<br/>
Freely our part. Ye, who appointed stand,<br/>
Do as you have in charge, and briefly touch<br/>
What we propound, and loud that all may hear.'

"So scoffing in ambiguous words, he scarce<br/>
Had ended, when to right and left the front<br/>
Divided, and to either flank retired;<br/>
Which to our eyes discovered, new and strange,<br/>
A triple mounted row of pillars laid<br/>
On wheels (for like to pillars most they seemed,<br/>
Or hollowed bodies made of oak or fir,<br/>
With branches lopt, in wood or mountain felled),<br/>
Brass, iron, stony mould, had not their mouths<br/>
With hideous orifice gaped on us wide,<br/>
Portending hollow truce. At each, behind,<br/>
A Seraph stood, and in his hand a reed<br/>
Stood waving tipt with fire; while we, suspense,<br/>
Collected stood within our thoughts amused.<br/>
Not long! for sudden all at once their reeds<br/>
Put forth, and to a narrow vent applied<br/>
With nicest touch. Immediate in a flame,<br/>
But soon obscured with smoke, all Heaven appeared,<br/>
From those deep-throated engines belched, whose roar<br/>
Embowelled with outrageous noise the air,<br/>
And all her entrails tore, disgorging foul<br/>
Their devilish glut, chained thunderbolts and hail<br/>
Of iron globes; which, on the Victor Host<br/>
Levelled, with such impetuous fury smote,<br/>
That whom they hit none on their feet might stand,<br/>
Though standing else as rocks, but down they fell<br/>
By thousands, Angel on Archangel rowled,<br/>
The sooner for their arms. Unarmed, they might<br/>
Have easily, as Spirits, evaded swift<br/>
By quick contraction or remove; but now<br/>
Foul dissipation followed, and forced rout;<br/>
Nor served it to relax their serried files.<br/>
What should they do? If on they rushed, repulse<br/>
Repeated, and indecent overthrow<br/>
Doubled, would render them yet more despised,<br/>
And to their foes a laughter-for in view<br/>
Stood ranked of Seraphim another row,<br/>
In posture to displode their second tire<br/>
Of thunder; back defeated to return<br/>
They worse abhorred. Satan beheld their plight,<br/>
And to his mates thus in derision called:—

"'O friends, why come not on these victors proud?<br/>
Erewhile they fierce were coming; and, when we,<br/>
To entertain them fair with open front<br/>
And breast (what could we more?), propounded terms<br/>
Of composition, straight they changed their minds,<br/>
Flew off, and into strange vagaries fell,<br/>
As they would dance. Yet for a dance they seemed<br/>
Somewhat extravagant and wild; perhaps<br/>
For joy of offered peace. But I suppose,<br/>
If our proposals once again were heard,<br/>
We should compel them to a quick result.'

"To whom thus Belial, in like gamesome mood:<br/>
'Leader, the terms we sent were terms of weight,<br/>
Of hard contents, and full of force urged home,<br/>
Such as we might perceive amused them all,<br/>
And stumbled many. Who receives them right<br/>
Had need from head to foot well understand;<br/>
Not understood, this gift they have besides—<br/>
They shew us when our foes walk not upright.'

"So they among themselves in pleasant vein<br/>
Stood scoffing, heightened in their thoughts beyond<br/>
All doubt of victory; Eternal Might<br/>
To match with their inventions they presumed<br/>
So easy, and of his thunder made a scorn,<br/>
And all his host derided, while they stood<br/>
A while in trouble. But they stood not long;<br/>
Rage prompted them at length, and found them arms<br/>
Against such hellish mischief fit to oppose.<br/>
Forthwith (behold the excellence, the power,<br/>
Which God hath in his mighty Angels placed!)<br/>
Their arms away they threw, and to the hills<br/>
(For Earth hath this variety from Heaven<br/>
Of pleasure situate in hill and dale)<br/>
Light as the lightning-glimpse they ran, they flew,<br/>
From their foundations, loosening to and fro,<br/>
They plucked the seated hills, with all their load,<br/>
Rocks, waters, woods, and, by the shaggy tops<br/>
Uplifting, bore them in their hands. Amaze,<br/>
Be sure, and terror, seized the rebel Host,<br/>
When coming towards them so dread they saw<br/>
The bottom of the mountains upward turned,<br/>
Till on those cursed engines' triple row<br/>
They sawethem whelmed, and all their confidence<br/>
Under the weight of mountains buried deep;<br/>
Themselves invaded next, and on their heads<br/>
Main promontories flung, which in the air<br/>
Came shadowing, and oppressed whole legions armed.<br/>
Their armour helped their harm, crushed in and bruised,<br/>
Into their substance pent-which wrought them pain<br/>
Implacable, and many a dolorous groan,<br/>
Long struggling underneath, ere they could wind<br/>
Out of such prison, though Spirits of purest light,<br/>
Purest at first, now gross by sinning grown.<br/>
The rest, in imitation, to like arms<br/>
Betook them, and the neighbouring hills uptore;<br/>
So hills amid the air encountered hills,<br/>
Hurled to and fro with jaculation dire,<br/>
That underground they fought in dismal shade:<br/>
Infernal noise! war seemed a civil game<br/>
To this uproar; horrid confusion heaped<br/>
Upon confusion rose. And now all Heaven<br/>
Had gone to wrack, with ruin overspread,<br/>
Had not the Almighty Father, where he sits<br/>
Shrined in his sanctuary of Heaven secure,<br/>
Consulting on the sum of things, foreseen<br/>
This tumult, and permitted all, advised,<br/>
That his great purpose he might so fulfil,<br/>
To honour his Anointed Son, avenged<br/>
Upon his enemies, and to declare<br/>
All power on him transferred. Whence to his Son,<br/>
The assessor of his Throne, he thus began:—

"'Effulgence of my glory, Son beloved,<br/>
Son in whose face invisible is beheld<br/>
Visibly, what by Deity I am,<br/>
And in whose hand what by decree I do,<br/>
Second Omnipotence! two days are passed,<br/>
Two days, as we compute the days of Heaven,<br/>
Since Michael and his Powers went forth to tame<br/>
These disobedient. Sore hath been their fight,<br/>
As likeliest was when two such foes met armed:<br/>
For to themselves I left them; and thou know'st<br/>
Equal in their creation they were formed,<br/>
Save what sin hath impaired-which yet hath wrought<br/>
Insensibly, for I suspend their doom:<br/>
Whence in perpetual fight they needs must last<br/>
Endless, and no solution will be found.<br/>
War wearied hath performed what war can do,<br/>
And to disordered rage let loose the reins,<br/>
With mountains, as with weapons, armed; which makes<br/>
Wild work in Heaven, and dangerous to the main.<br/>
Two days are, therefore, passed; the third is thine:<br/>
For thee I have ordained it, and thus far<br/>
Have suffered, that the glory may be thine<br/>
Of ending this great war, since none but thou<br/>
Can end it. Into thee such virtue and grace<br/>
Immense I have transfused, that all may know<br/>
In Heaven and Hell thy power above compare,<br/>
And this perverse commotion governed thus,<br/>
To manifest thee worthiest to be Heir<br/>
Of all things-to be Heir, and to be King<br/>
By sacred unction, thy deserved right.<br/>
Go, then, thou Mightiest, in thy Father's might;<br/>
Ascend my chariot; guide the rapid wheels<br/>
That shake Heaven's basis; bring forth all my war;<br/>
My bow and thunder, my Almighty arms,<br/>
Gird on, and sword upon thy puissant thigh;<br/>
Pursue these Sons of Darkness, drive them out<br/>
From all Heaven's bounds into the utter Deep;<br/>
There let them learn, as likes them, to despise<br/>
God, and Messiah his anointed King.'

"He said, and on his Son with rays direct<br/>
Shon full. He all his Father full expressed<br/>
Ineffably into his face received;<br/>
And thus the Filial Godhead answering spake:—

"'O Father, O Supreme of Heavenly Thrones,<br/>
First, Highest, Holiest, Best, thou always seek'st<br/>
To glorify thy Son; I always thee,<br/>
As is most just. This I my glory account,<br/>
My exaltation, and my whole delight,<br/>
That thou in me, well pleased, declar'st thy will<br/>
Fulfilled, which to fulfil is all my bliss.<br/>
Sceptre and power, thy giving, I assume,<br/>
And gladlier shall resign when in the end<br/>
Thou shalt be all in all, and I in thee<br/>
For ever, and in me all whom thou lov'st.<br/>
But whom thou hat'st I hate, and can put on<br/>
Thy terrors, as I put thy mildness on,<br/>
Image of thee in all things: and shall soon,<br/>
Armed with thy might, rid Heaven of these rebelled,<br/>
To their prepared ill mansion driven down,<br/>
To chains of darkness and the undying Worm,<br/>
That from thy just obedience could revolt,<br/>
Whom to obey is happiness entire.<br/>
Then shall thy Saints, unmixed, and from the impure<br/>
Far separate, circling thy holy Mount,<br/>
Unfeigned halleluiahs to thee sing,<br/>
Hymns of high praise, and I among them chief,'

"So said, He, o'er his sceptre bowing, rose<br/>
From the right hand of Glory where He sat;<br/>
And the third sacred morn began to shine,<br/>
Dawning through Heaven. Forth rushed with whirlwind sound<br/>
The chariot of Paternal Deity,<br/>
Flashing thick flames, wheel within wheel; undrawn,<br/>
Itself instinct with spirit, but convoyed<br/>
By four cherubic Shapes. Four faces each<br/>
Had wondrous; as with stars, their bodies all<br/>
And wings were set with eyes; with eyes the wheels<br/>
Of beryl, and careering fires between;<br/>
Over their heads a crystal firmament,<br/>
Whereon a sapphire throne, inlaid with pure<br/>
Amber and colours of the showery arch.<br/>
He, in celestial panoply all armed<br/>
Of radiant Urim, work divinely wrought,<br/>
Ascended; at his right hand Victory<br/>
Sat eagle-winged; beside him hung his bow,<br/>
And quiver, with three-bolted thunder stored;<br/>
And from about him fierce effusion rowled<br/>
Of smoke and bickering flame and sparkles dire.<br/>
Attended with ten thousand Saints,<br/>
He onward came; far off his coming shon;<br/>
And twenty thousand (I their number heard)<br/>
Chariots of God, half on each hand, were seen.<br/>
He on the wings of Cherub rode sublime<br/>
On the crystallin sky, in saphir throned—<br/>
Illustrious far and wide, but by his own<br/>
First seen. Them unexpected joy surprised<br/>
When the great ensign of Messiah blazed<br/>
Aloft, by Angels borne, his Sign in Heaven;<br/>
Under whose conduct Michael soon reduced<br/>
His army, circumfused on either wing,<br/>
Under their Head embodied all in one.<br/>
Before him Power Divine his way prepared;<br/>
At his command the uprooted hills retired<br/>
Each to his place; they heard his voice, and went<br/>
Obsequious; Heaven his wonted face renewed,<br/>
And with fresh flowerets hill and valley smiled.

"This saw his hapless foes, but stood obdured,<br/>
And to rebellious fight rallied their Powers,<br/>
Insensate, hope conceiving from despair.<br/>
In Heavenly Spirits could such perverseness dwell?<br/>
But to convince the proud what signs avail,<br/>
Or wonders move the obdurate to relent?<br/>
They, hardened more by what might most reclaim,<br/>
Grieving to see his glory, at the sight<br/>
Took envy, and, aspiring to his highth,<br/>
Stood re-imbattled fierce, by force or fraud<br/>
Weening to prosper, and at length prevail<br/>
Against God and Messiah, or to fall<br/>
In universal ruin last; and now<br/>
To final battle drew, disdaining flight,<br/>
Or faint retreat: when the great Son of God<br/>
To all his host on either hand thus spake:—

"'Stand still in bright array, ye Saints; here stand,<br/>
Ye Angels armed; this day from battle rest.<br/>
Faithful hath been your warfare, and of God<br/>
Accepted, fearless in his righteous cause;<br/>
And, as ye have received, so have ye done,<br/>
Invincibly. But of this cursed crew<br/>
The punishment to other hand belongs;<br/>
Vengeance is his, or whose He sole appoints.<br/>
Number to this day's work is not ordained,<br/>
Nor multitude; stand only and behold<br/>
God's indignation on these godless poured<br/>
By me. Not you, but me, they have despised,<br/>
Yet envied; against me is all their rage,<br/>
Because the Father, to whom in Heaven supreme<br/>
Kingdom and power and glory appertains,<br/>
Hath honoured me, according to his will.<br/>
Therefore to me their doom he hath assigned,<br/>
That they may have their wish, to try with me<br/>
In battle which the stronger proves-they all,<br/>
Or I alone against them; since by strength<br/>
They measure all, of other excellence<br/>
Not emulous, nor care who them excels;<br/>
Nor other strife with them do I voutsafe.'

"So spake the Son, and into terror changed<br/>
His countenance, too severe to be beheld,<br/>
And full of wrauth bent on his enemies.<br/>
At once the Four spread out their starry wings<br/>
With dreadful shade continguous, and the orbs<br/>
Of his fierce chariot rowled, as with the sound<br/>
Of torrent floods, or of a numerous host.<br/>
He on his impious foes right onward drove,<br/>
Gloomy as Night. Under his burning wheels<br/>
The steadfast Empyrean shook throughout,<br/>
All but the Throne itself of God. Full soon<br/>
Among them he arrived, in his right hand<br/>
Grasping ten thousand thunders, which he sent<br/>
Before him, such as in their souls infixed<br/>
Plagues. They, astonished, all resistance lost,<br/>
All courage; down their idle weapons dropt;<br/>
O'er shields, and helms, and helmed heads he rode<br/>
Of Thrones and mighty Seraphim prostate,<br/>
That wished the mountains now might be again<br/>
Thrown on them, as a shelter from his ire.<br/>
Nor less on either side tempestuous fell<br/>
His arrows, from the fourfold-visaged Four,<br/>
Distinct with eyes, and from the living wheels,<br/>
Distinct alike with multitude of eyes;<br/>
One spirit in them ruled, and every eye<br/>
Glared lightning, and shot forth pernicious fire<br/>
Among the accursed, that withered all their strength,<br/>
And of their wonted vigour left them drained,<br/>
Exhausted, spiritless, afflicted, fallen,<br/>
Yet half his strength he put not forth, but checked<br/>
His thunder in mid-volley; for he meant<br/>
Not to destroy, but root them out of Heaven.<br/>
The overthrown he raised, and, as a herd<br/>
Of goats or timorous flock together thronged,<br/>
Drove them before him thunderstruck, pursued<br/>
With terrors and with furies to the bounds<br/>
And crystal wall of Heaven; which, opening wide,<br/>
Rowled inward, and a spacious gap disclosed<br/>
Into the wasteful Deep. The monstrous sight<br/>
Strook them with horror backward; but far worse<br/>
Urged them behind: headlong themselves they threw<br/>
Down from the verge of Heaven: eternal wrauth<br/>
Burnt after them to the bottomless pit.

"Hell heard the unsufferable noise; Hell saw<br/>
Heaven ruining from Heaven, and would have fled<br/>
Affrighted; but strict Fate had cast too deep<br/>
Her dark foundations, and too fast had bound.<br/>
Nine days they fell; confounded Chaos roared,<br/>
And felt tenfold confusion in their fall<br/>
Through his wild Anarchy; so huge a rout<br/>
Incumbered him with ruin. Hell at last,<br/>
Yawning, received them whole, and on them closed—<br/>
Hell, their fit habitation, fraught with fire<br/>
Unquenchable, the house of woe and pain.<br/>
Disburdened Heaven rejoiced, and soon repaired<br/>
Her mural breach, returning whence it rowled.<br/>
Sole victor, from the expulsion of his foes<br/>
Messiah his triumphal chariot turned.<br/>
To meet him all his Saints, who silent stood<br/>
Eye-witnesses of His Almighty acts,<br/>
With jubilee advanced; and, as they went,<br/>
Shaded with branching palm, each order bright<br/>
Sung triumph, and him sung victorious King,<br/>
Son, Heir, and Lord, to him dominion given,<br/>
Worthiest to reign. He celebrated rode<br/>
Triumphant through mid Heaven, into the courts<br/>
And temple of his mighty Father throned<br/>
On high; who into glory him received,<br/>
Where now he sits at the right hand of bliss.

"Thus measuring things in Heaven by things on Earth,<br/>
At thy request, and that thou may'st beware<br/>
By what is past, to thee I have revealed<br/>
What might have else to human race been hid—<br/>
The discord which befell, and war in Heaven<br/>
Among the Angelic Powers, and the deep fall<br/>
Of those too high aspiring who rebelled<br/>
With Satan: he who envies now thy state,<br/>
Who now is plotting how he may seduce<br/>
Thee also from obedience, that, with him<br/>
Bereaved of happiness, thou may'st partake<br/>
His punishment, eternal misery;<br/>
Which would be all his solace and revenge,<br/>
As a despite done against the Most High,<br/>
Thee once to gain companion of his woe.<br/>
But listen not to his temptations; warn<br/>
Thy weaker; let it profit thee to have heard,<br/>
By terrible example, the reward<br/>
Of disobedience. Firm they might have stood,<br/>
Yet fell. Remember, and fear to transgress."

<h2>Book 7</h2>

Descend from Heaven, Urania, by that name<br/>
If rightly thou art called, whose voice divine<br/>
Following, above the Olympian hill I soar,<br/>
Above the flight of Pegasean wing!<br/>
The meaning, not the name, I call; for thou<br/>
Nor of the Muses nine, nor on the top<br/>
Of old Olympus dwell'st; but, heavenly-born,<br/>
Before the hills appeared or fountain flowed,<br/>
Thou with Eternal Wisdom didst converse,<br/>
Wisdom thy sister, and with her didst play<br/>
In presence of the Almighty Father, pleased<br/>
With thy celestial song. Up led by thee,<br/>
Into the Heaven of Heavens I have presumed,<br/>
An earthly guest, and drawn empyreal air,<br/>
Thy tempering. With like safety guided down,<br/>
Return me to my native element;<br/>
Lest, from this flying steed unreined (as once<br/>
Bellerophon, though from a lower clime)<br/>
Dismounted, on the Aleian field I fall,<br/>
Erroneous there to wander and forlorn.<br/>
Half yet remains unsung, but narrower bound<br/>
Within the visible Diurnal Sphere.<br/>
Standing on Earth, not rapt above the pole,<br/>
More safe I sing with mortal voice, unchanged<br/>
To hoarse or mute, though fallen on evil days,<br/>
On evil days though fallen, and evil tongues,<br/>
In darkness, and with dangers compassed round,<br/>
And solitude; yet not alone, while thou<br/>
Visit'st my slumbers nightly, or when Morn<br/>
Purples the East. Still govern thou my song,<br/>
Urania, and fit audience find, though few.<br/>
But drive far off the barbarous dissonance<br/>
Of Bacchus and his revellers, the race<br/>
Of that wild rout that tore the Thracian Bard<br/>
In Rhodope, where woods and rocks had ears<br/>
To rapture, till the savage clamour drowned<br/>
Both harp and voice; nor could the Muse defend<br/>
Her son. So fail not thou who thee implores;<br/>
For thou art heavenly, she an empty dream.

Say, Goddess, what ensued when Raphael,<br/>
The affable Archangel, had forewarned<br/>
Adam, by dire example, to beware<br/>
Apostasy, by what befell in Heaven<br/>
To those apostates, lest the like befall<br/>
In Paradise to Adam or his race,<br/>
Charged not to touch the interdicted Tree,<br/>
If they transgress, and slight that sole command,<br/>
So easily obeyed amid the choice<br/>
Of all tastes else to please their appetite,<br/>
Though wandering. He, with his consorted Eve,<br/>
The story heard attentive, and was filled<br/>
With admiration and deep muse, to hear<br/>
Of things so high and strange-things to their thought<br/>
So unimaginable as hate in Heaven,<br/>
And was so near the peace of God in bliss,<br/>
With such confusion; but the evil, soon<br/>
Driven back, redounded as a flood on those<br/>
From whom it sprung, impossible to mix<br/>
With blessedness. Whence Adam soon repealed<br/>
The doubts that in his heart arose; and, now<br/>
Led on, yet sinless, with desire to know<br/>
What nearer might concern him-how this World<br/>
Of heaven and earth conspicuous first began;<br/>
When, and whereof, created; for what cause;<br/>
What within Eden, or without, was done<br/>
Before his memory-as one whose drouth,<br/>
Yet scarce allayed, still eyes the current stream,<br/>
Whose liquid murmur heard new thirst excites,<br/>
Proceeded thus to ask his Heavenly Guest:—

"Great things, and full of wonder in our ears,<br/>
Far differing from this World, thou hast revealed,<br/>
Divine Interpreter! by favour sent<br/>
Down from the Empyrean to forewarn<br/>
Us timely of what might else have been our loss,<br/>
Unknown, which human knowledge could not reach;<br/>
For which to the infinitely Good we owe<br/>
Immortal thanks, and his admonishment<br/>
Receive with solemn purpose to observe<br/>
Immutably his sovran will, the end<br/>
Of what we are. But, since thou hast voutsafed<br/>
Gently, for our instruction, to impart<br/>
Things above Earthly thought, which yet concerned<br/>
Our knowing, as to highest Wisdom seemed,<br/>
Deign to descend now lower, and relate<br/>
What may no less perhaps avail us known—<br/>
How first began this Heaven which we behold<br/>
Distant so high, with moving fires adorned<br/>
Innumerable; and this which yields or fills<br/>
All space, the ambient Air, wide interfused,<br/>
Imbracing round this florid Earth; what cause<br/>
Moved the Creator, in his holy rest<br/>
Through all eternity, soelate to build<br/>
In Chaos; and, the work begun, how soon<br/>
Absolved: if unforbid thou may'st unfold<br/>
What we not to explore the secrets ask<br/>
Of his eternal empire, but the more<br/>
To magnify his works the more we know.<br/>
And the great Light of Day yet wants to run<br/>
Much of his race, though steep. Suspense in heaven<br/>
Held by thy voice, thy potent voice he hears<br/>
And longer will delay, to hear thee tell<br/>
His generation, and the rising birth<br/>
Of Nature from the unapparent Deep:<br/>
Or, if the Star of Evening and the Moon<br/>
Haste to thy audience, Night with her will bring<br/>
Silence, and Sleep listening to thee will watch;<br/>
Or we can bid his absence till thy song<br/>
End, and dismiss thee ere the morning shine."

Thus Adam his illustrious guest besought;<br/>
And thus the godlike Angel answered mild:—

"This also thy request, with caution asked,<br/>
Obtain; though to recount Almighty works<br/>
What words or tongue of Seraph can suffice,<br/>
Or heart of man suffice to comprehend?<br/>
Yet what thou canst attain, which best may serve<br/>
To glorify the Maker, and infer<br/>
Thee also happier, shall not be withheld<br/>
Thy hearing. Such commission from above<br/>
I have received, to answer thy desire<br/>
Of knowledge within bounds; beyond abstain<br/>
To ask, nor let thine own inventions hope<br/>
Things not revealed, which the invisible King,<br/>
Only Omniscient, hath suppressed in night,<br/>
To none communicable in Earth or Heaven,<br/>
Enough is left besides to search and know;<br/>
But Knowledge is as food, and needs no less<br/>
Her temperance over appetite, to know<br/>
In measure what the mind may well contain;<br/>
Oppresses else with surfeit, and soon turns<br/>
Wisdom to folly, as nourishment to wind.

"Know then that, after Lucifer from Heaven<br/>
(So call him, brighter once amidst the host<br/>
Of Angels then that star the stars among)<br/>
Fell with his flaming Legions through the Deep<br/>
Into his place, and the great Son returned<br/>
Victorious with his Saints, the Omnipotent<br/>
Eternal Father from his Throne beheld<br/>
Their multitude, and to his Son thus spake:—

"'At least our envious foe hath failed, who thought<br/>
All like himself rebellious; by whose aid<br/>
This inaccessible high strength, the seat<br/>
Of Deity supreme, us dispossessed,<br/>
He trusted to have seized, and into fraud<br/>
Drew many whom their place knows here no more.<br/>
Yet far the greater part have kept, I see,<br/>
Their station; Heaven, yet populous, retains<br/>
Number sufficient to possess her realms,<br/>
Though wide, and this high temple to frequent<br/>
With ministeries due and solemn rites.<br/>
But, lest his heart exalt him in the harm<br/>
Already done, to have dispeopled Heaven—<br/>
My damage fondly deemed-I can repair<br/>
That detriment, if such it be to lose<br/>
Self-lost, and in a moment will create<br/>
Another world; out of one man a race<br/>
Of men innumerable, there to dwell,<br/>
Not here, till, by degrees of merit raised,<br/>
They open to themselves at length the way<br/>
Up hither, under long obedience tried,<br/>
And Earth be changed to Heaven, and Heaven to Earth,<br/>
One kingdom, joy and union without end.<br/>
Meanwhile inhabit lax, ye Powers of Heaven;<br/>
And thou, my Word, begotten Son, by thee<br/>
This I perform; speak thou, and be it done!<br/>
My overshadowing Spirit and might with thee<br/>
I send along; ride forth, and bid the Deep<br/>
Within appointed bounds be heaven and earth.<br/>
Boundless the Deep, because I am who fill<br/>
Infinitude; nor vacuous the space,<br/>
Though I, uncircumscribed, myself retire,<br/>
And put not forth my goodness, which is free<br/>
To act or not. Necessity and Chance<br/>
Approach not me, and what I will is Fate.'

"So spake the Almighty; and to what he spake<br/>
His Word, the Filial Godhead, gave effect.<br/>
Immediate are the acts of God, more swift<br/>
Than time or motion, but to human ears<br/>
Cannot without process' of speech be told,<br/>
So told as earthly notion can receive.<br/>
Great triumph and rejoicing was in Heaven<br/>
When such was heard declared the Almighty's will.<br/>
Glory they sung to the Most High, goodwill<br/>
To future men, and in their dwellings peace—<br/>
Glory to Him whose just avenging ire<br/>
Had driven out the ungodly from his sight<br/>
And the habitations of the just; to Him<br/>
Glory and praise whose wisdom had ordained<br/>
Good out of evil to create-instead<br/>
Of Spirits malign, a better Race to bring<br/>
Into their vacant room, and thence diffuse<br/>
His good to worlds and ages infinite.

"So sang the Hierarchies. Meanwhile the Son<br/>
On his great expedition now appeared,<br/>
Girt with omnipotence, with radiance crowned<br/>
Of majesty divine, sapience and love<br/>
Immense; and all his Father in him shon.<br/>
About his chariot numberless were poured<br/>
Cherub and Seraph, Potentates and Thrones,<br/>
And Virtues, winged Spirits, and chariots winged<br/>
From the armoury of God, where stand of old<br/>
Myriads, between two brazen mountains lodged<br/>
Against a solemn day, harnessed at hand,<br/>
Celestial equipage; and now came forth<br/>
Spontaneous, for within them Spirit lived,<br/>
Attendant on their Lord. Heaven opened wide<br/>
Her ever-during gates, harmonious sound<br/>
On golden hinges moving, to let forth<br/>
The King of Glory, in his powerful Word<br/>
And Spirit coming to create new worlds.<br/>
On Heavenly ground they stood, and from the shore<br/>
They viewed the vast immeasurable Abyss,<br/>
Outrageous as a sea, dark, wasteful, wild,<br/>
Up from the bottom turned by furious winds<br/>
And surging waves, as mountains to assault<br/>
Heaven's highth, and with the centre mix the pole.

"'Silence, ye troubled waves, and, thou Deep, peace!'<br/>
Said then the omnific Word: 'your discord end!'<br/>
Nor stayed; but, on the wings of Cherubim<br/>
Uplifted, in paternal glory rode<br/>
Far into Chaos and the World unborn;<br/>
For Chaos heard his voice. Him all his train<br/>
Followed in bright procession, to behold<br/>
Creation, and the wonders of his might.<br/>
Then stayed the fervid wheels, and in his hand<br/>
He took the golden compasses, prepared<br/>
In God's eternal store, to circumscribe<br/>
This Universe, and all created things.<br/>
One foot he centred, and the other turned<br/>
Round through the vast profundity obscure,<br/>
And said, 'Thus far extend, thus far thy bounds;<br/>
This be thy just circumference, O World!<br/>
Thus God the Heaven created, thus the Earth,<br/>
Matter unformed and void. Darkness profound<br/>
Covered the Abyss; but on the watery calm<br/>
His brooding wings the Spirit of God outspread,<br/>
And vital virtue infused, and vital warmth,<br/>
Throughout the fluid mass, but downward purged<br/>
The black, tartareous, cold, infernal dregs,<br/>
Adverse to life; then founded, then conglobed,<br/>
Like things to like, the rest to several place<br/>
Disparted, and between spun out the Air,<br/>
And Earth, self-balanced, on her centre hung.

"'Let there be Light!" said God; and forthwith Light<br/>
Ethereal, first of things, quintessence pure,<br/>
Sprung from the Deep, and from her native East<br/>
To journey through the aery gloom began,<br/>
Sphered in a radiant cloud-for yet the Sun<br/>
Was not; she in a cloudy tabernacle<br/>
Sojourned the while. God saw the Light was good;<br/>
And light from darkness by the hemisphere<br/>
Divided: Light the Day, and Darkness Night,<br/>
He named. Thus was the first Day even and morn;<br/>
Nor passed uncelebrated, nor unsung<br/>
By the celestial quires, when orient light<br/>
Exhaling first from darkness they beheld,<br/>
Birth-day of Heaven and Earth. With joy and shout<br/>
The hollow universal orb they filled,<br/>
And touched their golden harps, and hymning praised<br/>
God and his works; Creator him they sung,<br/>
Both when first evening was, and when first morn.

"Again God said, 'Let there be firmament<br/>
Amid the waters, and let it divide<br/>
The waters from the waters!' And God made<br/>
The firmament, expanse of liquid, pure,<br/>
Transparent, elemental air, diffused<br/>
In circuit to the uttermost convex<br/>
Of this great round-partition firm and sure,<br/>
The waters underneath from those above<br/>
Dividing; for as Earth, so he the World<br/>
Built on cicumfluous waters calm, in wide<br/>
Crystallin ocean, and the loud misrule<br/>
Of Chaos far removed, lest fierce extremes<br/>
Contiguous might distemper the whole frame:<br/>
And Heaven he named the Firmament. So even<br/>
And morning chorus sung the second Day.

"The Earth was formed, but, in the womb as yet<br/>
Of waters, embryon immature, involved,<br/>
Appeared not; over all the face of Earth<br/>
Main ocean flowed, not idle, but, with warm<br/>
Prolific humour softening all her globe,<br/>
Fermented the great Mother to conceive,<br/>
Satiate with genial moisture; when God said,<br/>
'Be gathered now, ye waters under heaven,<br/>
Into one place, and let dry land appear!'<br/>
Immediately the mountains huge appear<br/>
Emergent, and their broad bare backs upheave<br/>
Into the clouds; their tops ascend the sky.<br/>
So high as heaved the tumid hills, so low<br/>
Down sunk a hollow bottom broad and deep,<br/>
Capacious bed of waters. Thither they<br/>
Hasted with glad precipitance, uprowled,<br/>
As drops on dust conglobing, from the dry:<br/>
Part rise in crystal wall, or ridge direct,<br/>
For haste; such flight the great command impressed<br/>
On the swift floods. As armies at the call<br/>
Of trumpet (for of armies thou hast heard)<br/>
Troop to their standard, so the watery throng,<br/>
Wave rowling after wave, where way they found—<br/>
If steep, with torrent rapture, if through plain,<br/>
Soft-ebbing; nor withstood them rock or hill;<br/>
But they, or underground, or circuit wide<br/>
With serpent error wandering, found their way,<br/>
And on the washy ooze deep channels wore:<br/>
Easy, ere God had bid the ground be dry,<br/>
All but within those banks where rivers now<br/>
Stream, and perpetual draw their humid train.<br/>
The dry land Earth, and the great receptacle<br/>
Of congregated waters he called Seas;<br/>
And saw that it was good, and said, 'Let the Earth<br/>
Put forth the verdant grass, herb yielding seed,<br/>
And fruit-tree yielding fruit after her kind,<br/>
Whose seed is in herself upon the Earth!'<br/>
He scarce had said when the bare Earth, till then<br/>
Desert and bare, unsightly, unadorned,<br/>
Brought forth the tender grass, whose verdure clad<br/>
Her universal face with pleasant green;<br/>
Then herbs of every leaf, that sudden flowered,<br/>
Opening their various colours, and made gay<br/>
Her bosom, smelling sweet; and, these scarce blown,<br/>
Forth flourished thick the clustering vine, forth crept<br/>
The smelling gourd, up stood the corny reed<br/>
Imbattled in her field: add the humble shrub,<br/>
And bush with frizzled hair implicit: last<br/>
Rose, as in dance, the stately trees, and spread<br/>
Their branches hung with copious fruit, or gemmed<br/>
Their blossoms. With high woods the hills were crowned,<br/>
With tufts the valleys and each fountain-side,<br/>
With borders long the rivers, that Earth now<br/>
Seemed like to Heaven, a seat where gods might dwell,<br/>
Or wander with delight, and love to haunt<br/>
Her sacred shades; though God had yet not rained<br/>
Upon the Earth, and man to till the ground<br/>
None was, but from the Earth a dewy mist<br/>
Went up and watered all the ground, and each<br/>
Plant of the field, which ere it was in the Earth<br/>
God made, and every herb before it grew<br/>
On the green stem. God saw that it was good;<br/>
So even and morn recorded the third Day.

"Again the Almighty spake, 'Let there be Lights<br/>
High in the expanse of Heaven, to divide<br/>
The Day from Night; and let them be for signs,<br/>
For seasons, and for days, and circling years;<br/>
And let them be for lights, as I ordain<br/>
Their office in the firmament of heaven,<br/>
To give light on the Earth!' and it was so.<br/>
And God made two great Lights, great for their use<br/>
To Man, the greater to have rule by day,<br/>
The less by night, alternor; and made the Stars,<br/>
And set them in the firmament of heaven<br/>
To illuminate the Earth, and rule the day<br/>
In their vicissitude, and rule the night,<br/>
And light from darkness to divide. God saw,<br/>
Surveying his great work, that it was good:<br/>
For, of celestial bodies, first the Sun<br/>
A mighty sphere he framed, unlightsome first,<br/>
Though of ethereal mould; then formed the Moon<br/>
Globose, and every magnitude of Stars,<br/>
And sowed with stars the heaven thick as a field.<br/>
Of light by far the greater part he took,<br/>
Transplanted from her cloudy shrine, and placed<br/>
In the Sun's orb, made porous to receive<br/>
And drink the liquid light, firm to retain<br/>
Her gathered beams, great palace now of Light.<br/>
Hither, as to their fountain, other stars<br/>
Repairing, in their golden urns draw light,<br/>
And hence the morning planet gilds her horns;<br/>
By tincture or reflection they augment<br/>
Their small peculiar, though, from human sight<br/>
So far remote, with diminution seen.<br/>
First in his east the glorious lamp was seen,<br/>
Regent of day, and all the horizon round<br/>
Invested with bright rays, jocond to run<br/>
His longitude through heaven's high-road; the grey<br/>
Dawn, and the Pleiades, before him danced,<br/>
Shedding sweet influence. Less bright the Moon,<br/>
But opposite in levelled west, was set,<br/>
His mirror, with full face borrowing her light<br/>
From him; for other light she needed none<br/>
In that aspect, and still that distance keeps<br/>
Till night; then in the east her turn she shines,<br/>
Revolved on heaven's great axle, and her reign<br/>
With thousand lesser lights dividual holds,<br/>
With thousand thousand stars, that then appeared<br/>
Spangling the hemisphere. Then first adorned<br/>
With her bright luminaries, that set and rose,<br/>
Glad evening and glad morn crowned the fourth Day.

"And God said, 'Let the waters generate<br/>
Reptile with spawn abundant, living soul;<br/>
And let Fowl fly above the earth, with wings<br/>
Displayed on the open firmament of Heaven!'<br/>
And God created the great Whales, and each<br/>
Soul living, each that crept, which plenteously<br/>
The waters generated by their kinds,<br/>
And every bird of wing after his kind,<br/>
And saw that it was good, and blessed them, saying,<br/>
'Be fruitful, multiply, and, in the seas,<br/>
And lakes, and running streams, the waters fill;<br/>
And let the fowl be multiplied on the earth!'<br/>
Forthwith the sounds and seas, each creek and bay,<br/>
With fry innumerable swarm, and shoals<br/>
Of fish that, with their fins and shining scales,<br/>
Glide under the green wave in sculls that oft<br/>
Bank the mid-sea. Part, single or with mate,<br/>
Graze the sea-weed, their pasture, and through groves<br/>
Of coral stray, or, sporting with quick glance,<br/>
Shew to the sun their waved coats dropt with gold,<br/>
Or, in their pearly shells at ease, attend<br/>
Moist nutriment, or under rocks their food<br/>
In jointed armour watch; on smooth the seal<br/>
And bended dolphins play; part, huge of bulk,<br/>
Wallowing unwieldy, enormous in their gait,<br/>
Tempest the ocean. There Leviathan,<br/>
Hugest of living creatures, on the deep<br/>
Stretched like a promontory, sleeps or swims,<br/>
And seems a moving land, and at his gills<br/>
Draws in, and at his trunk spouts out, a sea.<br/>
Meanwhile the tepid caves, and fens, and shores,<br/>
Their brood as numerous hatch from the egg, that soon,<br/>
Bursting with kindly rupture, forth disclosed<br/>
Their callow young; but feathered soon and fledge<br/>
They summed their pens, and, soaring the air sublime,<br/>
With clang despised the ground, under a cloud<br/>
In prospect. There the eagle and the stork<br/>
On cliffs and cedar-tops their eyries build.<br/>
Part loosely wing the Region; part, more wise,<br/>
In common, ranged in figure, wedge their way,<br/>
Intelligent of seasons, and set forth<br/>
Their aerie caravan, high over seas<br/>
Flying, and over lands, with mutual wing<br/>
Easing their flight: so steers the prudent crane<br/>
Her annual voyage, borne on winds: the air<br/>
Floats as they pass, fanned with unnumbered plumes.<br/>
From branch to branch the smaller birds with song<br/>
Solaced the woods, and spread their painted wings,<br/>
Till even; nor then the solemn nightingale<br/>
Ceased warbling, but all night tuned her soft lays.<br/>
Others, on silver lakes and rivers, bathed<br/>
Their downy breast; the swan, with arched neck<br/>
Between her white wings mantling proudly, rows<br/>
Her state with oary feet; yet oft they quit<br/>
The dank, and, rising on stiff pennons, tower<br/>
The mid aerial sky. Others on ground<br/>
Walked firm-the crested cock, whose clarion sounds<br/>
The silent hours, and the other, whose gay train<br/>
Adorns him, coloured with the florid hue<br/>
Of rainbows and starry eyes. The waters thus<br/>
With Fish replenished, and the air with Fowl,<br/>
Evening and morn solemnized the fifth Day.

"The sixth, and of Creation last, arose<br/>
With evening harps and matin; when God said,<br/>
'Let the Earth bring forth soul living in her kind,<br/>
Cattle, and creeping things, and beast of the earth,<br/>
Each in their kind!' The Earth obeyed, and, straight<br/>
Opening her fertile womb, teemed at a birth<br/>
Innumerous living creatures, perfect forms,<br/>
Limbed and full-grown. Out of the ground up rose,<br/>
As from his lair, the wild beast, where he wons<br/>
In forest wild, in thicket, brake, or den—<br/>
Among the trees in pairs they rose, they walked;<br/>
The cattle in the fields and meadows green:<br/>
Those rare and solitary, these in flocks<br/>
Pasturing at once and in broad herds, upsprung.<br/>
The grassy clods now calved; now half appeared<br/>
The tawny Lion, pawing to get free<br/>
His hinder parts-then springs, as broke from bonds,<br/>
And rampant shakes his brinded mane; the Ounce,<br/>
The Libbard, and the Tiger, as the Mole<br/>
Rising, the crumbled earth above them threw<br/>
In hillocks; the swift Stag from underground<br/>
Bore up his branching head; scarce from his mould<br/>
Behemoth, biggest born of earth, upheaved<br/>
His vastness; fleeced the flocks and bleating rose,<br/>
As plants; ambiguous between sea and land,<br/>
The River-horse and scaly Crocodile.<br/>
At once came forth whatever creeps the ground,<br/>
Insect or worm. Those waved their limber fans<br/>
For wings, and smallest lineaments exact<br/>
In all the liveries decked of summer's pride,<br/>
With spots of gold and purple, azure and green;<br/>
These as a line their long dimension drew,<br/>
Streaking the ground with sinuous trace: not all<br/>
Minims of nature; some of serpent kind,<br/>
Wondrous in length and corpulence, involved<br/>
Their snaky folds, and added wings. First crept<br/>
The parsimonious Emmet, provident<br/>
Of future, in small room large heart enclosed—<br/>
Pattern of just equality perhaps<br/>
Hereafter-joined in her popular tribes<br/>
Of commonalty. Swarming next appeared<br/>
The female Bee, that feeds her husband drone<br/>
Deliciously, and builds her waxen cells<br/>
With honey stored. The rest are numberless,<br/>
And thou their natures know'st, and gav'st them names<br/>
Needless to thee repeated; nor unknown<br/>
The Serpent, subtlest beast of all the field,<br/>
Of huge extent sometimes, with brazen eyes<br/>
And hairy mane terrific, though to thee<br/>
Not noxious, but obedient at thy call.

"Now Heaven in all her glory shon, and rowled<br/>
Her motions, as the great First Mover's hand<br/>
First wheeled their course; Earth, in her rich attire<br/>
Consummate, lovely smiled; Air, Water, Earth,<br/>
By fowl, fish, beast, was flown, was swum, was walked<br/>
Frequent; and of the sixth Day yet remained.<br/>
There wanted yet the master-work, the end<br/>
Of all yet done-a creature who, not prone<br/>
And brute as other creatures, but endued<br/>
With sanctity of reason, might erect<br/>
His stature, and, upright with front serene<br/>
Govern the rest, self-knowing, and from thence<br/>
Magnanimous to correspond with Heaven,<br/>
But grateful to acknowledge whence his good<br/>
Descends; thither with heart, and voice, and eyes<br/>
Directed in devotion, to adore<br/>
And worship God Supreme, who made him chief<br/>
Of all his works. Therefore the Omnipotent<br/>
Eternal Father (for where is not He<br/>
Present?) thus to his Son audibly spake:—<br/>
'Let us make now Man in our image, Man<br/>
In our Timilitude, and let them rule<br/>
Over the fish and fowl of sea and air,<br/>
Beast of the field, and over all the earth,<br/>
And every creeping thing that creeps the ground!'<br/>
This said, he formed thee, Adam, thee, O Man,<br/>
Dust of the ground, and in thy nostrils breathed<br/>
The breath of life; in his own image he<br/>
Created thee, in the image of God<br/>
Express, and thou becam'st a living Soul.<br/>
Male he created thee, but thy consort'<br/>
Female, for race; then blessed mankind, and said,<br/>
'Be fruitful, multiply, and fill the Earth;<br/>
Subdue it, and throughout dominion hold<br/>
Over fish of the sea and fowl of the air,<br/>
And every living thing that moves on the Earth!<br/>
Wherever thus created-for no place<br/>
Is yet distinct by name-thence, as thou know'st,<br/>
He brought thee into this delicious grove,<br/>
This Garden, planted with the tress of God,<br/>
Delectable both to behold and taste,<br/>
And freely all their pleasant fruit for food<br/>
Gave thee. All sorts are here that all the earth yields,<br/>
Variety without end; but of the tree<br/>
Which tasted works knowledge of good and evil<br/>
Thou may'st not; in the day thou eat'st, thou diest.<br/>
Death is the penalty imposed; beware,<br/>
And govern well thy appetite, least Sin<br/>
Surprise thee, and her black attendant, Death.

"Here finished He, and all that he had made<br/>
Viewed, and behold! all was entirely good.<br/>
So even and morn accomplished the sixth Day;<br/>
Yet not till the Creator, from his work<br/>
Desisting, though unwearied, up returned,<br/>
Up to the Heaven of Heavens, his high abode,<br/>
Thence to behold this new-created World,<br/>
The addition of his empire, how it shewed<br/>
In prospect from his Throne, how good, how fair,<br/>
Answering his great Idea. Up he rode,<br/>
Followed with acclamation, and the sound<br/>
Symphonious of ten thousand harps, that tuned<br/>
Angelic harmonies. The Earth, the Air<br/>
Resounded (thou remember'st, for thou heard'st),<br/>
The heavens and all the constellations rung,<br/>
The planets in their stations listening stood,<br/>
While the bright pomp ascended jubilant.<br/>
'Open, ye everlasting gates!' they sung;<br/>
'Open, ye Heavens, your living doors! let in<br/>
The great Creator, from his work returned<br/>
Magnificent, his six days' work, a World!<br/>
Open, and henceforth oft; for God will deign<br/>
To visit oft the dwellings of just men<br/>
Delighted, and with frequent intercourse<br/>
Thither will send his winged messengers<br/>
On errands of supernal grace.' So sung<br/>
The glorious train ascending. He through Heaven,<br/>
That opened wide her blazing portals, led<br/>
To God's eternal house direct the way—<br/>
A broad and ample road, whose dust is gold,<br/>
And pavement stars, as stars to thee appear<br/>
Seen in the Galaxy, that milky way<br/>
Which nightly as a circling zone thou seest<br/>
Powdered with stars. And now on Earth the seventh<br/>
Evening arose in Eden-for the sun<br/>
Was set, and twilight from the east came on,<br/>
Forerunning night-when at the holy mount<br/>
Of Heaven's high-seated top, the imperial throne<br/>
Of Godhead, fixed for ever firm and sure,<br/>
The Filial Power arrived, and sat him down<br/>
With his great Father; for He also went<br/>
Invisible, yet stayed (such privilege<br/>
Hath Omnipresence) and the work ordained,<br/>
Author and end of all things, and from work<br/>
Now resting. Blessed and hallowed the seventh Day,<br/>
As resting on that day from all his work;<br/>
But not in silence holy kept: the harp<br/>
Had work, and rested not; the solemn pipe<br/>
And dulcimer, all organs of sweet stop,<br/>
All sounds on fret by string or golden wire,<br/>
Tempered soft tunings, intermixed with voice<br/>
Choral or unison; of incense clouds,<br/>
Fuming from golden censers, hid the Mount.<br/>
Creation and the Six Days' acts they sung:—<br/>
'Great are thy works, Jehovah! infinite<br/>
Thy power! what thought can measure thee, or tongue<br/>
Relate thee-greater now in thy return<br/>
Than from the Giant-angels? Thee that day<br/>
Thy thunders magnified; but to create<br/>
Is greater than created to destroy.<br/>
Who can impair thee, mighty King, or bound<br/>
Thy empire? Easily the proud attempt<br/>
Of Spirits apostate, and their counsels vain,<br/>
Thou hast repelled, while impiously they thought<br/>
Thee to diminish, and from thee withdraw<br/>
The number of thy worshipers. Who seeks<br/>
To lessen thee, against his purpose, serves<br/>
To manifest the more thy might; his evil<br/>
Thou usest, and from thence creat'st more good.<br/>
Witness this new-made World, another Heaven<br/>
From Heaven-gate not far, founded in view<br/>
On the clear hyalin, the glassy sea;<br/>
Of amplitude almost immense, with stars<br/>
Numerous, and every star perhaps a world<br/>
Of destined habitation-but thou know'st<br/>
Their seasons; among these the seat of men,<br/>
Earth, with her nether ocean circumfused,<br/>
Their pleasant dwelling-place. Thrice happy men,<br/>
And sons of men, whom God hath thus advanced,<br/>
Created in his image, there to dwell<br/>
And worship him, and in reward to rule<br/>
Over his works, on earth, in sea, or air,<br/>
And multiply a race of worshipers<br/>
Holy and just! thrice happy, if they know<br/>
Their happiness, and persevere upright!'

"So sung they, and the Empyrean rung<br/>
With halleluiahs. Thus was Sabbath kept.<br/>
And thy request think now fulfilled that asked<br/>
How first this World and face of things began,<br/>
And what before thy memory was done<br/>
From the beginning, that posterity,<br/>
Informed by thee, might know. If else thou seek'st<br/>
Aught, not surpassing human measure, say."

<h2>Book 8</h2>

The Angel ended, and in Adam's ear<br/>
So charming left his voice that he a while<br/>
Thought him still speaking, still stood fixed to hear;<br/>
Then, as new-waked, thus gratefully replied:—

"What thanks sufficient, or what recompense<br/>
Equal, have I to render thee, divine<br/>
Historian, who thus largely hast allayed<br/>
The thirst I had of knowledge, and voutsafed<br/>
This friendly condescension to relate<br/>
Things else by me unsearchable-now heard<br/>
With wonder, but delight, and, as is due,<br/>
With glory attributed to the high<br/>
Creator? Something yet of doubt remains,<br/>
Which only thy solution can resolve.<br/>
When I behold this goodly frame, this World,<br/>
Of Heaven and Earth consisting, and compute<br/>
Their magnitudes-this Earth, a spot, a grain,<br/>
An atom, with the Firmament compared<br/>
And all her numbered stars, that seem to rowl<br/>
Spaces incomprehensible (for such<br/>
Their distance argues, and their swift return<br/>
Diurnal) merely to officiate light<br/>
Round this opacous Earth, this punctual spot,<br/>
One day and night, in all their vast survey<br/>
Useless besides-reasoning, I oft admire<br/>
How Nature, wise and frugal, could commit<br/>
Such disproportions, with superfluous hand<br/>
So many nobler bodies to create,<br/>
Greater so manifold, to this one use,<br/>
For aught appears, and on their Orbs impose<br/>
Such restless revolution day by day<br/>
Repeated, while the sedentary Earth,<br/>
That better might with far less compass move,<br/>
Served by more noble than herself, attains<br/>
Her end without least motion, and receives,<br/>
As tribute, such a sumless journey brought<br/>
Of incorporeal speed her warmth and light:<br/>
Speed, to describe whose swiftness number fails."

So spake our Sire, and by his countenance seemed<br/>
Entering on studious thoughts abstruse; which Eve<br/>
Perceiving, where, she sat retired in sight,<br/>
With lowliness majestic from her seat,<br/>
And grace that won who saw to wish her stay,<br/>
Rose, and went forth among her fruits and flowers,<br/>
To visit how they prospered, bud and bloom,<br/>
Her nursery; they at her coming sprung,<br/>
And, touched by her fair tendance, gladlier grew.<br/>
Yet went she not as not with such discourse<br/>
Delighted, or not capable her ear<br/>
Of what was high. Such pleasure she reserved,<br/>
Adam relating, she sole auditress;<br/>
Her husband the relater she preferred<br/>
Before the Angel, and of him to ask<br/>
Chose rather; he, she knew, would intermix<br/>
Grateful digressions, and solve high dispute<br/>
With conjugal caresses: from his lip<br/>
Not words alone pleased her. Oh, when meet now<br/>
Such pairs, in love and mutual honour joined?<br/>
With goddess-like demeanour forth she went,<br/>
Not unattended; for on her as Queen<br/>
A pomp of winning Graces waited still,<br/>
And from about her shot darts of desire<br/>
Into all eyes, to wish her still in sight.<br/>
And Raphael now to Adam's doubt proposed<br/>
Benevolent and facile thus replied:—

"To ask or search I blame thee not; for Heaven<br/>
Is as the Book of God before thee set,<br/>
Wherein to read his wondrous works, and learn<br/>
His seasons, hours, or days, or months, or years.<br/>
This to attain, whether Heaven move or Earth<br/>
Imports not, if thou reckon right; the rest<br/>
From Man or Angel the great Architect<br/>
Did wisely to conceal, and not divulge<br/>
His secrets, to be scanned by them who ought<br/>
Rather admire. Or, if they list to try<br/>
Conjecture, he his fabric of the Heavens<br/>
Hath left to their disputes-perhaps to move<br/>
His laughter at their quaint opinions wide<br/>
Hereafter, when they come to model Heaven,<br/>
And calculate the stars; how they will wield<br/>
The mighty frame; how build, unbuild, contrive<br/>
To save appearances; how gird the Sphere<br/>
With Centric and Eccentric scribbled o'er,<br/>
Cycle and Epicycle, orb in orb.<br/>
Already by thy reasoning this I guess,<br/>
Who art to lead thy offspring, and supposest<br/>
That bodies bright and greater should not serve<br/>
The less not bright, nor Heaven such journeys run,<br/>
Earth sitting still, when she alone receives<br/>
The benefit. Consider, first, that great<br/>
Or bright infers not excellence. The Earth,<br/>
Though, in comparison of Heaven, so small,<br/>
Nor glistering, may of solid good contain<br/>
More plenty than the Sun that barren shines,<br/>
Whose virtue on itself works no effect,<br/>
But in the fruitful Earth; there first receivod,<br/>
His beams, unactive else, their vigour find.<br/>
Yet not to Earth are those bright luminaries<br/>
Officious, but to thee, Earth's habitant.<br/>
And, for the Heaven's wide circuit, let it speak<br/>
The Maker's high magnificence, who built<br/>
So spacious, and his line stretched out so far,<br/>
That Man may know he dwells not in his own—<br/>
An edifice too large for him to fill,<br/>
Lodged in a small partition, and the rest<br/>
Ordained for uses to his Lord best known.<br/>
The swiftness of those Circles at'tribute,<br/>
Though numberless, to his Omnipotence,<br/>
That to corporeal substances could add<br/>
Speed almost spiritual. Me thou think'st not slow,<br/>
Who since the morning-hour set out from Heaven<br/>
Where God resides, and ere mid-day arrived<br/>
In Eden-distance inexpressible<br/>
By numbers that have name. But this I urge,<br/>
Admitting motion in the Heavens, to shew<br/>
Invalid that which thee to doubt it moved;<br/>
Not that I so affirm, though so it seem<br/>
To thee who hast thy dwelling here on Earth.<br/>
God, to remove his ways from human sense,<br/>
Placed Heaven from Earth so far, that earthly sight,<br/>
If it presume, might err in things too high,<br/>
And no advantage gain. What if the Sun<br/>
Be centre to the World, and other Stars,<br/>
By his attractive virtue and their own<br/>
Incited, dance about him various rounds?<br/>
Their wandering course, now high, now low, then hid,<br/>
Progressive, retrograde, or standing still,<br/>
In six thou seest; and what if, seventh to these<br/>
The planet Earth, so steadfast though she seem,<br/>
Insensibly three different motions move?<br/>
Which else to several spheres thou must ascribe,<br/>
Moved contrary with thwart obliquities,<br/>
Or save the Sun his labour, and that swift<br/>
Nocturnal and diurnal rhomb supposed,<br/>
Invisible else above all stars, the wheel<br/>
Of Day and Night; which needs not they belief,<br/>
If Earth, industrious of herself, fetch Day,<br/>
Travelling east, and with her part averse<br/>
From the Sun's beam meet Night, her other part<br/>
Still luminous by his ray. What if that light,<br/>
Sent from her through the wide transpicuous air,<br/>
To the terrestrial Moon to be as a star,<br/>
Enlightening her by day, as she by night<br/>
This Earth-reciprocal, if land be there,<br/>
Fields and inhabitants? Her spots thou seest<br/>
As clouds, and clouds may rain, and rain produce<br/>
Fruits in her softened soil, for some to eat<br/>
Allotted there; and other Suns, perhaps,<br/>
With their attendant Moons, thou wilt descry,<br/>
Communicating male and female light—<br/>
Which to great sexes animate the World,<br/>
Stored in each Orb perhaps with some that live.<br/>
For such vast room in Nature unpossessed<br/>
By living soul, desert and desolate,<br/>
Only to shine, yet scarce to con'tribute<br/>
Each Orb a glimpse of light, conveyed so far<br/>
Down to this habitable, which returns<br/>
Light back to them, is obvious to dispute.<br/>
But whether thus these things, or whether not—<br/>
Whether the Sun, predominant in heaven,<br/>
Rise on the Earth, or Earth rise on the Sun;<br/>
He from the east his flaming road begin,<br/>
Or she from west her silent course advance<br/>
With inoffensive pace that spinning sleeps<br/>
On her soft axle, while she paces even,<br/>
And bears thee soft with the smooth air along—<br/>
Solicit not thy thoughts with matters hid:<br/>
Leave them to God above; him serve and fear.<br/>
Of other creatures as him pleases best,<br/>
Wherever placed, let him dispose; joy thou<br/>
In what he gives to thee, this Paradise<br/>
And thy fair Eve; Heaven is for thee too high<br/>
To know what passes there. Be lowly wise;<br/>
Think only what concerns thee and thy being;<br/>
Dream not to other worlds, what creatures there<br/>
Live, in what state, condition, or degreed-<br/>
Contented that thus far hath been revealed<br/>
Not of Earth only, but of highest Heaven."

To whom thus Adam, cleared of doubt, replied:—<br/>
"How fully hast thou satisfied me, pure<br/>
Intelligence of Heaven, Angel serene,<br/>
And, freed from intricacies, taught to live<br/>
The easiest way, nor with perplexing thoughts<br/>
To interrupt the sweet of life, from which<br/>
God hath bid dwell far off all anxious cares,<br/>
And not molest us, unless we ourselves<br/>
Seek them with wandering thoughts, and notions vain!<br/>
But apt the mind or fancy is to rove<br/>
Unchecked; and of her roving is no end,<br/>
Till, warned, or by experience taught, she learn<br/>
That not to know at large of things remote<br/>
From use, obscure and subtle, but to know<br/>
That which before us lies in daily life,<br/>
Is the prime wisdom: what is more is fume,<br/>
Or emptiness, or fond impertinence,<br/>
And renders us in things that most concern<br/>
Unpractised, unprepared, and still to seek.<br/>
Therefore from this high pitch let us descend<br/>
A lower flight, and speak of things at hand<br/>
Useful; whence, haply, mentiin may arise<br/>
Of something not unreasonable to ask,<br/>
By sufferance, and thy wonted favour, deigned.<br/>
Thee I have heard relating what was done<br/>
Ere my remembrance; now hear me relate<br/>
My story, which perhaps, thou hast not heard.<br/>
And day is yet not spent; till then thou seest<br/>
How subtly to detain thee I devise,<br/>
Inviting thee to hear while I relate—<br/>
Fond, were it not in hope of thy reply.<br/>
For, while I sit with thee, I seem in Heaven;<br/>
And sweeter thy discourse is to my ear<br/>
Than fruits of palm-tree, pleasantest to thirst<br/>
And hunger both, from labour, at the hour<br/>
Of sweet repast. They satiate, and soon fill,<br/>
Though pleasant; but thy words, with grace divine<br/>
Imbued, bring to their sweetness no satiety."

To whom thus Raphael answered, heavenly meek:—<br/>
"Nor are thy lips ungrateful, Sire of Men,<br/>
Nor tongue ineloquent; for God on thee<br/>
Abundantly his gifts hath also poured,<br/>
Inward and outward both, his image fair:<br/>
Speaking, or mute, all comeliness and grace<br/>
Attends thee, and each word, each motion, forms.<br/>
Nor less think we in Heaven of thee on Earth<br/>
Than of our fellow-servant, and inquire<br/>
Gladly into the ways of God with Man;<br/>
For God, we see, hath honoured thee, and set<br/>
On Man his equal love. Say therefore on;<br/>
For I that day was absent, as befell,<br/>
Bound on a voyage uncouth and obscure,<br/>
Far on excursion toward the gates of Hell,<br/>
Squared in full legion (such command we had),<br/>
To see that none thence issued forth a spy<br/>
Or enemy, while God was in his work,<br/>
Lest he, incensed at such eruption bold,<br/>
Destruction with Creation might have mixed.<br/>
Not that they durst without his leave attempt;<br/>
But us he sends upon his high behests<br/>
For state, as sovran King, and to inure<br/>
Our prompt obedience. Fast we found, fast shut,<br/>
The dismal gates, and barricaded strong,<br/>
But, long ere our approaching, heard within<br/>
Noise, other than the sound of dance or song—<br/>
Torment, and loud lament, and furious rage.<br/>
Glad we returned up to the coasts of Light<br/>
Ere Sabbath-evening; so we had in charge.<br/>
But thy relation now: for I attend,<br/>
Pleased with thy words no less than thou with mine."

So spake the godlike Power, and thus our Sire:—<br/>
"For Man to tell how human life began<br/>
Is hard; for who himself beginning knew?<br/>
Desire with thee still longer to converse<br/>
Induced me. As new-waked from soundest sleep,<br/>
Soft on the flowery herb I found me laid,<br/>
In balmy sweat, which with his beams the Sun<br/>
Soon dried, and on the reeking moisture fed.<br/>
Straight toward Heaven my wondering eyes I turned,<br/>
And gazed a while the ample sky, till, raised<br/>
By quick instinctive motion, up I sprung,<br/>
As thitherward endeavoring, and upright<br/>
Stood on my feet. About me round I saw<br/>
Hill, dale, and shady woods, and sunny plains,<br/>
And liquid lapse of murmuring streams; by these,<br/>
Creatures that lived and moved, and walked or flew,<br/>
Birds on the branches warbling: all things smiled;<br/>
With fragrance and with joy my heart o'erflowed.<br/>
Myself I then perused, and limb by limb<br/>
Surveyed, and sometimes went, and sometimes ran<br/>
With supple joints, as lively vigour led;<br/>
But who I was, or where, or from what cause,<br/>
Knew not. To speak I tried, and forthwith spake;<br/>
My tongue obeyed, and readily could name<br/>
Whate'er I saw. 'Thou Sun,' said I, 'fair light,<br/>
And thou enlightened Earth, so fresh and gay,<br/>
Ye hills and dales, ye rivers, woods, and plains,<br/>
And ye that live and move, fair creatures, tell,<br/>
Tell, if ye saw, how came I thus, how here!<br/>
Not of myself; by some great Maker then,<br/>
tin goodness and in power prae-eminent.<br/>
Tell me, how may I know him, how adore,<br/>
From whom I have that thus I move and live,<br/>
And feel that I am happier than I know!'<br/>
While thus I called, and strayed I knew not whither,<br/>
From where I first drew air, and first beheld<br/>
This happy light, when answer none returned,<br/>
On a green shady bank, profuse of flowers,<br/>
Pensive I sat me down. There gentle sleep<br/>
First found me, and with soft oppression seized<br/>
My drowsed sense, untroubled, though I thought<br/>
I then was passing to my former state<br/>
Insensible, and forthwith to dissolve:<br/>
When suddenly stood at my head a Dream,<br/>
Whose inward apparition gently moved<br/>
My fancy to believe I yet had being,<br/>
And lived. One came, methought, of shape divine,<br/>
And said, 'Thy mansion wants thee, Adam; rise,<br/>
First Man, of men innumerable ordained<br/>
First father! called by thee, I come thy guide<br/>
To the Garden of bliss, thy seat prepared.'<br/>
So saying, by the hand he took me, raised,<br/>
And over fields and waters, as in air<br/>
Smooth sliding without step, last led me up<br/>
A woody mountain, whose high top was plain,<br/>
A circuit wide, enclosed, with goodliest trees<br/>
Planted, with walks and bowers, that what I saw<br/>
Of Earth before scarce pleasant seemed. Each tree<br/>
Loaden with fairest fruit, that hung to the eye<br/>
Tempting, stirred in me sudden appetite<br/>
To pluck and eat; whereat I waked, and found<br/>
Before mine eyes all real, as the dream<br/>
Had lively shadowed. Here had new begun<br/>
My wandering, had not He who was my guide<br/>
Up hither from among the trees appeared,<br/>
Presence Divine. Rejoicing, but with awe,<br/>
In adoration at his feet I fell<br/>
Submiss. He reared me, and, 'Whom thou sought'st I am,'<br/>
Said mildly, 'Author of all this thou seest<br/>
Above, or round about thee, or beneath.<br/>
This Paradise I give thee; count it thine<br/>
To till and keep, and of the fruit to eat.<br/>
Of every tree that in the Garden grows<br/>
Eat freely with glad heart; fear here no dearth.<br/>
But of the tree whose operation brings<br/>
Knowledge of Good and Ill, which I have set,<br/>
The pledge of thy obedience and thy faith,<br/>
Amid the garden by the Tree of Life—<br/>
Remember what I warn thee-shun to taste,<br/>
And shun the bitter consequence: for know,<br/>
The day thou eat'st thereof, my sole command<br/>
Transgressed, inevitably thou shalt die,<br/>
From that day mortal, and this happy state<br/>
Shalt lose, expelled from hence into a world<br/>
Of woe and sorrow.' Sternly he pronounced<br/>
The rigid interdiction, which resounds<br/>
Yet dreadful in mine ear, though in my choice<br/>
Not to incur; but soon his clear aspect'<br/>
Returned, and gracious purpose thus renewed:—<br/>
'Not only these fair bounds, but all the Earth<br/>
To thee and to thy race I give; as lords<br/>
Possess it, and all things that therein live,<br/>
Or live in sea or air, beast, fish, and fowl.<br/>
In sign whereof, each bird and beast behold<br/>
After their kinds; I bring them to receive<br/>
From thee their names, and pay thee fealty<br/>
With low subjection. Understand the same<br/>
Of fish within their watery residence,<br/>
Not hither summoned, since they cannot change<br/>
Their element to draw the thinner air.'<br/>
As thus he spake, each bird and beast behold<br/>
Approaching two and two-these cowering low<br/>
With blandishment; each bird stooped on his wing.<br/>
I named them as they passed, and understood<br/>
Their nature; with such knowledge God endued<br/>
My sudden apprehension. But in these<br/>
I found not what methought I wanted still,<br/>
And to the Heavenly Vision thus presumed:—

"'O, by what name-or Thou above all these,<br/>
Above mankind, or aught than mankind higher,<br/>
Surpassestwfar my naming-how may I<br/>
Adore thee, Author of this Universe,<br/>
And all this good to Man, for whose well-being<br/>
So amply, and with hands so liberal,<br/>
Thou hast provided all things? But with me<br/>
I see not who partakes. In solitude<br/>
What happiness? who can enjoy alone,<br/>
Or, all enjoying, what contentment find?'<br/>
Thus I, presumptuous; and the Vision bright,<br/>
As with a smile more brightened, thus replied:—

"'What call'st thou solitude? Is not the Earth<br/>
With various living creatures, and the Air,<br/>
Replenished, and all these at thy command<br/>
To come and play before thee? Know'st thou not<br/>
Their language and their ways? They also know,<br/>
And reason not contemptibly; with these<br/>
Find pastime, and bear rule; thy realm is large.'<br/>
So spake the Universal Lord and seemed<br/>
So ordering. I, with leave of speech implored,<br/>
And humble deprecation, thus replied:—

"'Let not my words offend thee, Heavenly Power;<br/>
My Maker, be propitious while I speak.<br/>
Hast thou not made me here thy substitute,<br/>
And these inferior far beneath me set?<br/>
Among unequals what society<br/>
Can sort, what harmony or true delight?<br/>
Which must be mutual, in proportion due<br/>
Given and received; but, in disparity,<br/>
The one intense, the other still remiss,<br/>
Clnnot well suit with either, but soon prove<br/>
Tedious alike. Of fellowship I speak<br/>
Such as I seek, fit to participate<br/>
All rational delight, wherein the brute<br/>
Cannot be human consort. They rejoice<br/>
Each with their kind, lion with lioness;<br/>
So fitly them in pairs thou hast combined:<br/>
Much less can bird with beast, or fish with fowl,<br/>
So well converse, nor with the ox the ape;<br/>
Worse, then, can man with beast, and least of all.'

"Whereto the Almighty answered, not displeased:—<br/>
'A nice and subtle happiness, I see,<br/>
Thou to thyself proposest, in the choice<br/>
Of thy associates, Adam, and wilt taste<br/>
No pleasure, though in pleasure, solitary.<br/>
What think'st thou, then, of Me, and this my state?<br/>
Seem I to thee sufficiently possessed<br/>
Of happiness, or not, who am alone<br/>
From all eternity? for none I know<br/>
Second to me or like, equal much less.<br/>
How have I, then, with whom to hold converse,<br/>
Save with the creatures which I made, and those<br/>
To me inferior infinite descents<br/>
Beneath what other creatures are to thee?'

"He ceased. I lowly answered:—'To attain<br/>
The highth and depth of thy eternal ways<br/>
All human thoughts come short, Supreme of Things!<br/>
Thou in thyself art perfect, and in Thee<br/>
Is no deficience found. Not so is Man,<br/>
But in degree-the cause of his desire<br/>
By conversation with his like to help<br/>
Or solace his defects. No need that thou<br/>
Should'st propagate, already infinite,<br/>
And through all numbers absolute, though One;<br/>
But Man by number is to manifest<br/>
His single imperfection, and beget<br/>
Like of his like, his image multiplied,<br/>
In unity defective; which requires<br/>
Collateral love, and dearest amity.<br/>
Thou, in thy secrecy although alone,<br/>
Best with thyself accompanied, seek'st not<br/>
Social communication-yet, so pleased,<br/>
Canst raise thy creature to what highth thou wilt<br/>
Of union or communion, deified;<br/>
I, by conversing, cannot these erect<br/>
From prone, nor in their ways complacence find.<br/>
Thus I emboldened spake, and freedom used<br/>
Permissive, and acceptance found; which gained<br/>
This answer from the gratious Voice Divine:—

"'Thus far to try thee, Adam, I was pleased,<br/>
And find thee knowing not of beasts alone,<br/>
Which thou hast rightly named, but of thyself—<br/>
Expressing well the spirit within thee free,<br/>
My image, not imparted to the brute;<br/>
Whose fellowship, therefore, unmeet for thee,<br/>
Good Reason was thou freely shouldst dislike.<br/>
And be so minded still. I, ere thou spak'st,<br/>
Knew it not good for Man to be alone,<br/>
And no such company as then thou saw'st<br/>
Intended thee-for trial only brought,<br/>
To see how thou couldst judge of fit and meet.<br/>
What next I bring shall please thee, be assured,<br/>
Thy likeness, thy fit help, thy other self,<br/>
Thy wish exactly to thy heart's desire.'

"He ended, or I heard no more; for now<br/>
My earthly, by his heavenly overpowered,<br/>
Which it had long stood under, strained to the highth<br/>
In that celestial colloquy sublime,<br/>
As with an object that excels the sense,<br/>
Dazzled and spent, sunk down, and sought repair<br/>
Of sleep, which instantly fell on me, called<br/>
By Nature as in aid, and closed mine eyes.<br/>
Mine eyes he closed, but open left the cell<br/>
Of fancy, my internal sight; by which,<br/>
Abstract as in a trance, methought I saw,<br/>
Though sleeping, where I lay, and saw the Shape<br/>
Still glorious before whom awake I stood;<br/>
Who, stooping, opened my left side, and took<br/>
From thence a rib, with cordial spirits warm,<br/>
And life-blood streaming fresh; wide was the wound,<br/>
But suddenly with flesh filled up and healed.<br/>
The rib he formed and fashioned with his hands;<br/>
Under his forming hands a creature grew,<br/>
Man-like, but different sex, so lovely fair<br/>
That what seemed fair in all the world seemed now<br/>
Mean, or in her summed up, in her contained<br/>
And in her looks, which from that time infused<br/>
Sweetness into my heart unfelt before,<br/>
And into all things from her air inspired<br/>
The spirit of love and amorous delight.<br/>
She disappeared, and left me dark; I waked<br/>
To find her, or for ever to deplore<br/>
Her loss, and other pleasures all adjure:<br/>
When, out of hope, behold her not far off,<br/>
Such as I saw her in my dream, adorned<br/>
With what all Earth or Heaven could bestow<br/>
To make her amiable. On she came,<br/>
Led by her Heavenly Maker, though unseen<br/>
And guided by his voice, nor uninformed<br/>
Of nuptial sanctity and marriage rites.<br/>
Grace was in all her steps, heaven in her eye,<br/>
In every gesture dignity and love.<br/>
I, overjoyed, could not forbear aloud:—

"'This turn hath made amends; thou hast fulfilled<br/>
Thy words, Creator bounteous and benign,<br/>
Giver of all things fair-but fairest this<br/>
Of all thy gifts!-nor enviest. I now see<br/>
Bone of my bone, flesh of my flesh, my Self<br/>
Before me. Woman is her name, of Man<br/>
Extracted; for this cause he shall forgo<br/>
Father and mother, and to his wife adhere,<br/>
And they shall be one flesh, one heart, one soul.'

"She heard me thus; and, though divinely brought,<br/>
Yet innocence and virgin modesty,<br/>
Her virtue, and the conscience of her worth,<br/>
That would be wooed, and not unsought be won,<br/>
Not obvious, not obtrusive, but retired,<br/>
The most desirable-or, to say all,<br/>
Nature herself, though pure of sinful thought—<br/>
Wrought in her so, that, seeing me, she turned.<br/>
I followed her; she what was honour knew,<br/>
And with obsequious majesty approved<br/>
My pleaded reason. To the nuptial bower<br/>
I led her blushing like the Morn; all Heaven,<br/>
And happy constellations, on that hour<br/>
Shed their selectest influence; the Earth<br/>
Gave sign of gratulation, and each hill;<br/>
Joyous the birds; fresh gales and gentle airs<br/>
Whispered it to the woods, and from their wings<br/>
Flung rose, flung odours from the spicy shrub,<br/>
Disporting, till the amorous bird of night<br/>
Sung spousal, and bid haste the Evening-star<br/>
On his hill-top to light the bridal lamp.

"Thus have I told thee all my state, and brought<br/>
My story to the sum of earthly bliss<br/>
Which I enjoy, and must confess to find<br/>
In all things else delight indeed, but such<br/>
As, use or not, works in the mind no change,<br/>
Nor vehement desire-these delicacies<br/>
I mean of taste, sight, smell, herbs, fruits, and flowers,<br/>
Walks, and the melody of birds: but here,<br/>
Far otherwise, transported I behold,<br/>
Transported touch; here passion first I felt,<br/>
Commotion strange, in all enjoyments else<br/>
Superior and unmoved, here only weak<br/>
Against the charm of beauty's powerful glance.<br/>
Or Nature failed in me, and left some part<br/>
Not proof enough such object to sustain,<br/>
Or, from my side subducting, took perhaps<br/>
More than enough-at least on her bestowed<br/>
Too much of ornament, in outward show<br/>
Elaborate, of inward less exact.<br/>
For well I understand in the prime end<br/>
Of Nature her the inferior, in the mind<br/>
And inward faculties, which most excel;<br/>
In outward also her resembling less<br/>
His image who made both, and less expressing<br/>
The character of that dominion given<br/>
O'er other creatures. Yet when I approach<br/>
Her loveliness, so absolute she seems<br/>
And in herself complete, so well to know<br/>
Her own, that what she wills to do or say<br/>
Seems wisest, virtuousest, discreetest, best.<br/>
All higher Knowledge in her presence falls<br/>
Degraded; Wisdom in discourse with her<br/>
Loses, discountenanced, and like Folly shews;<br/>
Authority and Reason on her wait,<br/>
As one intended first, not after made<br/>
Occasionally; and, to consum'mate all,<br/>
Greatness of mind and nobleness their seat<br/>
Build in her loveliest, and create an awe<br/>
About her, as a guard angelic placed."

To whom the Angel, with contracted brow:—<br/>
"Accuse not Nature! she hath done her part;<br/>
Do thou but thine! and be not diffident<br/>
Of Wisdom; she deserts thee not, if thou<br/>
Dismiss not her, when most thou need'st her nigh,<br/>
By attribu'ting overmuch to things<br/>
Less excellent, as thou thyself perceiv'st.<br/>
For, what admir'st thou, what transports thee so?<br/>
An outside-fair, no doubt, and worthy well<br/>
Thy cherishing, thy honouring, and thy love;<br/>
Not thy subjection. Weigh with her thyself;<br/>
Then value. Oft-times nothing profits more<br/>
Than self-esteem, grounded on just and right<br/>
Well managed. Of that skill the more thou know'st,<br/>
The more she will acknowledge thee her head,<br/>
And to realities yield all her shows—<br/>
Made so adorn for thy delight the more,<br/>
So awful, that with honour thou may'st love<br/>
Thy mate, who sees when thou art seen least wise<br/>
But, if the sense of touch, whereby mankind<br/>
Is propagated, seem such dear delight<br/>
Beyond all other, think the same voutsafed<br/>
To cattle and each beast; which would not be<br/>
