To them made common and divulged, if aught<br/>
Therein enjoyed were worthy to subdue<br/>
The soul of Man, or passion in him move.<br/>
What higher in her society thou find'st<br/>
Attractive, human, rational, love still;<br/>
In loving thou dost well; in passion not,<br/>
Wherein true Love consists not. Love refines<br/>
The thoughts, and heart enlarges-hath his seat<br/>
In Reason, and is judicious, is the scale<br/>
By which to Heavenly Love thou may'st ascend,<br/>
Not sunk in carnal pleasure; for which cause<br/>
Among the beasts no mate for thee was found."

To whom thus, half abashed, Adam replied:—<br/>
"Neither her outside formed so fair, nor aught<br/>
In procreation, common to all kinds<br/>
(Though higher of the genial bed by far,<br/>
And with mysterious reverence, I deem),<br/>
So much delights me as those graceful acts,<br/>
Those thousand decencies, that daily flow<br/>
From all her words and actions, mixed with love<br/>
And sweet compliance, which declare unfeigned<br/>
Union of mind, or in us both one soul—<br/>
Harmony to behold in wedded pair<br/>
More grateful than harmonious sound to the ear.<br/>
Yet these subject not; I to thee disclose<br/>
What inward thence I feel, not therefore foiled,<br/>
Who meet with various objects, from the sense<br/>
Variously representing, yet, still free,<br/>
Approve the best, and follow what I approve.<br/>
To love thou blam'st me not-for Love, thou say'st,<br/>
Leads up to Heaven, is both the way and guide;<br/>
Bear with me, then, if lawful what I ask.<br/>
Love not the Heavenly Spirits, and how their love<br/>
Express they-by looks only, or do they mix<br/>
Irradiance, virtual or immediate touch?"

To whom the Angel, with a smile that glowed<br/>
Celestial rosy-red, Love's proper hue,<br/>
Answered:—"Let it suffice thee that thou know'st<br/>
Us happy, and without Love no happiness.<br/>
Whatever pure thou in the body enjoy'st<br/>
(And pure thou wert created) we enjoy<br/>
In eminence, and obstacle find none<br/>
Of membrane, joint, or limb, exclusive bars.<br/>
Easier than air with air, if Spirits embrace,<br/>
Total they mix, union of pure with pure<br/>
Desiring, nor restrained conveyance need<br/>
As flesh to mix with flesh, or soul with soul.<br/>
But I can now no more: the parting Sun<br/>
Beyond the Earth's green Cape and verdant Isles<br/>
Hesperean sets, my signal to depart.<br/>
Be strong, live happy, and love! but first of all<br/>
Him whom to love is to obey, and keep<br/>
His great command; take heed lest passion sway<br/>
Thy judgment to do aught which else free-will<br/>
Would not admit; thine and of all thy sons<br/>
The weal or woe in thee is placed; beware!<br/>
I in thy persevering shall rejoice,<br/>
And all the Blest. Stand fast; to stand or fall<br/>
Free in thine own arbitrement it lies.<br/>
Perfet within, no outward aid require;<br/>
And all temptation to transgress repel."

So saying, he arose; whom Adam thus<br/>
Followed with benediction:—"Since to part,<br/>
Go, Heavenly Guest, Ethereal Messenger,<br/>
Sent from whose sovran goodness I adore!<br/>
Gentle to me and affable hath been<br/>
Thy condescension, and shall be honoured ever<br/>
With grateful memory. Thou to Mankind<br/>
Be good and friendly still, and oft return!"

So parted they, the Angel up to Heaven<br/>
From the thick shade, and Adam to his bower.

<h2>Book 9</h2>

No more of talk where God or Angel Guest<br/>
With Man, as with his friend, familiar used<br/>
To sit indulgent, and with him partake<br/>
Rural repast, permitting him to while<br/>
Venial discourse unblamed. I now must change<br/>
Those notes to tragic-foul distrust, and breach<br/>
Disloyal, on the part of man, revolt<br/>
And disobedience; on the part of Heaven,<br/>
Now alienated, distance and distaste,<br/>
Anger and just rebuke, and judgment given,<br/>
That brought into this World a world of woe,<br/>
Sin and her shadow Death, and Misery,<br/>
Death's harbinger. Sad task! yet argument<br/>
Not less but more heroic than the wrauth<br/>
Of stern Achilles on his foe pursued<br/>
Thrice fugitive about Troy wall; or rage<br/>
Of Turnus for Lavinia disespoused;<br/>
Or Neptune's ire, or Juno's that so long<br/>
Perplexed the Greek, and Cytherea's son:<br/>
If answerable style I can obtain<br/>
Of my celestial Patroness, who deigns<br/>
Her nightly visitation unimplored,<br/>
And dictates to me slumbering, or inspires<br/>
Easy my unpremeditated verse,<br/>
Since first this subject for heroic song<br/>
Pleased me, long choosing and beginning late,<br/>
Not sedulous by nature to indite<br/>
Wars, hitherto the only argument<br/>
Heroic deemed, chief maistrie to dissect<br/>
With long and tedious havoc fabled knights<br/>
In battles feigned (the better fortitude<br/>
Of patience and heroic martyrdom<br/>
Unsung), or to describe races and games,<br/>
Or tilting furniture, emblazoned shields,<br/>
Impreses quaint, caparisons and steeds,<br/>
Bases and tinsel trappings, gorgeous knights<br/>
At joust and tournament; then marshalled feast<br/>
Served up in hall with sewers and seneshals:<br/>
The skill of artifice or office mean;<br/>
Not that which justly gives heroic name<br/>
To person or to poem! Me, of these<br/>
Nor skilled nor studious, higher argument<br/>
Remains, sufficient of itself to raise<br/>
That name, unless an age too late, or cold<br/>
Climat, or years, damp my intended wing<br/>
Depressed; and much they may if all be mine,<br/>
Not Hers who brings it nightly to my ear.

The Sun was sunk, and after him the Star<br/>
Of Hesperus, whose office is to bring<br/>
Twilight upon the Earth, short arbiter<br/>
'Twixt day and night, and now from end to end<br/>
Night's hemisphere had veiled the horizon round,<br/>
When Satan, who late fled before the threats<br/>
Of Gabriel out of Eden, now improved<br/>
In meditated fraud and malice, bent<br/>
On Man's destruction, maugre what might hap<br/>
Of heavier on himself, fearless returned.<br/>
By night he fled, and at midnight returned<br/>
From compassing the Earth-cautious of day<br/>
Since Uriel, Regent of the Sun, descried<br/>
His entrance, and forwarned the Cherubim<br/>
That kept their watch. Thence, full of anguish, driven,<br/>
The space of seven continued nights he rode<br/>
With darkness-thrice the equinoctial line<br/>
He circled, four times crossed the car of Night<br/>
From pole to pole, traversing each colure—<br/>
On the eighth returned, and on the coast averse<br/>
From entrance or cherubic watch by stealth<br/>
Found unsuspected way. There was a place<br/>
(Now not, though Sin, not Time, first wraught the change)<br/>
Where Tigris, at the foot of Paradise,<br/>
Into a gulf shot under ground, till part<br/>
Rose up a fountain by the Tree of Life.<br/>
In with the river sunk, and with it rose,<br/>
Satan, involved in rising mist; then sought<br/>
Where to lie hid. Sea he had searched and land<br/>
From Eden over Pontus, and the Pool<br/>
Maeotis, up beyond the river Ob;<br/>
Downward as far Antartic; and, in length,<br/>
West from Orontes to the ocean barred<br/>
At Darien, thence to the land where flows<br/>
Ganges and Indus. Thus the orb he roamed<br/>
With narrow search, and with inspection deep<br/>
Considered every creature, which of all<br/>
Most opportune might serve his wiles, and found<br/>
The Serpent subtlest beast of all the field.<br/>
Him, after long debate, irresolute<br/>
Of thoughts revolved, his final sentence chose<br/>
Fit vessel, fittest Imp of fraud, in whom<br/>
To enter, and his dark suggestions hide<br/>
From sharpest sight; for in the wily snake<br/>
Whatever sleights none would suspicious mark<br/>
As from his wit and native subtlety<br/>
Proceeding, which, in other beasts observed,<br/>
Doubt might beget of diabolic power<br/>
Active within beyond the sense of brute.<br/>
Thus he resolved, but first from inward grief<br/>
His bursting passion into plaints thus poured:—

"O Earth, how like to Heaven, if not preferred<br/>
More justly, seat worthier of Gods, as built<br/>
With second thoughts, reforming what was old!<br/>
For what God, after better, worse would build?<br/>
Terrestrial Heaven, danced round by other Heavens,<br/>
That shine, yet bear their bright officious lamps,<br/>
Light above light, for thee alone, as seems,<br/>
In thee concentring all their precious beams<br/>
Of sacred influence! As God in Heaven<br/>
Is centre, yet extends to all, so thou<br/>
Centring receiv'st from all those orbs; in thee,<br/>
Not in themselves, all their known virtue appears,<br/>
Productive in herb, plant, and nobler birth<br/>
Of creatures animate with gradual life<br/>
Of growth, sense, reason, all summed up in Man.<br/>
With what delight could I have walked thee round,<br/>
If I could joy in aught-sweet interchange<br/>
Of hill and valley, rivers, woods, and plains,<br/>
Now land, now sea, and shores with forest crowned,<br/>
Rocks, dens, and caves! But I in none of these<br/>
Find place or refuge; and the more I see<br/>
Pleasures about me, so much more I feel<br/>
Torment within me, as from the hateful siege<br/>
Of contraries; all good to me becomes<br/>
Bane, and in Heaven much worse would be my state.<br/>
But neither here seek I, nor in Heaven,<br/>
To dwell, unless by maistring Heaven's Supreme;<br/>
Nor hope to be myself less miserable<br/>
By what I seek, but others to make such<br/>
As I, though thereby worse to me redound.<br/>
For only in destroying I find ease<br/>
To my relentless thoughts; and him destroyed,<br/>
Or won to what may work his utter loss,<br/>
For whom all this was made, all this will soon<br/>
Follow, as to him linked in weal or woe:<br/>
In woe then, that destruction wide may range!<br/>
To me shall be the glory sole among<br/>
The Infernal Powers, in one day to have marred<br/>
What he, Almighty styled, six nights and days<br/>
Continued making, and who knows how long<br/>
Before had been contriving? though perhaps<br/>
Not longer than since I in one night freed<br/>
From servitude inglorious well nigh half<br/>
The Angelic Name, and thinner left the throng<br/>
Of his adorers. He, to be avenged,<br/>
And to repair his numbers thus impaired—<br/>
Whether such virtue, spent of old, now failed<br/>
More Angels to create (if they at least<br/>
Are his created), or to spite us more—<br/>
Determined to advance into our room<br/>
A creature formed of earth, and him endow,<br/>
Exalted from so base original,<br/>
With heavenly spoils, our spoils. What he decreed<br/>
He effected; Man he made, and for him built<br/>
Magnificent this World, and Earth his seat,<br/>
Him Lord pronounced, and, O indignity!<br/>
Subjected to his service Angel-wings<br/>
And flaming ministers, to watch and tend<br/>
Their earthly charge. Of these the vigilance<br/>
I dread, and to elude, thus wrapt in mist<br/>
Of midnight vapour, glide obscure, and pry<br/>
In every bush and brake, where hap may find<br/>
The Serpent sleeping, in whose mazy folds<br/>
To hide me, and the dark intent I bring.<br/>
O foul descent! that I, who erst contended<br/>
With Gods to sit the highest, am now constrained<br/>
Into a beast, and, mixed with bestial slime,<br/>
This essence to incarnate and imbrute,<br/>
That to the highth of Deity aspired!<br/>
But what will not ambition and revenge<br/>
Descend to? Who aspires must down as low<br/>
As high he soared, obnoxious, first or last,<br/>
To basest things. Revenge, at first though sweet,<br/>
Bitter ere long back on itself recoils.<br/>
Let it; I reck not, so it light well aimed,<br/>
Since higher I fall short, on him who next<br/>
Provokes my envy, this new favourite<br/>
Of Heaven, this Man of Clay, son of despite,<br/>
Whom, us the more to spite, his Maker raised<br/>
From dust: spite then with spite is best repaid."

So saying, through each thicket, dank or dry,<br/>
Like a black mist low-creeping, he held on<br/>
His midnight search, where soonest he might find<br/>
The Serpent. Him fast sleeping soon he found,<br/>
In labyrinth of many a round self-rowled,<br/>
His head the midst, well stored with subtle wiles:<br/>
Not yet in horrid shade or dismal den:<br/>
Nor nocent yet, but on the grassy herb,<br/>
Fearless, unfeared, he slept. In at his mouth<br/>
The Devil entered, and his brutal sense.<br/>
In heart or head, possessing soon inspired<br/>
With act intelligential; but his sleep<br/>
Disturbed not, waiting close the approach of morn.

Now, whenas sacred light began to dawn<br/>
In Eden on the humid flowers, that breathed<br/>
Their morning incense, when all things that breathe<br/>
From the Earth's great altar send up silent praise<br/>
To the Creator, and his nostrils fill<br/>
With grateful smell, forth came the human pair,<br/>
And joined their vocal worship to the quire<br/>
Of creatures wanting voice; that done, partake<br/>
The season, prime for sweetest scents and airs;<br/>
Then commune how that day they best may ply<br/>
Their growing work-for much their work outgrew<br/>
The hands' dispatch of two gardening so wide:<br/>
And Eve first to her husband thus began:—

"Adam, well may we labour still to dress<br/>
This Garden, still to tend plant, herb, and flower,<br/>
Our pleasant task enjoined; but, till more hands<br/>
Aid us, the work under our labour grows,<br/>
Luxurious by restraint: what we by day<br/>
Lop overgrown, or prune, or prop, or bind,<br/>
One night or two with wanton growth derides,<br/>
Tending to wild. Thou, therefore, now advise,<br/>
Or hear what to my mind first thoughts present.<br/>
Let us divide our labours-thou where choice<br/>
Leads thee, or where most needs, whether to wind<br/>
The woodbine round this arbour, or direct<br/>
The clasping ivy where to climb; while I<br/>
In yonder spring of roses intermixed<br/>
With myrtle find what to redress till noon.<br/>
For, while so near each other thus all day<br/>
Our task we choose, what wonder if so near<br/>
Looks intervene and smiles, or objects new<br/>
Casual discourse draw on, which intermits<br/>
Our day's work, brought to little, though begun<br/>
Early, and the hour of supper comes unearned!"

To whom mild answer Adam thus returned:—<br/>
"Sole Eve, associate sole, to me beyond<br/>
Compare above all living creatures dear!<br/>
Well hast thou motioned, well thy thoughts imployed<br/>
How we might best fulfil the work which here<br/>
God hath assigned us, nor of me shalt pass<br/>
Unpraised; for nothing lovelier can be found<br/>
In woman than to study household good,<br/>
And good works in her husband to promote.<br/>
Yet not so strictly hath our Lord imposed<br/>
Labour as to debar us when we need<br/>
Refreshment, whether food, or talk between,<br/>
Food of the mind, or this sweet intercourse<br/>
Of looks and smiles; for smiles from reason flow<br/>
To brute denied, and are of love the food—<br/>
Love, not the lowest end of human life.<br/>
For not to irksome toil, but to delight,<br/>
He made us, and delight to reason joined.<br/>
These paths and bowers doubt not but our joint hands<br/>
Will keep from wilderness with ease, as wide<br/>
As we need walk, till younger hands ere long<br/>
Assist us. But, if much converse perhaps<br/>
Thee satiate, to short absence I could yield;<br/>
For solitude sometimes is best society,<br/>
And short retirement urges sweet return.<br/>
But other doubt possesses me, lest harm<br/>
Befall thee, severed from me; for thou know'st<br/>
What hath been warned us-what malicious foe,<br/>
Envying our happiness, and of his own<br/>
Despairing, seeks to work us woe and shame<br/>
By sly assault and somewhere nigh at hand<br/>
Watches, no doubt, with greedy hope to find<br/>
His wish and best advantage, us asunder,<br/>
Hopeless to circumvent us joined, where each<br/>
To other speedy aid might lend at need.<br/>
Whether his first design be to withdraw<br/>
Our fealty from God, or to disturb<br/>
Conjugal love-than which perhaps no bliss<br/>
Enjoyed by us excites his envy more—<br/>
Or this, or worse, leave not the faithful side<br/>
That gave thee being, still shades thee and protects.<br/>
The wife, where danger or dishonour lurks,<br/>
Safest and seemliest by her husband stays,<br/>
Who guards her, or with her the worst endures."

To whom the virgin majesty of Eve,<br/>
As one who loves, and some unkindness meets,<br/>
With sweet austere composure thus replied:—

"Offspring of Heaven and Earth, and all Earth's lord!<br/>
That such an Enemy we have, who seeks<br/>
Our ruin, both by thee informed I learn,<br/>
And from the parting Angel overheard,<br/>
As in a shady nook I stood behind,<br/>
Just then returned at shut of evening flowers.<br/>
But that thou shouldst my firmness therefore doubt<br/>
To God or thee, because we have a foe<br/>
May tempt it, I expected not to hear.<br/>
His violence thou fear'st not, being such<br/>
As we, not capable of death or pain,<br/>
Can either not receive, or can repel.<br/>
His fraud is, then, thy fear; which plain infers<br/>
Thy equal fear that my firm faith and love<br/>
Can by his fraud be shaken or seduced:<br/>
Thoughts, which how found they harbour in thy breast,<br/>
Adam! misthought of her to thee so dear?"

To whom, with healing words, Adam replied:—<br/>
"Daughter of God and Man, immortal Eve!—<br/>
For such thou art, from sin and blame entire—<br/>
Not diffident of thee do I dissuade<br/>
Thy absence from my sight, but to avoid<br/>
The attempt itself, intended by our Foe.<br/>
For he who tempts, though in vain, at least asperses<br/>
The tempted with dishonour foul, supposed<br/>
Not incorruptible of faith, not proof<br/>
Against temptation. Thou thyself with scorn<br/>
And anger wouldst resent the offered wrong,<br/>
Though ineffectual found; misdeem not, then,<br/>
If such affront I labour to avert<br/>
From thee alone, which on us both at once<br/>
The enemy, though bold, will hardly dare;<br/>
Or, daring, first on me the assault shall light.<br/>
Nor thou his malice and false guile contemn—<br/>
Subtle he needs must be who could seduce<br/>
Angels-nor think superfluous others' aid.<br/>
I from the influence of thy looks receive<br/>
Access in every virtue-in thy sight<br/>
More wise, more watchful, stronger, if need were<br/>
Of outward strength; while shame, thou looking on,<br/>
Shame to be overcome or overreached,<br/>
Would utmost vigour raise, and raised unite.<br/>
Why shouldst not thou like sense within thee feel<br/>
When I am present, and thy trial choose<br/>
With me, best witness of thy virtue tried?"

So spake domestic Adam in his care<br/>
And matrimonial love; but Eve, who thought<br/>
Less attributed to her faith sincere,<br/>
Thus her reply with accent sweet renewed:—

"If this be our condition, thus to dwell<br/>
In narrow circuit straitened by a Foe,<br/>
Subtle or violent, we not endued<br/>
Single with like defence wherever met,<br/>
How are we happy, still in fear of harm?<br/>
But harm precedes not sin: only our Foe<br/>
Tempting affronts us with his foul esteem<br/>
Of our integrity: his foul esteem<br/>
Sticks no dishonour on our front, but turns<br/>
Foul on himself; then wherefore shunned or feared<br/>
By us, who rather double honour gain<br/>
From his surmise proved false, find peace within,<br/>
Favour from Heaven, our witness, from the event?<br/>
And what is faith, love, virtue, unassayed<br/>
Alone, without exterior help sustained?<br/>
Let us not then suspect our happy state<br/>
Left so imperfet by the Maker wise<br/>
As not secure to single or combined.<br/>
Frail is our happiness, if this be so;<br/>
And Eden were no Eden, thus exposed."

To whom thus Adam fervently replied:—<br/>
"O Woman, best are all things as the will<br/>
Of God ordained them; his creating hand<br/>
Nothing imperfet or deficient left<br/>
Of all that he created-much less Man,<br/>
Or aught that might his happy state secure,<br/>
Secure from outward force. Within himself<br/>
The danger lies, yet lies within his power;<br/>
Against his will he can receive no harm.<br/>
But God left free the Will; for what obeys<br/>
Reason is free; and Reason he made right,<br/>
But bid her well beware, and still erect,<br/>
Lest, by some fair appearing good surprised,<br/>
She dictate false, and misinform the Will<br/>
To do what God expressly hath forbid.<br/>
Not then mistrust, but tender love, enjoins<br/>
That I should mind thee oft; and mind thou me,<br/>
Firm we subsist, yet possible to swerve,<br/>
Since Reason not impossibly may meet<br/>
Some specious object by the foe suborned,<br/>
And fall into deception unaware,<br/>
Not keeping strictest watch, as she was warned.<br/>
Seek not temptation, then, which to avoid<br/>
Were better, and most likely if from me<br/>
Thou sever not: trial will come unsought.<br/>
Wouldst thou approve thy constancy, approve<br/>
First thy obedience; the other who can know,<br/>
Not seeing thee attempted, who attest?<br/>
But, if thou think trial unsought may find<br/>
Us both securer than thus warned thou seem'st,<br/>
Go; for thy stay, not free, absents thee more.<br/>
Go in thy native innocence; rely<br/>
On what thou hast of virtue; summon all;<br/>
For God towards thee hath done his part: do thine."

So spake the Patriarch of Mankind; but Eve<br/>
Persisted; yet submiss, though last, replied:—

"With thy permission, then, and thus forewarned,<br/>
Chiefly by what thy own last reasoning words<br/>
Touched only, that our trial, when least sought,<br/>
May find us both perhaps far less prepared,<br/>
The willinger I go, nor much expect<br/>
A Foe so proud will first the weaker seek;<br/>
So bent, the more shall shame him his repulse."

Thus saying, from her husband's hand her hand<br/>
Soft she withdrew, and, like a wood-nymph light,<br/>
Oread or Dryad, or of Delia's train,<br/>
Betook her to the groves, but Delia's self<br/>
In gait surpassed and goddess-like deport,<br/>
Though not as she with bow and quiver armed,<br/>
But with such gardening tools as Art, yet rude,<br/>
Guiltless of fire had formed, or Angels brought.<br/>
To Pales, or Pomona, thus adorned,<br/>
Likest she seemed-Pomona when she fled<br/>
Vertumnus-or to Ceres in her prime,<br/>
Yet virgin of Proserpina from Jove.<br/>
Her long with ardent look his eye pursued<br/>
Delighted, but desiring more her stay.<br/>
Oft he to her his charge of quick return<br/>
Repeated; she to him as oft engaged<br/>
To be returned by noon amid the bower,<br/>
And all things in best order to invite<br/>
Noontide repast, or afternoon's repose.<br/>
O much deceived, much failing, hapless Eve,<br/>
Of thy presumed return! event perverse!<br/>
Thou never from that hour in Paradise<br/>
Found'st either sweet repast or sound repose;<br/>
Such ambush, hid among sweet flowers and shades,<br/>
Waited, with hellish rancour imminent,<br/>
To intercept thy way, or send thee back<br/>
Despoiled of innocence, of faith, of bliss.<br/>
For now, and since first break of dawn, the Fiend,<br/>
Mere Serpent in appearance, forth was come,<br/>
And on his quest where likeliest he might find<br/>
The only two of mankind, but in them<br/>
The whole included race, his purposed prey.<br/>
In bower and field he sought, where any tuft<br/>
Of grove or garden-plot more pleasant lay,<br/>
Their tendance or plantation for delight;<br/>
By fountain or by shady rivulet<br/>
He sought them both, but wished his hap might find<br/>
Eve separate; he wished, but not with hope<br/>
Of what so seldom chanced, when to his wish,<br/>
Beyond his hope, Eve separate he spies,<br/>
Veiled in a cloud of fragrance, where she stood,<br/>
Half-spied, so thick the roses bushing round<br/>
About her glowed, oft stooping to support<br/>
Each flower of tender stalk, whose head, though gay<br/>
Carnation, purple, azure, or specked with gold,<br/>
Hung drooping unsustained. Them she upstays<br/>
Gently with myrtle band, mindless the while<br/>
Herself, though fairest unsupported flower,<br/>
From her best prop so far, and storm so nigh.<br/>
Nearer he drew, and many a walk traversed<br/>
Of stateliest covert, cedar, pine, or palm;<br/>
Then voluble and bold, now hid, now seen<br/>
Among thick-woven arborets, and flowers<br/>
Imbordered on each bank, the hand of Eve:<br/>
Spot more delicious than those gardens feigned<br/>
Or of revived Adonis, or renowned<br/>
Alcinous, host of old Laertes' son,<br/>
Or that, not mystic, where the sapient king<br/>
Held dalliance with his fair Egyptian spouse.<br/>
Much he the place admired, the person more.<br/>
As one who, long in populous city pent,<br/>
Where houses thick and sewers annoy the air,<br/>
Forth issuing on a summer's morn, to breathe<br/>
Among the pleasant villages and farms<br/>
Adjoined, from each thing met conceives delight—<br/>
The smell of grain, or tedded grass, or kine,<br/>
Or dairy, each rural sight, each rural sound—<br/>
If chance with nymph-like step fair virgin pass,<br/>
What pleasing seemed for her now pleases more,<br/>
She most, and in her look sums all delight:<br/>
Such pleasure took the Serpent to behold<br/>
This flowery plat, the sweet recess of Eve<br/>
Thus early, thus alone. Her heavenly form<br/>
Angelic, but more soft and feminine,<br/>
Her graceful innocence, her every air<br/>
Of gesture or least action, overawed<br/>
His malice, and with rapine sweet bereaved<br/>
His fierceness of the fierce intent it brought.<br/>
That space the Evil One abstracted stood<br/>
From his own evil, and for the time remained<br/>
Stupidly good, of enmity disarmed,<br/>
Of guile, of hate, of envy, of revenge.<br/>
But the hot hell that always in him burns,<br/>
Though in mid Heaven, soon ended his delight,<br/>
And tortures him now more, the more he sees<br/>
Of pleasure not for him ordained. Then soon<br/>
Fierce hate he recollects, and all his thoughts<br/>
Of mischief, gratulating, thus excites:—

"Thoughts, whither have ye led me? with what sweet<br/>
Compulsion thus transported to forget<br/>
What hither brought us? hate, not love, nor hope<br/>
Of Paradise for Hell, here to taste<br/>
Of pleasure, but all pleasure to destroy,<br/>
Save what is in destroying; other joy<br/>
To me is lost. Then let me not let pass<br/>
Occasion which now smiles. Behold alone<br/>
The Woman, opportune to all attempts—<br/>
Her husband, for I view far round, not nigh,<br/>
Whose higher intellectual more I shun,<br/>
And strength, of courage haughty, and of limb<br/>
Heroic built, though of terrestrial mould;<br/>
Foe not informidable, exempt from wound—<br/>
I not; so much hath Hell debased, and pain<br/>
Infeebled me, to what I was in Heaven.<br/>
She fair, divinely fair, fit love for Gods,<br/>
Not terrible, though terror be in love,<br/>
And beauty, not approached by stronger hate,<br/>
Hate stronger under show of love well feigned—<br/>
The way which to her ruin now I tend."

So spake the Enemy of Mankind, enclosed<br/>
In serpent, inmate bad, and toward Eve<br/>
Addressed his way-not with indented wave,<br/>
Prone on the ground, as since, but on his rear,<br/>
Circular base of rising folds, that towered<br/>
Fold above fold, a surging maze; his head<br/>
Crested aloft, and carbuncle his eyes;<br/>
With burnished neck of verdant gold, erect<br/>
Amidst his circling spires, that on the grass<br/>
Floated redundant. Pleasing was his shape<br/>
And lovely; never since the serpent kind<br/>
Lovelier-not those that in Illyria changed<br/>
Hermione and Cadmus, or the God<br/>
In Epidaurus; nor to which transformed<br/>
Ammonian Jove, or Capitoline, was seen,<br/>
He with Olympias, this with her who bore<br/>
Scipio, the highth of Rome. With tract oblique<br/>
At first, as one who sought access but feared<br/>
To interrupt, sidelong he works his way.<br/>
As when a ship, by skilful steersman wrought<br/>
Nigh river's mouth or foreland, where the wind<br/>
Veers oft, as oft so steers, and shifts her sail,<br/>
So varied he, and of his tortuous train<br/>
Curled many a wanton wreath in sight of Eve,<br/>
To lure her eye. She, busied, heard the sound<br/>
Of rustling leaves, but minded not, as used<br/>
To such disport before her through the field<br/>
From every beast, more duteous at her call<br/>
Than at Circean call the herd disguised.<br/>
He, bolder now, uncalled before her stood,<br/>
But as in gaze admiring. Oft he bowed<br/>
His turret crest and sleek enamelled neck,<br/>
Fawning, and licked the ground whereon she trod.<br/>
His gentle dumb expression turned at length<br/>
The eye of Eve to mark his play; he, glad<br/>
Of her attention gained, with serpent-tongue<br/>
Organic, or impulse of vocal air,<br/>
His fraudulent temptation thus began:—

"Wonder not, sovran mistress (if perhaps<br/>
Thou canst who art sole wonder), much less arm<br/>
Thy looks, the heaven of mildness, with disdain,<br/>
Displeased that I approach thee thus, and gaze<br/>
Insatiate, I thus single, nor have feared<br/>
Thy awful brow, more awful thus retired.<br/>
Fairest resemblance of thy Maker fair,<br/>
Thee all things living gaze on, all things thine<br/>
By gift, and thy celestial beauty adore,<br/>
With ravishment beheld-there best beheld<br/>
Where universally admired. But here,<br/>
In this enclosure wild, these beasts among,<br/>
Beholders rude, and shallow to discern<br/>
Half what in thee is fair, one man except,<br/>
Who sees thee (and what is one?) who shouldst be seen<br/>
A Goddess among Gods, adored and served<br/>
By Angels numberless, thy daily train?"

So glozed the Tempter, and his proem tuned.<br/>
Into the heart of Eve his words made way,<br/>
Though at the voice much marvelling; at length,<br/>
Not unamazed, she thus in answer spake:—

"What may this mean? Language of Man pronounced<br/>
By tongue of brute, and human sense expressed!<br/>
The first at least of these I thought denied<br/>
To beasts, whom God on their creation-day<br/>
Created mute to all articulate sound;<br/>
The latter I demur, for in their looks<br/>
Much reason, and in their actions, oft appears.<br/>
Thee, Serpent, subtlest beast of all the field<br/>
I knew, but not with human voice endued;<br/>
Redouble, then, this miracle, and say,<br/>
How cam'st thou speakable of mute, and how<br/>
To me so friendly grown above the rest<br/>
Of brutal kind that daily are in sight:<br/>
Say, for such wonder claims attention due."

To whom the guileful Tempter thus replied:—<br/>
"Empress of this fair World, resplendent Eve!<br/>
Easy to me it is to tell thee all<br/>
What thou command'st, and right thou shouldst be obeyed.<br/>
I was at first as other beasts that graze<br/>
The trodden herb, of abject thoughts and low,<br/>
As was my food, nor aught but food discerned<br/>
Or sex, and apprehended nothing high:<br/>
Till on a day, roving the field, I chanced<br/>
A goodly tree far distant to behold,<br/>
Loaden with fruit of fairest colours mixed,<br/>
Ruddy and gold. In nearer drew to gaze;<br/>
When from the boughs a savoury odour blown,<br/>
Grateful to appetite, more pleased my sense<br/>
Than smell of sweetest fennel, or the teats<br/>
Of ewe or goat dropping with milk at even,<br/>
Unsucked of lamb or kid, that tend their play.<br/>
To satisfy the sharp desire I had<br/>
Of tasting those fair Apples, I resolved<br/>
Not to defer; hunger and thirst at once,<br/>
Powerful persuaders, quickened at the scent<br/>
Of that alluring fruit, urged me so keen.<br/>
About the mossy trunk I wound me soon;<br/>
For, high from ground, the branches would require<br/>
Thy utmost reach, or Adam's; round the Tree<br/>
All other beasts that saw, with like desire<br/>
Longing and envying stood, but could not reach.<br/>
Amid the tree now got, where plenty hung<br/>
Tempting so nigh, to pluck and eat my fill<br/>
I spared not; for such pleasure till that hour<br/>
At feed or fountain never had I found.<br/>
Sated at length, ere long I might perceive<br/>
Strange alteration in me, to degree<br/>
Of Reason in my inward powers, and Speech<br/>
Wanted not long, though to this shape retained.<br/>
Thenceforth to speculations high or deep<br/>
I turned my thoughts, and with capacious mind<br/>
Considered all things visible in Heaven,<br/>
Or Earth, or Middle, all things fair and good.<br/>
But all that fair and good in thy Divine<br/>
Semblance, and in thy beauty's heavenly ray,<br/>
United I beheld-no fair to thine<br/>
Equivalent or second; which compelled<br/>
Me thus, though importune perhaps, to come<br/>
And gaze, and worship thee of right declared<br/>
Sovran of creatures, universal Dame!"

So talked the spirited sly Snake; and Eve,<br/>
Yet more amazed, unwary thus replied:—

"Serpent, thy overpraising leaves in doubt<br/>
The virtue of that Fruit, in thee first proved.<br/>
But say, where grows the Tree? from hence how far?<br/>
For many are the trees of God that grow<br/>
In Paradise, and various, yet unknown<br/>
To us; in such abundance lies our choice<br/>
As leaves a greater store of fruit untouched,<br/>
Still hanging incorruptible, till men<br/>
Grow up to their provision, and more hands<br/>
Help to disburden Nature of her bearth."

To whom the wily Adder, blithe and glad;—<br/>
"Empress, the way is ready, and not long—<br/>
Beyond a row of myrtles, on a flat,<br/>
Fast by a fountain, one small thicket past<br/>
Of blowing myrrh and balm. If thou accept<br/>
My conduct, I can bring thee thither soon."

"Lead, then," said Eve. He, leading, swiftly rowled<br/>
In tangles, and made intricate seem straight,<br/>
To mischief swift. Hope elevates, and joy<br/>
Brightens his crest. As when a wandering fire,<br/>
Compact of unctuous vapour, which the night<br/>
Condenses, and the cold invirons round,<br/>
Kindled through agitation to a flame<br/>
(Which oft, they say, some evil Spirit attends),<br/>
Hovering and blazing with delusive light,<br/>
Misleads the amazed night-wanderer from his way<br/>
To bogs and mires, and oft through pond or pool,<br/>
There swallowed up and lost, from succour far:<br/>
So glistered the dire Snake, and into fraud<br/>
Led Eve, our credulous mother, to the Tree<br/>
Of Prohibition, root of all our woe;<br/>
Which when she saw, thus to her guide she spake:—

"Serpent, we might have spared our coming hither,<br/>
Fruitless to me, though fruit be here to excess,<br/>
The credit of whose virtue rest with thee—<br/>
Wondrous, indeed, if cause of such effects!<br/>
But of this tree we may not taste nor touch;<br/>
God so commanded, and left that command<br/>
Sole daughter of his voice: the rest, we live<br/>
Law to ourselves; our Reason is our Law."

To whom the Tempter guilefully replied:—<br/>
"Indeed! Hath God then said that of the fruit<br/>
Of all these garden-trees ye shall not eat,<br/>
Yet lords declared of all in Earth or Air?"

To whom thus Eve, yet sinless:—"Of the fruit<br/>
Of each tree in the garden we may eat;<br/>
But of the fruit of this fair Tree, amidst<br/>
The Garden, God hath said, 'Ye shall not eat<br/>
Thereof, nor shall ye touch it, lest ye die.'"

She scarce had said, though brief, when now more bold<br/>
The Tempter, but, with shew of zeal and love<br/>
To Man, and indignation at his wrong,<br/>
New part puts on, and, as to passion moved,<br/>
Fluctuates disturbed, yet comely, and in act<br/>
Raised, as of some great matter to begin.<br/>
As when of old some orator renowned<br/>
In Athens or free Rome, where eloquence<br/>
Flourished, since mute, to some great cause addressed,<br/>
Stood in himself collected, while each part,<br/>
Motion, each act, won audience ere the tongue<br/>
Sometimes in highth began, as no delay<br/>
Of preface brooking through his zeal of right:<br/>
So standing, moving, or to highth upgrown,<br/>
The Tempter, all impassioned, thus began:—<br/>
"O sacred, wise, and wisdom-giving Plant,<br/>
Mother of science! now I feel thy power<br/>
Within me clear, not only to discern<br/>
Things in their causes, but to trace the ways<br/>
Of highest agents, deemed however wise.<br/>
Queen of this Universe! do not believe<br/>
Those rigid threats of death. Ye shall not die.<br/>
How should ye? By the Fruit? it gives you life<br/>
To knowledge. By the Threatener? look on me,<br/>
Me who have touched and tasted, yet both live,<br/>
And life more perfect have attained than Fate<br/>
Meant me, by venturing higher than my lot.<br/>
Shall that be shut to Man which to the Beast<br/>
Is open? or will God incense his ire<br/>
For such a petty trespass, and not praise<br/>
Rather your dauntless virtue, whom the pain<br/>
Of death denounced, whatever thing Death be,<br/>
Deterred not from achieving what might lead<br/>
To happier life, knowledge of Good and Evil?<br/>
Of good, how just! of evil-if what is evil<br/>
Be real, why not known, since easier shunned?<br/>
God, therefore, cannot hurt ye and be just;<br/>
Not just, not God; not feared then, nor obeyed:<br/>
Your fear itself of death removes the fear.<br/>
Why, then, was this forbid? Why but to awe,<br/>
Why but to keep ye low and ignorant,<br/>
His worshipers? He knows that in the day<br/>
Ye eat thereof your eyes, that seem so clear,<br/>
Yet are but dim, shall perfectly be then<br/>
Opened and cleared, and ye shall be as Gods,<br/>
Knowing both good and evil, as they know.<br/>
That ye should be as Gods, since I as Man,<br/>
Internal Man, is but proportion meet—<br/>
I, of brute, human; ye, of human, Gods.<br/>
So ye shall die perhaps, by putting off<br/>
Human, to put on Gods-death to be wished,<br/>
Though threatened, which no worse than this can bring!<br/>
And what are Gods, that Man may not become<br/>
As they, participating godlike food?<br/>
The Gods are first, and that advantage use<br/>
On our belief, that all from them proceeds.<br/>
I question it; for this fair Earth I see,<br/>
Warmed by the Sun, producing every kind;<br/>
Them nothing. If they all things, who enclosed<br/>
Knowledge of Good and Evil in this Tree,<br/>
That whoso eats thereof forthwith attains<br/>
Wisdom without their leave? and wherein lies<br/>
The offence, that Man should thus attain to know?<br/>
What can your knowledge hurt him, or this Tree<br/>
Impart against his will, if all be his?<br/>
Or is it envy? and can envy dwell<br/>
In Heavenly breasts? These, these and many more<br/>
Causes import your need of this fair Fruit.<br/>
Goddess humane, reach, then, and freely taste!"

He ended; and his words, replete with guile,<br/>
Into her heart too easy entrance won.<br/>
Fixed on the Fruit she gazed, which to behold<br/>
Might tempt alone; and in her ears the sound<br/>
Yet rung of his persuasive words, impregned<br/>
With reason, to her seeming, and with truth.<br/>
Meanwhile the hour of noon drew on, and waked<br/>
An eager appetite, raised by the smell<br/>
So savoury of that Fruit, which with desire,<br/>
Inclinable now grown to touch or taste,<br/>
Solicited her longing eye; yet first,<br/>
Pausing a while, thus to herself she mused:—

"Great are thy virtues, doubtless, best of Fruits,<br/>
Though kept from Man, and worthy to be admired,<br/>
Whose taste, too long forborne, at first assay<br/>
Gave elocution to the mute, and taught<br/>
The tongue not made for speech to speak thy praise.<br/>
Thy praise he also who forbids thy use<br/>
Conceals not from us, naming thee the Tree<br/>
Of Knowledge, knowledge both of Good and Evil;<br/>
Forbids us then to taste. But his forbidding<br/>
Commends thee more, while it infers the good<br/>
By thee communicated, and our want;<br/>
For good unknown sure is not bad, or, had<br/>
And yet unknown, is as not had at all.<br/>
In plain, then, what forbids he but to know?<br/>
Forbids us good, forbids us to be wise!<br/>
Such prohibitions bind not. But, if Death<br/>
Bind us with after-bands, what profits then<br/>
Our inward freedom? In the day we eat<br/>
Of this fair Fruit, our doom is we shall die!<br/>
How dies the Serpent? He hath eaten, and lives,<br/>
And knows, and speaks, and reasons, and discerns,<br/>
Irrational till then. For us alone<br/>
Was death invented? or to us denied<br/>
This intellectual food, for beasts reserved?<br/>
For beasts it seems; yet that one beast which first<br/>
Hath tasted envies not, but brings with joy<br/>
The good befallen him, author unsuspect,<br/>
Friendly to Man, far from deceit or guile.<br/>
What fear I, then? rather, what know to fear<br/>
Under this ignorance of Good and Evil,<br/>
Of God or Death, of law or penalty?<br/>
Here grows the cure of all, this fruit divine,<br/>
Fair to the eye, inviting to the taste,<br/>
Of virtue to make wise. What hinders, then,<br/>
To reach, and feed at once both body and mind?"

So saying, her rash hand in evil hour<br/>
Forth-reaching to the Fruit, she plucked, she eat.<br/>
Earth felt the wound, and Nature from her seat,<br/>
Sighing through all her works, gave signs of woe<br/>
That all was lost. Back to the thicket slunk<br/>
The guilty Serpent, and well might, for Eve,<br/>
Intent now only her taste, naught else<br/>
Regarded; such delight till then, as seemed,<br/>
In fruit she never tasted, whether true,<br/>
Or fancied so through expectation high<br/>
Of knowledge; nor was Godhead from her thought.<br/>
Greedily she ingorged without restraint,<br/>
And knew not eating death. Satiate at length,<br/>
And hightened as with wine, jocond and boon,<br/>
Thus to herself she pleasingly began:—

"O sovran, virtuous, precious of all trees<br/>
In Paradise! of operation blest<br/>
To sapience, hitherto obscured, infamed,<br/>
And thy fair Fruit let hang, as to no end<br/>
Created! but henceforth my early care,<br/>
Not without song, each morning, and due praise,<br/>
Shall tend thee, and tde fertil burden ease<br/>
Of thy full branches, offered free to all;<br/>
Till, dieted by thee, I grow mature<br/>
In knowledge, as the Gods who all things know,<br/>
Though others envy what they cannot give—<br/>
For, had the gift been theirs, it had not here<br/>
Thus grown! Experience, next to thee I owe,<br/>
Best guide: not following thee, I had remained<br/>
In ignorance; thou open'st Wisdom's way,<br/>
And giv'st access, though secret she retire.<br/>
And I perhaps am secret: Heaven is high—<br/>
High, and remote to see from thence distinct<br/>
Each thing on Earth; and other care perhaps<br/>
May have diverted from continual watch<br/>
Our great Forbidder, safe with all his Spies<br/>
About him. But to Adam in what sort<br/>
Shall I appear? Shall I to him make known<br/>
As yet my change, and give him to partake<br/>
Full happiness with me, or rather not,<br/>
But keep the odds of knowledge in my power<br/>
Without copartner? so to add what wants<br/>
In female sex, the more to draw his love,<br/>
And render me more equal, and perhaps—<br/>
A thing not undesirable-sometime<br/>
Superior; for, inferior, who is free?<br/>
This may be well; but what if God have seen,<br/>
And death ensue? Then I shall be no more;<br/>
And Adam, wedded to another Eve,<br/>
Shall live with her enjoying, I extinct!<br/>
A death to think! Confirmed, then, I resolve<br/>
Adam shall share with me in bliss or woe.<br/>
So dear I love him that with him all deaths<br/>
I could endure, without him live no life."

So saying, from the Tree her step she turned,<br/>
But first low reverence done, as to the Power<br/>
That dwelt within, whose presence had infused<br/>
Into the plant sciential sap, derived<br/>
From nectar, drink of Gods. Adam the while,<br/>
Waiting desirous her return, had wove<br/>
Of choicest flowers a garland, to adorn<br/>
Her tresses, and her rural labours crown,<br/>
As reapers oft are wont their harvest-queen.<br/>
Great joy he promised to his thoughts, and new<br/>
Solace in her return, so long delayed;<br/>
Yet oft his heart, divine of something ill,<br/>
Misgave him. He the faltering measure felt,<br/>
And forth to meet her went, the way she took<br/>
That morn when first they parted. By the Tree<br/>
Of Knowledge he must pass; there he her met,<br/>
Scarce from the Tree returning; in her hand<br/>
A bough of fairest fruit, that downy smiled,<br/>
New gathered, and ambrosial smell diffused.<br/>
To him she hasted; in her face excuse<br/>
Came prologue, and apology to prompt,<br/>
Which, with bland words at will, she thus addressed:—

"Hast thou not wondered, Adam, at my stay?<br/>
Thee I have missed, and thought it long, deprived<br/>
Thy presence-agony of love till now<br/>
Not felt, nor shall be twice; for never more<br/>
Mean I to try, what rash untried I sought,<br/>
The pain of absence from thy sight. But strange<br/>
Hath been the cause, and wonderful to hear.<br/>
This Tree is not, as we are told, a Tree<br/>
Of danger tasted, nor to evil unknown<br/>
Opening the way, but of divine effect<br/>
To open eyes, and make them Gods who taste;<br/>
And hath been tasted such. The Serpent wise,<br/>
Or not restrained as we, or not obeying,<br/>
Hath eaten of the Fruit, and is become<br/>
Not dead, as we are threatened, but thenceforth<br/>
Endued with human voice and human sense,<br/>
Reasoning to admiration, and with me<br/>
Persuasively hath so prevailed that I<br/>
Have also tasted, and have also found<br/>
The effects to correspond-opener mine eyes,<br/>
Dim erst, dilated spirits, ampler heart,<br/>
And growing up to Godhead; which for thee<br/>
Chiefly I sought, without thee can despise.<br/>
For bliss, as thou hast part, to me is bliss;<br/>
Tedious, unshared with thee, and odious soon.<br/>
Thou, therefore, also taste, that equal lot<br/>
May join us, equal joy, as equal love;<br/>
Lest, thou not tasting, different degree<br/>
Disjoin us, and I then too late renounce<br/>
Deity for thee, when fate will not permit."

Thus Eve with countenance blithe her story told;<br/>
But in her cheek distemper flushing glowed.<br/>
On the other side, Adam, soon as he heard<br/>
The fatal trespass done by Eve, amazed,<br/>
Astonied stood and blank, while horror chill<br/>
Ran through his veins, and all his joints relaxed.<br/>
From his slack hand the garland wreathed for Eve<br/>
Down dropt, and all the faded roses shed.<br/>
Speechless he stood and pale, till thus at length<br/>
First to himself he inward silence broke:—

"O fairest of Creation, last and best<br/>
Of all God's works, creature in whom excelled<br/>
Whatever can to sight or thought be formed,<br/>
Holy, divine, good, amiable, or sweet!<br/>
How art thou lost! how on a sudden lost,<br/>
Defaced, deflowered, and now to death devote!<br/>
Rather, how hast thou yielded to transgress<br/>
The strict forbiddance, how to violate<br/>
The sacred Fruit forbidden? Some cursed fraud<br/>
Of enemy hath beguiled thee, yet unknown,<br/>
And me with thee hath ruined; for with thee<br/>
Certain my resolution is to die.<br/>
How can I live without thee? how forgo<br/>
Thy sweet converse, and love so dearly joined,<br/>
To live again in these wild woods forlorn?<br/>
Should God create another Eve, and I<br/>
Another rib afford, yet loss of thee<br/>
Would never from my heart. No, no! I feel<br/>
The link of nature draw me: flesh of flesh,<br/>
Bone of my bone thou art, and from thy state<br/>
Mine never shall be parted, bliss or woe."

So having said, as one from sad dismay<br/>
Recomforted, and, after thoughts disturbed,<br/>
Submitting to what seemed remediless,<br/>
Thus in calm mood his words to Eve he turned:—

"Bold deed thou hast presumed, adventrous Eve,<br/>
And peril great provoked, who thus hast dared<br/>
Had it been only coveting to eye<br/>
That sacred Food, sacred to abstinence;<br/>
Much more to taste it, under ban to touch.<br/>
But past who can recall, or done undo?<br/>
Not God Omnipotent, nor Fate! Yet so<br/>
Perhaps thou shalt not die; perhaps the fact<br/>
Is not so hainous now-foretasted Fruit,<br/>
Profaned first by the Serpent, by him first<br/>
Made common and unhallowed ere our taste,<br/>
Nor yet on him found deadly. He yet lives—<br/>
Lives, as thou saidst, and gains to live, as Man,<br/>
Higher degree of life: inducement strong<br/>
To us, as likely, tasting, to attain<br/>
Proportional ascent; which cannot be<br/>
But to be Gods, or Angels, Demi-gods.<br/>
Nor can I think that God, Creator wise,<br/>
Though threatening, will in earnest so destroy<br/>
Us, his prime creatures, dignified so high,<br/>
Set over all his works; which, in our fall,<br/>
For us created, needs with us must fail,<br/>
Dependent made. So God shall uncreate,<br/>
Be frustrate, do, undo, and labour lose—<br/>
Not well conceived of God; who, though his power<br/>
Creation could repeat, yet would be loth<br/>
Us to abolish, lest the Adversary<br/>
Triumph and say: 'Fickle their state whom God<br/>
Most favours; who can please him long? Me first<br/>
He ruined, now Mankind; whom will he next?'—<br/>
Matter of scorn not to be given the Foe.<br/>
However, I with thee have fixed my lot,<br/>
Certain to undergo like doom. If death<br/>
Consort with thee, death is to me as life;<br/>
So forcible within my heart I feel<br/>
The bond of Nature draw me to my own—<br/>
My own is thee; for what thou art is mine.<br/>
Our state cannot be severed; we are one,<br/>
One flesh; to lose thee were to lose myself."

So Adam; and thus Eve to him replied:—<br/>
"O glorious trial of exceeding love,<br/>
Illustrious evidence, example high!<br/>
Ingaging me to emulate; but, short<br/>
Of thy perfection, how shall I attain,<br/>
Adam? from whose dear side I boast me sprung,<br/>
And gladly of our union hear thee speak,<br/>
One heart, one soul in both; whereof good proof<br/>
This day affords, declaring thee resolved,<br/>
Rather than death, or aught than death more dread,<br/>
Shall separate us, linked in love so dear,<br/>
To undergo with me one guilt, one crime,<br/>
If any be, of tasting this fair Fruit;<br/>
Whose virtue (for of good still good proceeds,<br/>
Direct, or by occasion) hath presented<br/>
This happy trial of thy love, which else<br/>
So eminently never had been known.<br/>
Were it I thought death menaced would ensue<br/>
This my attempt, I would sustain alone<br/>
The worst, and not persuade thee-rather die<br/>
Deserted than oblige thee with a fact<br/>
Pernicious to thy peace, chiefly assured<br/>
Remarkably so late of thy so true,<br/>
So faithful love unequalled. But I feel<br/>
Far otherwise the event-not death, but life<br/>
Augmented, opened eyes, new hopes, new joys,<br/>
Taste so divine that what of sweet before<br/>
Hath touched my sense flat seems to this and harsh.<br/>
On my experience, Adam, freely taste,<br/>
And fear of death deliver to the winds."

So saying, she embraced him, and for joy<br/>
Tenderly wept, much won that he his love<br/>
Had so ennobled as of choice to incur<br/>
Divine displeasure for her sake, or death.<br/>
In recompense (for such compliance bad<br/>
Such recompense best merits), from the bough<br/>
She gave him of that fair enticing Fruit<br/>
With liberal hand. He scrupled not to eat,<br/>
Against his better knowledge, not deceived,<br/>
But fondly overcome with female charm.<br/>
Earth trembled from her entrails, as again<br/>
In pangs, and Nature gave a second groan;<br/>
Sky loured, and, muttering thunder, some sad drops<br/>
Wept at completing of the mortal Sin<br/>
Original; while Adam took no thought,<br/>
Eating his fill, nor Eve to iterate<br/>
Her former trespass feared, the more to soothe<br/>
Him with her loved society; that now,<br/>
As with new wine intoxicated both,<br/>
They swim in mirth, and fancy that they feel<br/>
Divinity within them breeding wings<br/>
Wherewith to scorn the Earth. But that false Fruit<br/>
Far other operation first displayed,<br/>
Carnal desire inflaming. He on Eve<br/>
Began to cast lascivious eyes; she him<br/>
As wantonly repaid; in lust they burn,<br/>
Till Adam thus 'gan Eve to dalliance move:—

"Eve, now I see thou art exact of taste<br/>
And elegant-of sapience no small part;<br/>
Since to each meaning savour we apply,<br/>
And palate call judicious. I the praise<br/>
Yield thee; so well this day thou hast purveyed.<br/>
Much pleasure we have lost, while we abstained<br/>
From this delightful Fruit, nor known till now<br/>
True relish, tasting. If such pleasure be<br/>
In things to us forbidden, it might be wished<br/>
For this one Tree had been forbidden ten.<br/>
But come; so well refreshed, now let us play,<br/>
As meet is, after such delicious fare;<br/>
For never did thy beauty, since the day<br/>
I saw thee first and wedded thee, adorned<br/>
With all perfections, so enflame my sense<br/>
With ardour to enjoy thee, fairer now<br/>
Than ever-bounty of this virtuous Tree!"

So said he, and forbore not glance or toy<br/>
Of amorous intent, well understood<br/>
Of Eve, whose eye darted contagious fire.<br/>
Her hand he seized, and to a shady bank,<br/>
Thick overhead with verdant roof imbowered,<br/>
He led her, nothing loth; flowers were the couch,<br/>
Pansies, and violets, and asphodel,<br/>
And hyacinth-Earth's freshest, softest lap.<br/>
There they their fill of love and love's disport<br/>
Took largely, of their mutual gilt the seal,<br/>
The solace of their sin, till dewy sleep<br/>
Oppressed them, wearied with their amorous play.

Soon as the force of that fallacious Fruit,<br/>
That with exhilarating vapour bland<br/>
About their spirits han played, and inmost powers<br/>
Made err, was now exhaled, and grosser sleep,<br/>
Bred of unkindly fumes, with conscious dreams<br/>
Incumbered, now had left them, up they rose<br/>
As from unrest, and, each the other viewing,<br/>
Soon found their eyes how opened, and their minds<br/>
How darkened. Innocence, that as a veil<br/>
Had shadowed them from knowing ill, was gone;<br/>
Just confidence, and native righteousness,<br/>
And honour, from about them, naked left<br/>
To guilty Shame: he covered, but his robe<br/>
Uncovered more. So rose the Danite strong,<br/>
Herculean Samson, from the harlot-lap<br/>
Of Philistean Dalilah, and waked<br/>
Shorn of his strength; they destitute and bare<br/>
Of all their virtue. Silent, and in face<br/>
Confounded, long they sat, as strucken mute;<br/>
Till Adam, though not less than Eve abashed,<br/>
At length gave utterance to these words constrained:—

"O Eve, in evil hour thou didst give ear<br/>
To that false Worm, of whomsoever taught<br/>
To counterfeit Man's voice-true in our fall,<br/>
False in our promised rising; since our eyes<br/>
Opened we find indeed, and find we know<br/>
Both good and evil, good lost and evil got:<br/>
Bad Fruit of Knowledge, if this be to know,<br/>
Which leaves us naked thus, of honour void,<br/>
Of innocence, of faith, of purity,<br/>
Our wonted ornaments now soiled and stained,<br/>
And in our faces evident the signs<br/>
Of foul concupiscence; whence evil store,<br/>
Even shame, the last of evils; of the first<br/>
Be sure then. How shall I behold the face<br/>
Henceforth of God or Angel, erst with joy<br/>
And rapture so oft beheld? Those Heavenly Shapes<br/>
Will dazzle now this earthly with their blaze<br/>
Insufferably bright. Oh, might I here<br/>
In solitude live savage, in some glade<br/>
Obscured, where highest woods, impenetrable<br/>
To star or sunlight, spread their umbrage broad,<br/>
And brown as evening. Cover me, ye pines!<br/>
Ye cedars, with innumerable boughs<br/>
Hide me, where I may never see them more!<br/>
But let us now, as in bad plight, devise<br/>
What best may, for the present, serve to hide<br/>
The parts of each other that seem most<br/>
To shame obnoxious, and unseemliest seen—<br/>
Some tree, whose broad smooth leaves, together sewed,<br/>
And girded on our loins, may cover round<br/>
Those middle parts, that this new comer, Shame,<br/>
There sit not, and reproach us as unclean."

So counselled he, and both together went<br/>
Into the thickest wood. There soon they choose<br/>
The fig tree-not that kind for fruit renowned,<br/>
But such, as at this day, to Indians known,<br/>
In Malabar or Decan spreads her arms<br/>
Braunching so broad and long that in the ground<br/>
The bended twigs take root, and daughters grow<br/>
About the mother tree, a pillared shade<br/>
High overarched, and echoing walks between:<br/>
There oft the Indian herdsman, shunning heat,<br/>
Shelters in cool, and tends his pasturing herds<br/>
At loop-holes cut through thickest shade. Those leaves<br/>
They gathered, broad as Amazonian targe,<br/>
And with what skill they had together sewed,<br/>
To gird their waist-vain covering, if to hide<br/>
Their guilt and dreaded shame! O how unlike<br/>
To that first naked glory! Such of late<br/>
Columbus found the American, so girt<br/>
With feathered cincture, naked else and wild,<br/>
Among the trees on isles and woody shores.<br/>
Thus fenced, and, as they thought, their shame in part<br/>
Covered, but not at rest or ease of mind,<br/>
They sat them down to weep. Nor only tears<br/>
Rained at their eyes, but high winds worse within<br/>
Began to rise, high passions-anger, hate,<br/>
Mistrust, suspicion, discord-and shook sore<br/>
Their inward state of mind, calm region once<br/>
And full of peace, now tost and turbulent:<br/>
For Understanding ruled not, and the Will<br/>
Heard not her lore, both in subjection now<br/>
To sensual Appetite, who, from beneath<br/>
Usurping over sovran Reason, claimed<br/>
Superior sway. From thus distempered breast<br/>
Adam, estranged in look and altered style,<br/>
Speech intermitted thus to Eve renewed:—

"Would thou hadst hearkened to my words, and stayed<br/>
With me, as I besought thee, when that strange<br/>
Desire of wandering, this unhappy morn,<br/>
I know not whence possessed thee! We had then<br/>
Remained still happy-not, as now, despoiled<br/>
Of all our good, shamed, naked, miserable!<br/>
Let none henceforth seek needless cause to approve<br/>
The faith they owe; when earnestly they seek<br/>
Such proof, conclude they then begin to fail."

To whom, soon moved with touch of blame, thus Eve:—<br/>
"What words have passed thy lips, Adam severe?<br/>
Imput'st thou that to my default, or will<br/>
Of wandering, as thou call'st it, which who knows<br/>
But might as ill have happened thou being by,<br/>
Or to thyself perhaps? Hadst thou been there,<br/>
Or here the attempt, thou couldst not have discerned<br/>
Fraud in the Serpent, speaking as he spake;<br/>
No ground of enmity between us known<br/>
Why he should mean me ill or seek to harm;<br/>
Was I to have never parted from thy side?<br/>
As good have grown there still, a lifeless rib.<br/>
Being as I am, why didst not thou, the Head,<br/>
Command me absolutely not to go,<br/>
Going into such danger, as thou saidst?<br/>
Too facile then, thou didst not much gainsay,<br/>
Nay, didst permit, approve, and fair dismiss.<br/>
Hadst thou been firm and fixed in thy dissent,<br/>
Neither had I transgressed, nor thou with me."

To whom, then first incensed, Adam replied:—<br/>
"Is this the love, is this the recompense<br/>
Of mine to thee, ingrateful Eve, expressed<br/>
Immutable when thou wert lost, not I—<br/>
Who might have lived, and joyed immortal bliss,<br/>
Yet willingly chose rather death with thee?<br/>
And am I now upbraided as the cause<br/>
Of thy transgressing? not enough severe,<br/>
It seems, in thy restraint! What could I more?<br/>
I warned thee, I admonished thee, foretold<br/>
The danger, and the lurking Enemy<br/>
That lay in wait; beyond this had been force,<br/>
And force upon free will hath here no place.<br/>
But confidence then bore thee on, secure<br/>
Either to meet no danger, or to find<br/>
Matter of glorious trial; and perhaps<br/>
I also erred in overmuch admiring<br/>
What seemed in thee so perfet that I thought<br/>
No evil durst attempt thee, But I rue<br/>
That error now, which is become my crime,<br/>
And thou the accuser. Thus it shall befall<br/>
Him who, to worth in women overtrusting,<br/>
Lets her will rule: restraint she will not brook;<br/>
And, left to herself, if evil thence ensue,<br/>
She first his weak indulgence will accuse."

Thus they in mutual accusation spent<br/>
The fruitless hours, but neither self-condemning;<br/>
And of their vain contest' appeared no end.

<h2>Book 10</h2>

Meanwhile the hainous and despiteful act<br/>
Of Satan done in Paradise, and how<br/>
He, in the Serpent, had perverted Eve,<br/>
Her Husband she, to taste the fatal Fruit,<br/>
Was known in Heaven; for what can scape the eye<br/>
Of God all-seeing, or deceive his heart<br/>
Omniscient? who, in all things wise and just,<br/>
Hindered not Satan to attempt the mind<br/>
Of Man, with strength entire and free will armed<br/>
Complete to have discovered and repulsed<br/>
Whatever wiles of foe or seeming friend.<br/>
For still they knew, and ought to have still remembered,<br/>
The high injunction not to taste that Fruit,<br/>
Whoever tempted; which they not obeying<br/>
Incurred (what could they less?) the penalty,<br/>
And, manifold in sin, deserved to fall.<br/>
Up into Heaven from Paradise in haste<br/>
The Angelic Guards ascended, mute and sad<br/>
For Man; for of his state by this they knew,<br/>
Much wondering how the subtle Fiend had stolen<br/>
Entrance unseen. Soon as the unwelcome news<br/>
From Earth arrived at Heaven-gate, displeased<br/>
All were who heard; dim sadness did not spare<br/>
That time celestial visages, yet, mixed<br/>
With pity, violated not their bliss.<br/>
About the new-arrived in multitudes,<br/>
The Ethereal People ran, to hear and know<br/>
How all befell. They towards the Throne supreme,<br/>
Accountable, made haste, to make appear,<br/>
With righteous plea, their utmost vigilance,<br/>
And easily approved; when the Most High,<br/>
Eternal Father, from his secret Cloud<br/>
Amidst, in thunder uttered thus his voice:—

"Assembled Angels, and ye Powers returned<br/>
From unsuccessful charge, be not dismayed<br/>
Nor troubled at these tidings from the Earth,<br/>
Which your sincerest care could not prevent,<br/>
Foretold so lately what would come to pass,<br/>
When first this Tempter crossed the gulf from Hell.<br/>
I told ye then he should prevail, and speed<br/>
On his bad errand-Man should be seduced,<br/>
And flattered out of all, believing lies<br/>
Against his Maker; no decree of mine,<br/>
Concurring to necessitate his fall,<br/>
Or touch with lightest moment of impulse<br/>
His free will, to her own inclining left<br/>
In even scale. But fallen he is; and now<br/>
What rests, but that the mortal sentence pass<br/>
On his transgression, Death denounced that day<br/>
Which he presumes already vain and void,<br/>
Because not yet inflicted, as he feared,<br/>
By some immediate stroke, but soon shall find<br/>
Forbearance no acquittance ere day end.<br/>
Justice shall not return, as bounty, scorned.<br/>
But whom send I to judge them? whom but thee,<br/>
Vicegerent Son? To thee I have transferred<br/>
All judgment, whether in Heaven, or Earth, or Hell.<br/>
Easy it may be seen that I intend<br/>
Mercy colleague with justice, sending thee,<br/>
Man's Friend, his Mediator, his designed<br/>
Both Ransom and Redeemer voluntary,<br/>
And destined Man himself to judge Man fallen."

So spake the Father; and, unfolding bright<br/>
Toward the right hand his glory, on the Son<br/>
Blazed forth unclouded deity. He full<br/>
Resplendent all his Father manifest<br/>
Expressed, and thus divinely answered mild:—

"Father Eternal, thine is to decree;<br/>
Mine both in Heaven and Earth to do thy will<br/>
Supreme, that thou in me, thy Son beloved,<br/>
May'st ever rest well pleased. I go to judge<br/>
On Earth these thy transgressors; but thou know'st,<br/>
Whoever judged, the worst on me must light,<br/>
When time shall be; for so I undertook<br/>
Before thee, and, not repenting, this obtain<br/>
Of right, that I may mitigate their doom<br/>
On me derived. Yet I shall temper so<br/>
Justice with mercy as may illustrate most<br/>
Them fully satisfied, and thee appease.<br/>
Attendance none shall need, nor train, where none<br/>
Are to behold the judgment but the judged,<br/>
Those two; the third best absent is condemned,<br/>
Convict by flight, and rebel to all law;<br/>
Conviction to the Serpent none belongs."

Thus saying, from his radiant Seat he rose<br/>
Of high collateral glory. Him Thrones and Powers,<br/>
Princedoms, and Dominations ministrant,<br/>
Accompanied to Heaven-gate, from whence<br/>
Eden and all the coast in prospect lay.<br/>
Down he descended straight; the speed of Gods<br/>
Time counts not, though with swiftest minutes winged.

Now was the Sun in western cadence low<br/>
From noon, and gentle airs due at their hour<br/>
To fan the Earth now waked, and usher in<br/>
The evening cool, when he, from wrauth more cool,<br/>
Came, the mild Judge and Intercessor both,<br/>
To sentence Man. The voice of God they heard<br/>
Now walking in the Garden, by soft winds<br/>
Brought to their ears, while day declined; they heard,<br/>
And from his presence hid themselves among<br/>
The thickest trees, both man and wife, till God,<br/>
Approaching, thus to Adam called aloud:—

"Where art thou, Adam, wont with joy to meet<br/>
My coming, seen far off? I miss thee here,<br/>
Not pleased thus entertained, with solitude,<br/>
Where obvious duty erewhile appeared unsought.<br/>
Or come I less conspicuous, or what change<br/>
Absents thee, or what chance detains? Come forth!"

He came, and with him Eve, more loth, though first<br/>
To offend, discountenanced both, and discomposed.<br/>
Love was not in their looks, either to God<br/>
Or to each other, but apparent guilt,<br/>
And shame, and perturbation, and despair,<br/>
Anger, and obstinacy, and hate, and guile.<br/>
Whence Adam, faltering long, thus answered brief:—

"I heard thee in the Garden, and, of thy voice<br/>
Afraid, being naked, hid myself." To whom<br/>
The gracious Judge, without revile, replied:—

"My voice thou oft has heard, and hast not feared,<br/>
But still rejoiced; how is it now become<br/>
So dreadful to thee? That thou art naked who<br/>
Hath told thee? Hast thou eaten of the Tree<br/>
Whereof I gave thee charge thou shouldst not eat?"<br/>
To whom thus Adam, sore beset, replied:—

"O Heaven! in evil strait this day I stand<br/>
Before my Judge-either to undergo<br/>
Myself the total crime, or to accuse<br/>
My other self, the partner of my life,<br/>
Whose failing, while her faith to me remains,<br/>
I should conceal, and not expose to blame<br/>
By my complaint. But strict necessity<br/>
Subdues me, and calamitous constraint,<br/>
Lest on my head both sin and punishment,<br/>
However insupportable, be all<br/>
Devolved; though, should I hold my peace, yet thou<br/>
Wouldst easily detect what I conceal.<br/>
This Woman, whom thou mad'st to be my help,<br/>
And gav'st me as thy perfect gift, so good,<br/>
So fit, so acceptable, so divine,<br/>
That from her hand I could suspect no ill,<br/>
And what she did, whatever in itself,<br/>
Her doing seemed to justify the deed—<br/>
She gave me of the Tree, and I did eat."<br/>
To whom the Sovran Presence thus replied:—

"Was she thy God, that her thou didst obey<br/>
Before his voice? or was she made thy guide,<br/>
Superior, or but equal, that to her<br/>
Thou didst resign thy manhood, and the place<br/>
Wherein God set thee above her, made of thee<br/>
And for thee, whose perfection far excelled<br/>
Hers in all real dignity? Adorned<br/>
She was indeed, and lovely, to attract<br/>
Thy love, not thy subjection; and her gifts<br/>
Were such as under government well seemed—<br/>
Unseemly to bear rule; which was thy part<br/>
And person, hadst thou known thyself aright."

So having said, he thus to Eve in few:—<br/>
"Say, Woman, what is this which thou hast done?"

To whom sad Eve, with shame nigh overwhelmed,<br/>
Confessing soon, yet not before her Judge<br/>
Bold or loquacious, thus abashed replied:—<br/>
"The Serpent me beguiled, and I did eat."

Which when the Lord God heard, without delay<br/>
To judgment he proceeded on the accused<br/>
Serpent, though brute, unable to transfer<br/>
The guilt on him who made him instrument<br/>
Of mischief, and polluted from the end<br/>
Of his creation-justly then accursed,<br/>
As vitiated in nature. More to know<br/>
Concerned not Man (since he no further knew),<br/>
Nor altered his offence; yet God at last<br/>
To Satan, first in sin, his doom applied,<br/>
Though in mysterious terms, judged as then best;<br/>
And on the Serpent thus his curse let fall:—

"Because thou hast done this, thou art accursed<br/>
Above all cattle, each beast of the field;<br/>
Upon thy belly grovelling thou shalt go,<br/>
And dust shalt eat all the days of thy life.<br/>
Between thee and the Woman I will put<br/>
Enmity, and between thine and her seed;<br/>
Her seed shall bruise thy head, thou bruise his heel."

So spake this oracle-then verified<br/>
When Jesus, son of Mary, second Eve,<br/>
Saw Satan fall like lightning down from Heaven,<br/>
Prince of the Air; then, rising from his grave,<br/>
Spoiled Principalities and Powers, triumphed<br/>
In open shew, and, with ascension bright,<br/>
Captivity led captive through the Air,<br/>
The realm itself of Satan, long usurped,<br/>
Whom He shall tread at last under our feet,<br/>
Even He who now foretold his fatal bruise,<br/>
And to the Woman thus his sentence turned:—

"Thy sorrow I will greatly multiply<br/>
By thy conception; children thou shalt bring<br/>
In sorrow forth, and to thy husband's will<br/>
Thine shall submit; he over thee shall rule."

On Adam last thus judgment he pronounced:—<br/>
"Because thou hast hearkened to the voice of thy wife,<br/>
And eaten of the Tree concerning which<br/>
I charged thee, saying, Thou shalt not eat thereof,<br/>
Curs'd is the ground for thy sake; thou in sorrow<br/>
Shalt eat thereof all the days of thy life;<br/>
Thorns also and thistles it shall bring thee forth<br/>
Unbid; and thou shalt eat the herb of the field;<br/>
In the sweat of thy face thou shalt eat bread,<br/>
Till thou return unto the ground; for thou<br/>
Out of the ground wast taken: know thy birth,<br/>
For dust thou art, and shalt to dust return."

So judged he Man, both Judge and Saviour sent,<br/>
And the instant stroke of death, denounced that day,<br/>
Removed far off; then, pitying how they stood<br/>
Before him naked to the air, that now<br/>
Must suffer change, disdained not to begin<br/>
Thenceforth the form of servant to assume.<br/>
As when he washed his servants' feet, so now,<br/>
As Father of his family, he clad<br/>
Their nakedness with skins of beasts, or slain,<br/>
Or, as the snake, with youthful coat repaid;<br/>
And thought not much to clothe his enemies.<br/>
Nor he their outward only with the skins<br/>
Of beasts, but inward nakedness, much more<br/>
Opprobrious, with his robe of righteousness<br/>
Arraying, covered from his Father's sight.<br/>
To him with swift ascent he up returned,<br/>
Into his blissful bosom reassumed<br/>
In glory as of old; to him, appeased,<br/>
All, though all-knowing, what had passed with Man<br/>
Recounted, mixing intercession sweet.

Meanwhile, ere thus was sinned and judged on Earth,<br/>
Within the gates of Hell sat Sin and Death,<br/>
In counterview within the gates, that now<br/>
Stood open wide, belching outrageous flame<br/>
Far into Chaos, since the Fiend passed through,<br/>
Sin opening; who thus now to Death began:—

"O Son, why sit we here, each other viewing<br/>
Idly, while Satan, our great author, thrives<br/>
In other worlds, and happier sent provides<br/>
For us, his offspring dear? It cannot be<br/>
But that success attends him; if mishap<br/>
Ere this he had returned, with fury driven<br/>
By his Avengers, since no place like this<br/>
Can fit his punishment, or their revenge.<br/>
Methinks I feel new strength within me rise,<br/>
Wings growing, and dominion given me large<br/>
Beyond this Deep-whatever draws me on,<br/>
Or sympathy, or some connatural force,<br/>
Powerful at greatest distance to unite<br/>
With secret amity things of like kind<br/>
By secretest conveyance. Thou, my shade<br/>
Inseparable, must with me along;<br/>
For Death from Sin no power can separate.<br/>
But, lest the difficulty of passing back<br/>
Stay his return perhaps over this gulf<br/>
Impassable, impervious, let us try<br/>
(Adventrous work, yet to thy power and mine<br/>
Not unagreeable!) to found a path<br/>
Over this Main from Hell to that new World<br/>
Where Satan now prevails-a monument<br/>
Of merit high to all the infernal Host,<br/>
Easing their passage hence, for intercourse<br/>
Or transmigration, as their lot shall lead.<br/>
Nor can I miss the way, so strongly drawn<br/>
By this new-felt attraction and instinct."

Whom thus the meagre Shadow answered soon:—<br/>
"Go whither fate and inclination strong<br/>
Leads thee; I shall not lag behind, nor err<br/>
The way, thou leading: such a scent I draw<br/>
Of carnage, prey innumerable, and taste<br/>
The savour of death from all things there that live.<br/>
Nor shall I do the work thou enterprisest<br/>
Be wanting, but afford thee equal aid."

So saying, with delight he snuffed the smell<br/>
Of mortal change on Earth. As when a flock<br/>
Of ravenous fowl, though many a league remote,<br/>
Against the day of battle, to a field<br/>
Where armies lie encamped come flying, lured<br/>
With scent of living carcases designed<br/>
For death the following day in bloody fight;<br/>
So scented the grim Feature, and upturned<br/>
His nostril wide into the murky air,<br/>
Sagacious of his quarry from so far.<br/>
Then both, from out Hell-gates, into the waste<br/>
Wide anarchy of Chaos, damp and dark,<br/>
Flew diverse, and, with power (their power was great)<br/>
Hovering upon the waters, what they met<br/>
Solid or slimy, as in raging sea<br/>
Tossed up and down, together crowded drove,<br/>
From each side shoaling, towards the mouth of Hell;<br/>
As when two polar winds, blowing adverse<br/>
Upon the Cronian sea, together drive<br/>
Mountains of ice, that stop the imagined way<br/>
Beyond Petsora eastward to the rich<br/>
Cathaian coast. The aggregated soil<br/>
Death with his mace petrific, cold and dry,<br/>
As with a trident smote, and fixed as firm<br/>
As Delos, floating once; the rest his look<br/>
Bound with Gorgonian rigour not to move,<br/>
And with asphaltic slime; broad as the gate,<br/>
Deep to the roots of Hell the gathered beach<br/>
They fastened, and the mole immense wraught on<br/>
Over the foaming Deep high-arched, a bridge<br/>
Of length prodigious, joining to the wall<br/>
Immovable of this now fenceless World,<br/>
Forfeit to Death-from hence a passage broad,<br/>
Smooth, easy, inoffensive, down to Hell.<br/>
So, if great things to small may be compared,<br/>
Xerxes, the liberty lf Greece to yoke,<br/>
From Susa, his Memnonian palace high,<br/>
Came to the sea, and, over Hellespont<br/>
Bridging his way, Europe with Asia joined,<br/>
And scourged with many a stroke the indignant waves.<br/>
Now had they brought the work by wondrous art<br/>
Pontifical-a ridge of pendent rock<br/>
Over the vexed Abyss, following the track<br/>
Of Satan, to the self-same place where he<br/>
First lighted from his wing and landed safe<br/>
From out of Chaos-to the outside bare<br/>
Of this round World. With pins of adamant<br/>
And chains they made all fast, too fast they made<br/>
And durable; and now in little space<br/>
The confines met of empyrean Heaven<br/>
And of this World, and on the left hand Hell,<br/>
With long reach interposed; three several ways<br/>
In sight of each of these three places led.<br/>
And now their way to Earth they had described,<br/>
To Paradise first tending, when, behold<br/>
Satan, in likeness of an Angel bright,<br/>
Betwixt the Centaur and the Scorpion steering<br/>
His zenith, while the Sun in Aries rose!<br/>
Disguised he came; but those his children dear<br/>
Their parent soon discerned, though in disguise.<br/>
He, after Eve seduced, unminded slunk<br/>
Into the wood fast by, and, changing shape<br/>
To observe the sequel, saw his guileful act<br/>
By Eve, though all unweeting, seconded<br/>
Upon her husband-saw their shame that sought<br/>
Vain covertures; but, when he saw descend<br/>
The Son of God to judge them, terrified<br/>
He fled, not hoping to escape, but shun<br/>
The present-fearing, guilty, what his wrauth<br/>
Might suddenly inflict; that past, returned<br/>
By night, and, listening where the hapless pair<br/>
Sat in their sad discourse and various plaint,<br/>
Thence gathered his own doom; which understood<br/>
Not instant, but of future time, with joy<br/>
And tidings fraught, to Hell he now returned,<br/>
And at the brink of Chaos, near the foot<br/>
Of this new wondrous pontifice, unhoped<br/>
Met who to meet him came, his offspring dear.<br/>
Great joy was at their meeting, and at sight<br/>
Of that stupendious bridge his joy increased.<br/>
Long he admiring stood, till Sin, his fair<br/>
Inchanting daughter, thus the silence broke:—

"O Parent, these are thy magnific deeds,<br/>
Thy trophies! which thou view'st as not thine own;<br/>
Thou art their Author and prime Architect.<br/>
For I no sooner in my heart divined<br/>
(My heart, which by a secret harmony<br/>
Still moves with thine, joined in connexion sweet)<br/>
That thou on Earth hadst prospered, which thy looks<br/>
Now also evidence, but straight I felt—<br/>
Though distant from thee worlds between, yet felt—<br/>
That I must after thee with this thy son;<br/>
Such fatal consequence unites us three.<br/>
Hell could no longer hold us in her bounds,<br/>
Nor this unvoyageable gulf obscure<br/>
Detain from following thy illustrious track.<br/>
Thou hast achieved our liberty, confined

Within Hell-gates till now; thou us impowered<br/>
To fortify thus far, and overlay<br/>
With this portentous bridge the dark Abyss.<br/>
Thine now is all this World; thy virtue hath won<br/>
What thy hands builded not; thy wisdom gained,<br/>
With odds, what war hath lost, and fully avenged<br/>
Our foil in Heaven. Here thou shalt Monarch reign,<br/>
There didst not; there let him still victor sway,<br/>
As battle hath adjudged, from this new World<br/>
Retiring, by his own doom alienated,<br/>
And henceforth monarchy with thee divide<br/>
Of all things, parted by the empyreal bounds,<br/>
His quadrature, from thy orbicular World,<br/>
Or try thee now more dangerous to his Throne."

Whom thus the Prince of Darkness answered glad:—<br/>
"Fair daughter, and thou, son and grandchild both,<br/>
High proof ye now have given to be the race<br/>
Of Satan (for I glory in the name,<br/>
Antagonist of Heaven's Almighty King),<br/>
Amply have merited of me, of all<br/>
The Infernal Empire, that so near Heaven's door<br/>
Triumphal with triumphal act have met,<br/>
Mine with this glorious work, and made one realm<br/>
Hell and this World-one realm, one continent<br/>
Of easy thoroughfare. Therefore, while I<br/>
Descend through Darkness, on your road with ease,<br/>
To my associate Powers, them to acquaint<br/>
With these successes, and with them rejoice<br/>
You two this way, among these numerous orbs,<br/>
All yours, right down to Paradise descend;<br/>
There dwell and reign in bliss; thence on the Earth<br/>
Dominion exercise and in the air,<br/>
Chiefly on Man, sole lord of all declared;<br/>
Him first make sure your thrall, and lastly kill.<br/>
My substitutes I send ye, and create<br/>
Plenipotent on Earth, of matchless might<br/>
Issuing from me. On your joint vigour now<br/>
My hold of this new kingdom all depends,<br/>
Through Sin to Death exposed by my exploit.<br/>
If your joint power prevail, the affairs of Hell<br/>
No detriment need fear; go, and be strong."

So saying, he dismissed them; they with speed<br/>
Their course through thickest constellations held,<br/>
Spreading their bane; the blasted stars looked wan,<br/>
And planets, planet-strook, real eclipse<br/>
Then suffered. The other way Satan went down<br/>
The causey to Hell-gate; on either side<br/>
Disparted Chaos overbuilt exclaimed,<br/>
And with rebounding surge the bars assailed,<br/>
That scorned his indignation. Through the gate,<br/>
